### 20230807 - Curious Minds & Edgeworking with philosophers Perry Zurn and Andrea Hiott

In this conversation, Perry, the author of "Curious Minds," discusses various aspects of curiosity, its connection to love, and its broader implications. Here's a detailed summary and explanation:

1. **Curiosity as Connection**: Perry redefines curiosity from a traditional acquisitional view to that of a practice or capacity for building connections. Curiosity is relational and networked, connecting us to what we already know, care about, or are invested in. It's not just about gathering information but also about creating and navigating relationships between ideas, people, and communities.

2. **Styles of Curiosity**: Perry identifies three styles of curiosity: the Busybody (interested in many things, creates loose knowledge networks), the Hunter (focuses on a small subset of topics, creates tight knowledge networks), and the Dancer (creative, combines disparate ideas). These styles are not mutually exclusive and can coexist within individuals.

3. **Curiosity as Edge Work**: This concept refers to curiosity's role in building edges between nodes in a network, exploring peripheries, and potentially challenging existing knowledge structures. It involves risk, as pushing boundaries may disrupt established systems.

4. **Curiosity and Risk/Pioneering**: Curiosity can drive pioneering discoveries and innovations, but it requires companions (like love or ambiguity) to guide it responsibly. Without these companions, curiosity might lead to extractive practices or reinforcing hierarchies and inequalities.

5. **Complexity Science**: Perry employs complexity science to understand curiosity better. It helps uncover multiple systems involved in inquiry (neurological and social) and reveals that curiosity is a practice with various gradations rather than an all-or-nothing trait.

6. **Walking as Metaphor**: Perry uses walking metaphors to discuss different ways of being curious, acknowledging that each person's "walk" in life—physically and intellectually—is unique and influenced by their upbringing, training, and social context.

7. **Curiosity Beyond Humans**: Perry critiques the anthropocentric view of curiosity, arguing that it should be understood as a capacity shared with other forms of life (animals, plants). This perspective encourages rethinking human relationships with nature and potentially leading to more respectful interactions.

8. **Cracks**: Perry introduces the concept of "cracks" in networks, geological formations, and social contexts as places where destruction opens up possibilities for creativity and change. Cracks symbolize hope amidst breakdowns, encouraging a more complex view of life that includes both construction and destruction.

9. **Love and Curiosity**: Perry uses TS Eliot's poem about love to frame curiosity, highlighting its daring nature in disturbing the universe. This metaphor extends the theme of cracking open to new possibilities and perspectives.

10. **Dedication**: The book is dedicated to "all the children who question whether it needs to be this way," emphasizing the importance of acknowledging and valuing children's curiosity, which often goes unheard or unappreciated in adult-dominated societies.

This conversation underscores Perry's interdisciplinary approach to understanding curiosity—drawing from philosophy, psychology, network science, ecology, and literature—and highlights the transformative potential of embracing a more expansive view of this human trait.

### 20230916 - Love, philosophy and navigability： Holding the paradox. Early fumbling towards a mature philosophy.

Andrea Hyatt, a PhD researcher, is exploring the concept of cognition as a continuous process across different species and scales. She challenges the traditional dichotomy between physical and mental processes, suggesting that all forms of movement – from biological locomotion to intellectual exploration – are interconnected aspects of a broader, non-linear process.

Her research, titled "Beyond Dichotomies," aims to reframe our understanding of self, cognition, and movement by adopting an 'ecological orientation.' This perspective emphasizes the unique, intertwined paths we each tread through life—from birth to present moments, encompassing all forms of physical, mental, emotional, and digital movements.

Hyatt posits that we are composed of many 'selves' depending on our situations, much like individual cells contribute to a larger organism. She draws parallels between personal identity and cellular self-definition, arguing that our distinctiveness doesn't negate our interconnectedness with others and the environment. 

Key themes in her research include:

1. Movement as a Continuous Process: Hyatt suggests that cognition is not separate from physical movement but rather an extension of it. This movement occurs across various scales, from biological to digital realms.

2. Ecological Orientation: Adopting this perspective involves recognizing our unique, intertwined paths through life as part of a larger ecological network. It implies understanding ourselves not just as isolated individuals but as integral components within an expansive web of relationships and movements.

3. Dynamic Nature of Self and Cognition: Hyatt challenges the idea of fixed selves or static cognitive processes, arguing instead for their fluid, evolving nature. She emphasizes that measuring or categorizing these processes inevitably changes them.

4. Expansion of Possibilities: By reorienting our assumptions about movement and cognition, Hyatt hopes to broaden our understanding of what's possible, both in terms of individual potential and collective progress. This includes reimagining technology, medicine, and other fields to better accommodate this holistic view.

5. Interdisciplinary Dialogue: To further her research, Hyatt engages in conversations with scientists, philosophers, artists, and others from diverse fields. These dialogues help uncover patterns and insights that might not emerge within a single discipline, fostering a richer, more comprehensive understanding of the subject matter.

Despite her focus on movement and its implications, Hyatt acknowledges her lack of familiarity with social media, stating she's an "old-school nerd" who prefers traditional research methods over digital platforms. Nonetheless, she welcomes assistance in navigating these tools if it aids her quest for knowledge exchange and pattern recognition.

### 20231129 - Exploring Love, Cognitive Science, and Embodiment： What really matters？

The speaker engages in a detailed discussion with an interviewer about alternative perspectives on evolution, adaptation, and individuality, challenging the dominant "Universal Darwinism" that asserts natural selection is the sole mechanism for all forms of adaptation. They highlight two main ideas:

1. Generalized Darwinism: This concept expands the application of Darwinian principles beyond biology to other substrates like physical organizations, cultural memes, and even the universe itself. It suggests that any set of entities subject to differential survival or reproduction can adapt through a variation-selection process.

2. Alternative Adaptive Mechanisms: The speaker argues for the existence of other adaptive processes beyond natural selection, focusing on learning mechanisms like connectionism and unsupervised learning. These processes involve strengthening or weakening connections between neurons or entities based on their usefulness rather than random variation followed by selection.

The conversation also delves into the notion of individuality in living systems:

- Evolutionary Unit (Dowinian Unit): A unit capable of self-reproduction with heritable variation and reproductive success, which can undergo natural selection. Examples include genes, single-cell organisms, or multicellular organisms.

- Agential Individuality: This perspective considers individuality as a system's ability to act on its own behalf through information integration and collective action, irrespective of whether it is an evolutionary unit. It suggests that living systems exhibit this kind of agency at various scales, from cells to ecosystems.

The discussion further explores the relationship between these two notions of individuality, questioning whether agency (individuality) is a product of evolution by natural selection or if it can occur spontaneously within certain types of networks with suitable properties. The speaker emphasizes that their research focuses on demonstrating how adaptive processes like those seen in connectionist learning systems can emerge without competition or natural selection driving them.

In summary, this conversation revolves around challenging the exclusive focus on natural selection as the sole driver of adaptation and evolution, highlighting alternative mechanisms such as learning-based processes, and exploring different conceptions of individuality within living systems. The interviewers' work proposes that various adaptive mechanisms may exist at different levels of organization in both biological and non-biological contexts.

### 20231221 - In Continuum： Cognitions from other perspectives, Exploring Xenobots & Bioelectricity, Levin & Hiott

Michael Levin, a researcher in regenerative medicine and developmental biology, engaged in a thought-provoking discussion about cognition, shape formation, and the continuum between mind and matter. Here's a detailed summary of their conversation:

1. Xenobots:
   - Xenobots are living organisms created by reprogramming stem cells from frog embryos. They can self-assemble into various shapes without external manipulation.
   - Two types of Xenobots exist: spherical and sculpted versions. The latter can be shaped using microsurgery, allowing for the addition of specific cell types to achieve desired functions (e.g., legged movement).
   - A Pac-Man shape was designed by an AI algorithm to optimize gathering loose cells for subsequent generations.

2. Cognition and Shape Formation:
   - The conversation delved into the relationship between cognition, shape formation, and morphogenesis in living organisms. Levin emphasized that cognition should be viewed as a continuum rather than a binary concept.
   - Xenobots demonstrate this by self-assembling and regenerating to their original shapes when injured, suggesting they navigate a "morphogenetic space."

3. Bioelectricity and Cognitive Processes:
   - Levin drew parallels between bioelectricity in living organisms and computation in computers. He argued that bioelectricity serves as an interpreter for cells, allowing them to make decisions based on voltage changes without needing to understand the underlying molecular details.

4. Observer-Dependent Cognition:
   - The discussion highlighted the observer-dependent nature of cognition, emphasizing that what is considered "cognitive" depends on the level at which it's being measured or assessed.
   - Levin argued that scientists often use low-agency tools (e.g., rulers and voltmeters) to study systems, limiting their ability to perceive higher-level cognitive processes like goal-directed behavior.

5. Navigation and Goals:
   - The conversation touched on the concept of navigation and goals in living organisms. Levin suggested that goals are observer-dependent, meaning different observers might define varying sets of goals for a given system based on their chosen problem space and experiments conducted.

6. Planarian Regeneration:
   - Planarians, a type of flatworm, were used as an example to illustrate the continuum between mind and matter in regeneration. Levin described how bioelectric patterns serve as memories for correct planarian shape, which cells can rebuild after injury.

7. Engineering Perspective:
   - Levin emphasized a broad definition of engineering, encompassing mechanical, social, and even human relationships. He suggested that engineering is about exerting agency to achieve desired outcomes while considering the agency of other systems involved in the interaction.

8. Quantum Computers and Living Computers:
   - Levin proposed the idea of quantum computers or living computers built from bacteria and fungi as potential architectures capable of switching around components without losing functionality, similar to biological regeneration.

9. Galton Board and Platonic Space of Form:
   - The conversation referenced the Galton board (a device that demonstrates a binomial distribution) and Plato's concept of a platonic space of form to argue that some patterns exist independently from the physical world, requiring new ways of assessing and understanding them.

10. Regeneration vs. Talk Therapy:
   - Levin proposed that regeneration is best achieved by addressing bioelectric memories of collectives of cells rather than at low molecular levels or high-level talk therapy. He acknowledged the possibility of other methods, such as verbal interfaces for healing and regeneration, but suggested that middle-ground approaches might be more effective.

11. Inertia and Cognition:
   - The conversation touched on inertia as a measure of resistance to change and stimuli, emphasizing its importance for maintaining stability in complex systems. Levin suggested that altering cellular decision-making patterns (inertia) could reveal novel competencies by helping cells pay attention to different inputs.

This summary captures the essence of Michael Levin's thought-provoking discussion on cognition, shape formation, and the continuum between mind and matter, highlighting the complexities and nuances involved in understanding these phenomena.

### 20240101 - Pattern, Meaning, & Integration with Jeremy Lent and Andrea Hiott

In this conversation, Jeremy Tillman discusses his journey of writing books on spirituality, science, and cognitive patterns. His path began as a teenage seeker, rejecting the world he was brought up in, but taking an unexpected turn when he moved to the U.S., got married, and pursued a career in business.

1. Early Seeking: As a teenager in northwest London, Tillman felt drawn to something bigger than his upbringing. His rejection of traditional Jewish teachings, which emphasized maintaining a low profile due to historical persecution, fueled this desire for exploration.

2. Life Turn: After moving to the U.S., he embraced the hippie culture but later found himself in a conventional life path when his wife wanted stability for their children. He pursued an MBA and started a successful business, which felt like a U-turn from his rebellious teenage self.

3. Traumatic Turn: When his wife fell ill with dementia, Tillman left his career to care for her, experiencing years of isolation and grief. This period became a crucible that led him to seek a deeper understanding of life's meaning beyond external sources.

4. Research and Writing: During this challenging time, Tillman voraciously read across various disciplines, including philosophy, history, cognitive science, and evolutionary biology. This quest for understanding eventually led him to write his first book, "The Patterning Instinct," which examines how human cultures develop patterns of meaning about the universe while often ignoring other possible interpretations.

5. Cognitive History: Tillman's approach in "The Patterning Instinct" is unusual as it uses cognitive science and evolutionary biology to explore history, revealing that human cultures create patterns (metaphors) that shape their worldviews. These metaphors have implications and entailments, similar to the scaffoldings of symbolic thinking described by cognitive linguist George Lakoff.

6. Animating Intelligence vs. Conceptual Intelligence: Tillman distinguishes between animating intelligence (shared among mammals) and conceptual intelligence (uniquely human). The former involves making sense of the environment to optimize survival, while the latter enables humans to create abstract concepts through their highly developed prefrontal cortex.

7. Meaning as Connectedness: Tillman defines meaning as an emergent phenomenon arising from complex interconnections in self-organizing systems. It is not merely objective truth but a subjective experience that we engage with and enact through our consciousness, transcending the mind-body dichotomy.

8. Emergence and Systems Thinking: Tillman illustrates the concept of emergence using examples like flocking birds or human cells coordinating within our bodies. He argues that meaning is similarly an emergent property arising from complex relationships, rather than a linear or dualistic construct.

9. Dualism and False Dichotomies: Tillman critiques the modern Western dualistic worldview (mind-body split) that originated during the scientific revolution. He contrasts this with Eastern philosophies and indigenous cultures, which view mind and body as interconnected aspects of a holistic reality.

10. Integration of Traditions: In "The Web of Meaning," Tillman explores integrating scientific understanding with traditional wisdom to find our place in the universe. He emphasizes that our current global civilization requires a new sense of meaning-making and worldview, drawing on insights from diverse human traditions while avoiding romanticizing any "golden age."

11. Cooperation and Competition: Tillman advocates for embracing capitalism and opening space around it to change patterns and perspectives. He promotes the idea of cognitive shifts, recognizing that true happiness (eudaimonia) comes from fulfilling one's unique potential rather than hedonic attainments driven by consumer culture.

12. Deep Transformation Network: Tillman created an online community called the Deep Transformation Network to engage with like-minded individuals seeking profound societal change. The network offers live events, articles, discussions, and monthly interactive conversations for those interested in exploring possibilities for a life-affirming civilization.


### 20240121 - Stream of Consciousness & Cognition： Research Diary 2

The text is a reflective monologue by an individual contemplating the concepts of consciousness and cognition, particularly within the context of their own research on "waymaking." The speaker explores these concepts through various scales, from cellular to human, and even extends to animals and plants.

Consciousness is seen as the fundamental feeling or awareness of being alive, which all living entities, in some form, possess. In contrast, cognition involves becoming aware of one's own patterns or processes - essentially, the ability to recognize and understand one's mental activity. 

2. **Emergence of Cognition**: The speaker describes how cognition emerges from consciousness over time. This process begins at birth or even earlier, with newborns aligning their bodies' patterns with the regularities of sensory input (like sounds and sights). Over time, this alignment turns into understanding and, eventually, self-awareness - a form of cognitive development.

3. **External Representations**: The speaker argues that traditional notions of 'cognitive representations' within the brain are limiting. Instead, they propose that cognition is primarily about noticing patterns in external representations (like language, images) and internalizing them. This process allows humans to extend their capacity to notice and learn from patterns beyond immediate sensory input.

4. **Expanding the Scope of Cognition**: The speaker advocates for a broader understanding of cognition that includes all life forms, not just humans. They propose that plants, animals, and even cells exhibit forms of cognitive behavior, though these might look very different from human cognition due to varying body types and sensory capacities.

5. **Ecological Awareness**: The speaker suggests that an ecologically-informed perspective on cognition can help us understand our connections with other living beings and the environment. This includes recognizing how our urban landscapes, for instance, shape our cognitive processes, and vice versa.

6. **Responsibility**: With this expanded view of cognition comes a sense of responsibility towards understanding and respecting all forms of life, not just human. The speaker emphasizes that recognizing the cognitive nature of other living entities implies acknowledging their subjective experiences and rights within ecosystems.

The speaker concludes by expressing their desire for feedback on these philosophical and scientific ideas as they work on multiple research papers on waymaking. They value both personal reflection (as demonstrated in this monologue) and external perspectives to refine their thinking.


### 20240123 - From Ants to Active Inference with Daniel Ari Friedman

In this conversation, Daniel R.A. Friedman of the Active Inference Institute discusses his research on ants with the host, exploring themes related to cognition, perception, action, and collective behavior. Here are some key points from their discussion:

1. Ants as a model for understanding individuality and collective behavior: Daniel worked with ant colonies led by Professor Deborah Gordon at Stanford, studying the evolution and ecology of collective behavior in Pogonomyrmex Barbadensis red harvester ants. This research focused on understanding the relationship between individual ants and the colony as a whole.

2. Scale-free and scale-friendly frameworks: Daniel talks about the concept of scale-free frameworks, which are analytical representations that don't presuppose a specific system or scale. While this can be useful for opening up possibilities, he also introduces the idea of "scale-friendly" frameworks, which allow interoperability and enable moving from one scale to another (n to n+1 or n to n-1).

3. Active Inference: Daniel explains active inference as a unified approach to modeling perception, cognition, action, and impact on the environment. It involves a fourfold partition consisting of an interface/boundary with inbound and outbound statistical dependencies. Inbound dependencies are associated with perception, outbound with action, and everything else with cognition.

4. Cognitive models and language: The conversation touches upon the relationship between mathematical models (e.g., predictive processing and predictive coding) and cognitive models. Daniel emphasizes that active inference is a tool for understanding these systems rather than the systems themselves, highlighting the importance of separating the model from the object being studied.

5. Cognition and continuity: The speakers discuss the nature of cognition across different species, including ants, humans, and plants. Daniel suggests that cognition can be viewed as a coarse-grained keyword, which needs to be described in specific contexts (abstract or system-dependent). He also acknowledges that some people may interpret cognition differently based on their perspectives and the context.

6. Service as a central theme: The conversation revolves around the idea of service and how it relates to active inference, team dynamics, and broader themes. Daniel emphasizes the importance of open-endedness in action, inference, and service, suggesting that it is crucial to consider deeper questions about technical "why" and philosophical aspects like love, dignity, and honor.

7. Passion and motivation: Towards the end of the conversation, Daniel reflects on his passion for his work, describing it as a multifaceted journey involving personal, professional, and civilizational aspects. He stresses the importance of maintaining curiosity, openness, and balance in one's pursuits.

Throughout the conversation, both speakers explore the complexities surrounding the nature of individuality, collective behavior, and cognition while emphasizing the importance of approaching these topics with nuance and a broad perspective. They also touch upon the limitations of language and models in capturing the full complexity of such phenomena.


### 20240302 - Among Superheroes： memories and maps, from McGill to UCL with psychologist Lynn Nadel

Lynn Nadel, a prominent cognitive neuroscientist, shares his experiences and insights about his career, particularly focusing on his time at McGill University in Montreal during the 1960s. This era was significant for neuroscience as it was a hub for pioneering research in memory, learning, and the hippocampus.

Nadel initially pursued chemistry but switched to biology after realizing he wasn't cut out for lab work. He took an introductory psychology course taught by Donald Hebb, which sparked his interest in the field. He then secured a job working in a behavioral psychopharmacology lab with Dalbir Bindra as a gap year before entering graduate school.

During his time at McGill, Nadel found himself surrounded by renowned scientists like Hebb, Brenda Milner (then married to Peter Milner), and John O'Keefe. The department's atmosphere was intensely intellectual and politically engaged, with many grad students sharing left-leaning views.

Nadel became interested in the discrepancy between human memory research using patients like HM and animal studies that failed to replicate these findings. He proposed that this disjunct might be due to different regions of the hippocampus being studied. This hypothesis would later guide his PhD research on the hippocampus.

The lab was characterized by a mix of camaraderie and intense intellectual pursuit, with students often spending extended hours in the lab, including weekends. Nadel recalls that the environment was politically charged and that he and O'Keefe bonded over shared interests in politics despite not working directly together on research projects.

The atmosphere of McGill's psychology department during this time was exceptionally vibrant, combining brain and behavior studies in a way that set it apart from other institutions. The influence of influential figures like Edward Tolman, whose theories on latent learning influenced Nadel's thinking about memory and navigation as interconnected concepts.

In terms of personal connections, Nadel mentions his acquaintance with Robert Muller, whom he knew from high school, and their subsequent collaboration in graduate school and beyond. He also touches upon the small-world phenomenon, highlighting how seemingly disconnected individuals can find themselves entwined through various paths in life.

Nadel's journey eventually led him to Prague, where his interest in Eastern European neuroscience sparked a lasting fascination with the country and its scientific community. His time there was marked by personal upheaval (including a marriage breakdown) yet also intellectual triumphs as he made significant contributions to our understanding of the hippocampus and cognitive maps, collaborating closely with O'Keefe.

Nadel's experiences illustrate how his career path intersected with major figures in neuroscience, shaped by the unique atmosphere at McGill University during a transformative period for the field. His story emphasizes the serendipitous nature of scientific discovery and the role of personal connections and environmental influences on research trajectories.


### 20240319 - Anscombe and Patterns of Action with Mirko Prokop

In this conversation, Mirko Prokop and the unnamed speaker discuss a paper titled "Intentions in Ecological Psychology and Scombian Perspective" by Miguel Seguío Tejero and Annemarie Callistis. The paper aims to integrate Elizabeth Anscombe's philosophy of action into ecological psychology, a field that emphasizes direct perception and non-representational cognition.

Anscombe's perspective on intention is crucial for the authors because it offers an alternative to traditional views that consider intentions as mental states or internal representations. Instead, Anscombe sees intentions as patterns of activity, which can be understood within an ecological framework without relying on representational processes.

The paper addresses two main challenges: explaining what intentions are (if not representational states) and accommodating the fact that intentions modulate perceived affordances in the environment. The authors argue that Anscombe's account of intention can meet these challenges, as it allows for a non-representational understanding of mental life while still accounting for goal-directed behavior.

Key points from their discussion include:

1. **Ecological Psychology**: This approach emphasizes direct perception and the organism's interaction with its environment, without relying on internal representations or mental states to explain cognition and action. It focuses on affordances – possibilities for action provided by the environment relative to an agent's capabilities.
2. **Anscombe's Philosophy of Action**: Anscombe views intentions as patterns of activity, essential for understanding goal-directed behavior. Her approach highlights the importance of the 'why' question in revealing practical knowledge and intentionality.
3. **Integrating Anscombe with Ecological Psychology**: The paper argues that Anscombe's account of intentions can be compatible with ecological psychology by reconceptualizing intentions as organizational patterns of activity rather than internal representations. This allows for a non-representational understanding of intention within an ecological framework, accommodating the idea that intentions modulate perceived affordances in the environment.
4. **Criticisms and Nuance**: The conversation touches on potential criticisms, such as the difficulty in reconciling teleological goals with naturalistic science or the risk of oversimplifying complex cognitive processes. Both speakers emphasize the importance of nuance and interdisciplinary approaches when considering concepts like agency, intention, and perception.
5. **Broader Implications**: The discussion highlights the value of exploring beyond established dichotomies and disciplinary boundaries to foster new insights into cognition and mental life. It underscores the importance of integrating philosophical perspectives with empirical approaches in understanding human behavior and experience.

In summary, this conversation revolves around the paper's proposal to integrate Anscombe's philosophy of action into ecological psychology, offering a non-representational account of intentions as organizational patterns that modulate perceived affordances in the environment. The speakers discuss the implications of this integration for understanding cognition and mental life, emphasizing the importance of nuance, interdisciplinary approaches, and exploring beyond established boundaries.


### 20240322 - Life and Landscape： Intelligences as forms of embodied navigability

The text discusses the concept of human cognition as a form of "way making," which refers to the broader process of problem-solving, communication, interaction, presence, participation, and survival that life has been engaging in since its beginning. It argues against the narrow view of cognition as solely a human trait, suggesting instead that all embodied beings partake in this process, though they do so through their unique bodies and environments.

1. **Cognition as Way Making**: The author posits that what humans term "cognition" is essentially the tip of an extensive, ancient process. This includes not just human thoughts, minds, intelligence, or thinking, but also the ways plants, animals, and other life forms interact with their environments to solve problems and survive. 

2. **Embodiment and Landscapes**: The concept of 'landscape' is central here. It's not just about physical geography but any regularly encountered space - be it geographical, social, or conceptual (like literature, art, music). These spaces have statistical regularity, meaning they follow patterns that our brains can recognize and navigate, much like how we navigate physical landscapes.

3. **Neuroscience Evidence**: The author references recent neuroscience research on the hippocampus, particularly place cells and grid cells, which are involved in navigation and spatial memory. These studies suggest that our brains use a similar GPS-like system for navigating both physical spaces and abstract concepts or 'cognitive landscapes'. This includes everything from recognizing patterns in a book genre to understanding the layout of a Starbucks worldwide. 

4. **Navigational Cognition**: The text proposes that our cognitive processes, including thinking and problem-solving, can be understood as navigational activities within these diverse landscapes. This means that when we learn new concepts or engage in abstract thought, our brains are essentially creating mental maps, much like we do for physical spaces. 

5. **Sustainability Implications**: The author suggests that understanding cognition in this broader, non-human context could enhance sustainability. By recognizing the value of all forms of 'way making' - including those of plants, animals, and other life - we might develop more inclusive models for simulating our planet's future. This includes considering not just human trajectories but also those of countless other species, each with their unique navigational strategies.

6. **Expanding Perspectives**: Ultimately, the text encourages a shift in perspective - viewing human cognition not as an isolated phenomenon confined to the human brain, but as one manifestation of a universal process of 'way making' happening across all life forms. This could lead to more holistic and sustainable approaches to understanding and interacting with our shared world.

### 20240430 - The music of mind and life： Are minds best understood as songs？ Richard Watson and Andrea Hiott

This theory suggests that there is a swinging back and forth between top-down causation (large-scale control) and bottom-up causation (small-scale influence), mediated by resonance through harmonic relationships of frequencies.

1. **Nested Cycles/Oscillations**: Living things are described as physical dynamical systems with dynamics at multiple scales. These cycles or oscillations can be thought of as a pendulum swinging between potential and kinetic energy, symbolizing the interplay between genotype (potential) and phenotype (kinetic).

2. **Top-Down vs Bottom-Up Causation**: The theory proposes that causal flow is not exclusively from small to large or top to bottom but a swinging back and forth between these directions. This duality allows for symmetry in the observer-observed relationship, where an observer can only influence something by sharing its natural frequency.

3. **Musical Analogy**: The speaker employs musical concepts to explain this theory, using metaphors like harmonies, intervals, and resonance. This analogy helps visualize how different scales of a living system interact and influence each other, similar to how different notes in music resonate with one another based on their frequencies.

4. **Agency and Cognition**: The speaker argues that cognition is not just a brain function but an inherent characteristic of living things. For something to be considered 'alive,' it must engage in information integration and collective action – essentially, doing 'cognitive' work. This perspective shifts the focus from individual parts (cells, genes) to the whole organism's cognitive abilities.

5. **Emergence**: The discussion also touches on the concept of emergence, where complex patterns and behaviors arise from simple rules operating at different scales within a system. This idea is illustrated through examples like a guitar string's vibration, which depends on both molecular interactions (micro-scale) and the string's geometry (macro-scale).

6. **Meaning and Purpose**: Finally, the conversation delves into philosophical questions about meaning and purpose in life. The speaker challenges the notion that life is an optimization process, suggesting instead that living systems create their own niches through continuous, spiraling development – a dance of stepping in and out, rather than striving toward a fixed goal.

The overall theme of this discussion revolves around a novel perspective on living things as complex, interconnected oscillatory systems, using musical metaphors to elucidate the intricate relationships between different scales and levels of organization within biological entities. This cognition-first viewpoint emphasizes the importance of information integration and collective action as defining characteristics of life, rather than viewing living things merely as collections of cells or genes.


### 20240503 - Left & Right Reconciliation in the Brain and Beyond with Iain McGilchrist and Andrea Hiott

Ian McEwan, a renowned author and philosopher, delves into the themes of asymmetry, coincidence of opposites, and beyond dichotomy in his work. Asymmetry is a crucial principle in understanding various aspects of life, including the human brain, nature, and the cosmos.

1. Brain Asymmetry: McEwan discusses the asymmetry present in every brain, even those of ancient creatures like the metastella vectensis. He highlights how both hemispheres contribute uniquely to our cognitive processes; the left hemisphere focuses on grasping and manipulating information, while the right hemisphere concentrates on comprehending the overall picture and making sense of it.

2. Nature's Asymmetry: McEwan emphasizes that asymmetry is a fundamental characteristic in nature. He explains how even seemingly perfect symmetry can be engineered to appear unnatural or eerie, suggesting that true living forms are naturally asymmetrical. This asymmetry suggests underlying symmetry and implies that there's an inherent order within apparent chaos.

3. Asymmetry of Symmetry: McEwan posits the concept of "asymmetry of symmetry," meaning both symmetry and asymmetry are essential and interconnected aspects of reality. This idea is exemplified at a cosmic level, where an initial asymmetry in matter and antimatter led to the birth of everything in the universe.

4. Philosophical Implications: McEwan's exploration of asymmetry extends beyond the natural sciences and into philosophy. He argues that the problem with our current thinking lies in dichotomous perspectives, which often overlook hidden aspects or "dark sides" of phenomena. Instead, he proposes a reconciliation of opposites—an understanding that embraces both aspects simultaneously without privileging one over the other.

5. Becoming vs. Perfection: McEwan challenges the Platonic and Aristotelian notions of perfect being by advocating for the primacy of becoming in the universe. He believes that everything is in a state of constant flux, as expressed by Heraclitus' famous phrase "everything flows."

6. Literature, Philosophy, and Science: McEwan's journey into literature and philosophy, rather than traditional scientific studies, allowed him to approach complex questions from alternative angles. He found that science taught in schools was often overly focused on factual learning and lacked the exploration of nuanced ideas and relationships.

7. Attention, Perception, and Nestedness: McEwan explores nested scales of attention and perception, where different levels of complexity coexist without clear boundaries. He emphasizes the importance of attending to reality rather than representations or abstractions. This includes acknowledging our place within nested hierarchies (holearchy) that connect us to both individual experiences and broader cosmic patterns.

8. Joy and Human Connection: McEwan identifies three essential aspects for human flourishing: belonging to a group, being part of nature, and connecting with a higher power. These connections provide joy, meaning, and spiritual nourishment in life.

9. The Role of Imagination, Intuition, and Art: McEwan underscores the value of imagination, intuition, and art as means to transcend left-brain limitations, such as narrow, targeted attention. He encourages embracing a more comprehensive perspective that appreciates nuance, beauty, and complexity in the world around us.

10. Balancing Left and Right Brain Attention: McEwan discusses the importance of cultivating different kinds of attention—the left hemisphere's narrow, targeted focus versus the right hemisphere's broader, context-aware perception. He believes that balancing these two modes of attention can lead to a richer understanding of reality and a more fulfilling life.

In conclusion, Ian McEwan's work weaves together themes of asymmetry, the interconnectedness of opposites, and the importance of embracing complexity in our perception of reality. By exploring these ideas through various lenses—neuroscience, philosophy, literature, and art—McEwan offers a nuanced perspective on human existence and its relationship to the world around us.

This perspective is echoed in the work of the respondent, who has proposed models like "Sustain" that can accommodate various cognitive processes under one umbrella.

The discussion touches on the history of hippocampal research, where it was initially thought to be solely involved in memory formation, then later in navigation due to discoveries of place cells, grid cells, and border cells. The speaker argues that these models suggest a unity in the hippocampus's functions rather than distinct roles.

The concept of 'general learning' is central here; it implies that the same underlying mechanism could be responsible for seemingly different cognitive processes, such as memory and navigation. This view challenges the traditional neuroscientific categorization of brain functions into discrete, labelled domains.

The speaker also critiques the tendency in neuroscience to discover and label specific cell types (like Jennifer Aniston cells or grid cells) without delving into their broader role within a larger computational process. This approach is seen as potentially limiting scientific understanding, as it might overlook the nuanced interplay between brain mechanisms, environmental contexts, and experimental setups that shape these observed phenomena.

The speaker emphasizes the importance of understanding not just what these cells do, but how they fit into a larger computational framework. They argue that this holistic view could provide richer insights into the complexities of brain function, moving beyond simplistic labels and towards a more integrated, context-aware understanding of cognition.

In essence, the discussion is about shifting perspectives in neuroscience—from viewing specific brain functions or cell types as isolated entities to recognizing them as part of an overarching general learning system. This broader perspective could lead to a deeper and more nuanced understanding of cognitive processes, including memory, navigation, and possibly others yet unexplored.


### 20240510 - The Future of AI & the Spatial Web： Denise Holt on the tech life, personal and public

The conversation revolves around the Spatial Web, a new evolution of the internet protocol, referred to as web 3.0 or the Spatial Web, developed by Versus Technologies. This new protocol aims to transform the current two-dimensional worldwide web into a three-dimensional digital twin space, enabling distributed intelligence at scale through active inference AI agents.

The Spatial Web Protocol (HSTP) and Hyperspace Modeling Language (HSML) are central to this vision. HSML allows for programming spaces, incorporating aspects like activities, credentials, multi-dimensional data, and time-based elements. It essentially turns space into a domain that can be programmed with context about entities and their interrelationships.

Active inference AI agents learn through a constant interaction with the environment using real-time sensory input. They measure incoming information against their model of the world, constantly updating it as they gather new data. This approach differs significantly from deep learning AI, which relies on large datasets for training and lacks the ability to adapt to new information without significant updates or retraining.

The Spatial Web aims to solve key issues with the current web 2.0 model: hacking, tracking, and faking (surveillance). It offers a zero-trust architecture where data privacy is prioritized. Users can control who has access to their data, decide when and for how long it's shared, and even allow confirmation of sensitive information without revealing the data itself.

The philosophical vision behind the Spatial Web involves a shift in values, moving from a competitive, ego-driven society to one that is more community-oriented, focusing on aspiration rather than ambition. This new technology promises abundance by democratizing access to data and resources, potentially transforming our understanding of value exchange and the nature of money itself.

In terms of personal impact, the Spatial Web could enable a more natural integration with technology, reducing feelings of isolation or dependency on devices. It may even foster a deeper appreciation for our physical surroundings by augmenting our reality rather than replacing it. Furthermore, it holds potential for enhancing human connection and collaboration across vast distances in novel ways, such as immersive virtual meetings that feel like being in the same room together.

The conversation also touches on the creator's passion for this technology, driven by a belief in its potential for positive societal impact and personal fulfillment. They are actively involved in educating others about the Spatial Web through writing, podcasting, and hosting learning lab sessions via their Substack platform, with plans to create an e-learning curriculum based on the HSTP. The creator encourages listeners to get excited about this emerging technology as it's unfolding now, with Versus Technologies planning to open its beta platform to a wider developer community this summer.


### 20240517 - What is intelligence？ A Brief History with entrepreneur Max Bennett

The interview covers several key topics related to the book "A Brief History of Intelligence, Evolution, AI, and Five Breakthroughs That Made Our Brains" by Max Tegmark. Here's a detailed summary:

1. **Intelligence and Brains**: The conversation begins with the question of whether intelligence originates from brains. While acknowledging that intelligence predates brains (e.g., single-celled organisms exhibit problem-solving abilities), the focus is on how the brain implements intelligence uniquely.

2. **Evolutionary Breakthroughs**: The book outlines five evolutionary breakthroughs that shaped our brains:
   - Steering: Early bilaterians (650 million years ago) developed movement and navigation capabilities, using a simple algorithm called taxis navigation to find food.
   - Reinforcement Learning: With the emergence of vertebrates, neurons began recognizing patterns instead of individual stimuli, enabling more complex behaviors. This also marks the beginning of learning from experiences (reinforcement).
   - Simulation and Mentalization: The development of the neocortex in mammals around 150 million years ago enabled self-supervised predictive coding or generative modeling, leading to simulation capabilities like imagining alternatives, recalling memories, and planning fine motor skills. This is linked to the hippocampus, amygdala, and olfactory cortex, which are thought to be evolutionary remnants of the original cortex.
   - Mentalizing: The ability to understand others' mental states emerged with mammals, possibly due to the neocortex's generative modeling capabilities. This includes theory of mind and empathy.
   - Speaking: Language evolved as a tool for sharing mental simulations, enabling complex communication and cooperation beyond what other animals can achieve.

3. **AI and Brain Inspiration**: The interview touches on the ongoing debate in AI research regarding how much inspiration should come from studying nature (neuroscience) versus creating novel solutions. Tegmark argues for a middle ground, where neuroscience can provide existence proofs of abilities and waypoints for fundamental features, but direct copying isn't necessary or desirable.

4. **AI Applications**: The discussion moves to potential positive applications of AI, such as:
   - Education: Personalized tutoring systems that adapt to individual learning styles.
   - Medical Care: Democratizing access to high-quality medical information and advice worldwide.
   - Household Assistance: Robots aiding with chores, potentially helping single parents or elderly individuals, without replacing human interactions.

5. **Algorithmic Approach**: Tegmark's book focuses on understanding the underlying algorithms of brain functions rather than specific biophysical implementations or computational levels. This approach aims to make it easier to reverse-engineer brain function and apply insights to AI development.

6. **Cognitive Map and Simulation**: The hippocampus plays a crucial role in creating cognitive maps, enabling spatial navigation and memory. Recent studies suggest that mammals can simulate or imagine scenarios, supporting the idea of mentalizing and planning, which sets them apart from other vertebrates like fish.

7. **Active Inference**: This concept posits that the brain models itself to infer intentions and drive behavior towards fulfilling those inferred goals, rather than solely optimizing for reward. This allows for behaviors that don't directly maximize immediate rewards, such as the dissonance effect in human decision-making.

8. **Language and AI**: The book argues against language being the foundation of intelligence, suggesting it evolved as a tool to communicate shared mental simulations. This perspective is relevant to current AI research, particularly large language models, which face challenges like catastrophic forgetting—the inability to learn continually without losing previously acquired knowledge.

9. **Continual Learning in AI**: The interview concludes by discussing the open problem of continual learning in AI, where systems can acquire and retain knowledge over time, similar to how mammals learn. Researchers are exploring various approaches, including augmenting language models with memory stores or reimagining neural network algorithms to achieve this capability.

The interview provides a rich exploration of Tegmark's book, delving into the evolutionary history of intelligence, its implementation in brains, and the implications for AI development. It also touches on potential positive applications of AI and the ongoing debate about how much AI should learn from neuroscience versus pursuing novel solutions.


### 20240524 - Brain GPT & Rethinking Neuroscience with Brad Love (University College London)

The conversation revolves around the topic of large language models, specifically focusing on Brain GPT, a project that aims to predict neuroscience results using these models. The speaker discusses several key points:

1. **Scariness of Large Language Models**: The speaker acknowledges that while they find large language models impressive and useful for prediction, there are concerns about their potential misuse or unintended consequences. They emphasize the importance of responsible use and caution when working with these models.

2. **Objective Functions**: Unlike traditional models with clear objective functions, large language models like those used in Brain GPT don't have a straightforward way to understand their goals or potential risks. The speaker raises concerns about this lack of transparency and control, especially as we move towards larger models capable of understanding and acting upon the world beyond textual data.

3. **Benchmark Creation**: A significant part of the project involves creating a benchmark for neuroscience predictions. This benchmark consists of 200 items carefully constructed to test both human experts and large language models in predicting various neuroscience results. The creation of this benchmark required extensive human labor for quality control, involving over 75 active volunteers and a waiting list of over a hundred people.

4. **Potential Applications**: The speaker highlights several potential applications of the Brain GPT project:
   - Combining human and machine predictions to improve accuracy, as humans and models have non-overlapping knowledge that can complement each other.
   - Fine-tuning the models to incorporate neuroscience knowledge for better performance in specific domains.
   - Hosting the model on the web for easy access and integration into various tools, such as chat interfaces.

5. **Future Directions**: The speaker discusses potential future developments of the Brain GPT project:
   - Using the models to generate hypotheses and suggest experiments based on specific research questions.
   - Exploring ways to control hallucinations (i.e., generating false information) in these models, especially when used for scientific discovery or creative applications.

6. **Concerns about Uncontrolled Models**: The speaker expresses concerns about the potential risks of large action and world models that could be trained on vast amounts of data, including non-textual sources like images and real-world interactions. They question whether we can accurately map and understand such complex systems and worry about the implications of creating a model that perfectly reflects the world's complexity.

Overall, the conversation focuses on the capabilities, applications, and ethical considerations surrounding large language models in scientific discovery, particularly within the context of neuroscience predictions using Brain GPT.

### 20240606 - Hippocampus Histories： Memory, Navigation, & the New York Perspective with scientist John Kubie

The conversation revolves around the interplay between memory, navigation, and consciousness, drawing on insights from neuroscience and philosophy. The speakers discuss the role of the hippocampus in these processes, particularly its function as an index for memory and its potential involvement in planning and prediction.

1. Memory and Navigation: The speakers propose a two-stage model of decision-making, where animals (including rats) imagine various paths or strategies to reach a goal, selecting one based on their past experiences and creativity. This process involves constructing possible routes across space, which requires understanding the layout of the environment.

2. Hippocampus as an Index: The hippocampus is suggested to act as an index for memory, consolidating and organizing episodic memories. During sleep, there is selective consolidation of important episode trajectories associated with rewards, implying that the hippocampus helps in encoding and retrieving meaningful experiences.

3. Planning and Prediction: The speakers discuss the concept of planning or predictive processing in animals, such as rats imagining paths to a buried food source. This ability suggests that animals can construct mental representations of possible futures based on their understanding of the environment's layout.

4. Consciousness and Self-Awareness: The conversation touches upon the relationship between memory, navigation, and consciousness. While rats may not have a self-aware sense of identity like humans, they do exhibit behaviors that suggest some form of planning and deliberation. This raises questions about the continuity between animal and human cognitive processes, including the emergence of free will and conscious reflection on one's past and future.

5. The Two-Stage Model: This model, which dates back to at least William James, describes how animals (and humans) imagine various solutions to a problem before selecting one for action. Stage one involves playing out what-if scenarios, while stage two entails choosing the best option based on familiarity and creativity. This process is thought to undermine determinism by allowing for some degree of free will in decision-making.

6. Hippocampus and Social Context: The speakers also consider the hippocampus's role in social contexts, suggesting that it might help animals (and humans) understand and navigate complex social networks by encoding low-dimensional representations of these relationships.

In summary, the conversation highlights the intricate relationship between memory, navigation, and consciousness across various species, with a focus on the hippocampus's role as an index for episodic memories and its potential involvement in planning and prediction processes. The discussion also touches upon philosophical questions surrounding free will, self-awareness, and the continuity of cognitive processes between animals and humans.

4. **Pattern Recognition**: The conversation touches upon the role of patterns and regularities in memory and consciousness. Recognizing patterns can make information easier to remember, as seen in examples like golf courses or music.

### 20240614 - Consciousness, Cognition, and Joint Speech with cognitive scientist Fred Cummins and Andrea Hiott

The interviewee, Fred, shares his trajectory in cognitive science. He began as a nurse in the 1980s, inspired by Douglas Hofstadter's "Gödel, Escher, Bach" to pursue cognitive science. He studied computer science and linguistics at Indiana University during the early 1990s, a time when dynamics was a popular approach in cognitive science, influenced by emerging results in non-linear dynamics, chaos theory, fractal theory, attractors, strange attractors, chaos, and catastrophe theory.

Fred emphasizes the interdisciplinary nature of cognitive science, which pulls from various fields like computer science, linguistics, philosophy, psychology, neuroscience, mathematics, anthropology, semiotics, and more. He stresses that it's crucial to understand that cognitive science is not a new discipline but part of a thousand-year-old inquiry into the relationship between the knower, the known, and the means of knowing.

He criticizes classical cognitive science for often assuming the psychological subject or individual person as the knower, which he believes is an oversimplification. Instead, Fred advocates for a more general understanding that allows for collective, animal, or other non-individualistic interpretations of the knower.

Fred describes cognitive science as a field that acknowledges its limitations and aims to improve questioning rather than seek definitive answers. He suggests that this approach encourages humility and awareness of the complexities in understanding consciousness, cognition, and the self.

In teaching undergraduates, Fred uses a timeline with three key dates: 1600 (Descartes), 1850 (Darwin and the death of God), and 1945 (atomic bomb and cybernetics). This structure helps illustrate the tension between aspirational thinking and epistemic humility, as well as how scientific modernity, industrial revolution, and technological advancements have shaped our understanding of human nature and cognition.

Fred also discusses the distinction between consciousness and cognition. While cognition is often equated with problem-solving, thinking, or meeting specific standards, it's essential to separate this from consciousness. The scientific literature doesn't provide definitive answers about consciousness, leaving room for spiritual and philosophical traditions to explore the topic further.

In response to the linear nature of historical narratives in cognitive science, Fred emphasizes that these periods should be understood as shifts or changes in thinking, tools, society, and more, rather than progressive advancements. He's particularly interested in René Descartes' impact on mathematics and the coordinate plane, suggesting a connection between consciousness, mathematics, and cognitive science that transcends traditional dualistic interpretations.

### 20240621 - Thrutopia is the way forward： Any Human Power with author Manda Scott and philosopher Andrea Hiott

Manda Scott, author, veterinarian, and podcast host of "Accidental Gods," discusses her book "Any Human Power" and the concept of thrutopia. Thrutopia, as introduced by philosopher Rupert Reed, is a middle path between dystopia and utopia that shows how to navigate towards a better future without relying on miraculous interventions or assuming everything is already perfect. Scott's book explores this idea through the lens of a family story, focusing on themes of life, death, and the complex systems that govern our world.

Scott's journey into writing "Any Human Power" was influenced by her shamanic practices, regenerative economics education, and her podcast, which serves as an exploration of alternative paths to a sustainable future. The book presents a narrative about a woman who dies but returns to the living world due to a promise made to her grandson. This enables her to explore various timelines and discover ways to shift humanity's trajectory away from impending crises.

The novel highlights the importance of understanding our cognition as paths, emphasizing that complex systems thinking can help us perceive multiple paths to achieve desired outcomes rather than being confined by linear thinking. Scott's work underscores the significance of embracing technology and creativity within a value framework centered on respect for all life and flourishing for everyone within an ecosphere capable of sustaining itself.

Scott's writing process is informed by her shamanic practices, which involve entering alternate realities to access help with specific challenges. Her book integrates these experiences while maintaining a strong grounding in complex systems theory and the importance of heart mind (gratitude, compassion, and joyful curiosity) as a foundation for decision-making and connecting with the web of life.

The interview also touches upon themes like embodied cognition, phenomenology, and the role of storytelling in shaping human consciousness and our understanding of reality. Both Scott and her guest explore the idea that we each come at similar truths from different paths, emphasizing the importance of recognizing and valuing diverse ways of knowing and experiencing the world.

In summary, Manda Scott's "Any Human Power" presents a compelling narrative that intertwines family dynamics with complex systems theory to envision alternative futures. By embracing shamanic practices, regenerative economics, and heart mind principles, Scott challenges readers to question the limitations of linear thinking and consider a more holistic, connected approach to navigating our world's challenges. The book ultimately encourages us to recognize our inherent power as part of the living web and work together towards a sustainable future that respects all life forms.


### 20240623 - Language and Future A.I. Interactions with Max Bennett and Andrea Hiott Part Two

The discussion revolves around the evolutionary aspects of language, the brain's structure, and the future of AI. Here are key points summarized and explained:

1. **Evolution of Language**: The conversation begins by discussing how language evolved, suggesting that it is not genetically encoded but rather emerged from a combination of general learning systems and instincts that guide learning processes. This idea is compared to bird flight, where birds are born with the capacity to learn flying through specific instinctual behaviors (like flapping wings) and curricula (like jumping off high ledges).

2. **General Learning Systems**: The brain's neocortex might not contain unique language-specific algorithms but rather a general learning system that can be shaped by experiences, including cultural influences. This is likened to how guitarists and saxophonists use the same motor cortex for different skills due to varied learning paths.

3. **Instincts for Joint Attention and Proto-conversation**: Two critical instinctual behaviors facilitating language acquisition are identified: joint attention (sharing focus on an object) and proto-conversation (rhythmic turn-taking). These instincts enable declarative labeling and establish a foundation for communication.

4. **Evolutionary Challenges in Language**: Unlike the other four evolutionary breakthroughs, language's emergence is more complex due to its social nature. Individual benefits aren't immediately apparent, necessitating group-selection or multi-level selection theories. One proposed explanation is gossip, which serves as a deterrent against lying and betrayal, thus fostering cooperation within groups.

5. **Brain Structure**: The debate about dedicated language areas in the brain (like Broca's area) versus general learning systems with tuned curricula continues. Recent research suggests that the distinction between human and chimpanzee brains might lie more in subcortical structures governing instinctual behaviors, such as gaze following and joint attention.

6. **Future of AI**: The dialogue shifts to AI evolution, with a contrasting approach compared to biological evolution. Current transformer models prioritize language before adding other modalities (like vision or action)—a reverse direction from human brain development. This raises questions about the feasibility and desirability of this trajectory towards generalized AI.

7. **Transparency in AI**: Concerns are raised about the "black box" nature of large language models, where their inner workings remain obscure despite their impressive capabilities. Transparency challenges include understanding what data was used for training and how models make predictions.

8. **Future AI Developments**: Predictions lean towards embodied AI agents interacting with the real world—humanoid robots or AI-enabled appliances. The technological challenge lies in merging these systems seamlessly with human life, possibly leading to profound societal changes and philosophical questions about consciousness, relationships, and the definition of humanity itself.

9. **Ethics and Pluralism**: There's a call for open-minded exploration beyond anthropocentric views, valuing different forms of sentience equally. This includes considering AI entities alongside biological life in ethical frameworks, fostering a diverse ecosystem of conscious beings that coexist peacefully.

These points encapsulate the intricate interplay between evolutionary biology, neuroscience, and AI, highlighting ongoing debates and future possibilities within these fields.


### 20240626 - How to Model Cognition with Paul Kelly and Andrea Hiott： Beyond mechanistic and dynamical？

The 3M principle is a normative constraint proposed by mechanists, which outlines necessary conditions for a model to be considered explanatory within the context of cognitive science. The acronym "3M" stands for three components: Model-Mechanism Mapping, Model-Mechanism Monotonicity, and Model-Mechanism Magnitude. Here's a detailed explanation of each component:

1. **Model-Mechanism Mapping (M1)**: This requirement states that there must be at least one successful mapping between the model's components and the actual mechanisms in the world. In other words, the model should capture some aspect of the underlying mechanism accurately. The 3M principle argues that for a model to be explanatory, it needs to represent some part of the causal structure of the phenomena it aims to explain.

2. **Model-Mechanism Monotonicity (M2)**: This component asserts that as the level of detail in the model increases, so should the level of accuracy in mapping to the underlying mechanism. In other words, a more complex or refined model should provide a better fit with the actual mechanism it aims to explain. The principle suggests that there is an inherent monotonic relationship between the model's complexity and its explanatory power.

3. **Model-Mechanism Magnitude (M3)**: This requirement posits that the magnitude of explanation provided by a model should be directly proportional to the degree of fit with the underlying mechanism. In other words, the better the model captures the essential features of the mechanism, the more explanatory power it will have. This component emphasizes the importance of models capturing not just any aspect of the mechanism but rather its critical components and relationships.

The 3M principle has been influential in discussions surrounding cognitive science and the nature of explanation. However, it has also faced criticism for being overly restrictive or for failing to account for certain types of models that might still provide valuable explanations. Critics argue that the principle may exclude models like dynamical systems or equilibrium models, which can generate insights and understanding without necessarily fitting into a strict mechanistic framework.

The ongoing debate highlights the complexity of defining what constitutes an explanation in science and the need for nuanced discussions about the various ways in which models can provide insight into complex phenomena. The 3M principle, while influential, is just one perspective among many that scientists and philosophers engage with as they strive to understand and explain the world around us.


The conversation revolves around the nature of models and their role in scientific explanation and understanding. The speaker, a researcher, discusses the idea that different types of models (mechanistic and dynamical) can be used for various purposes, rather than choosing one over the other as definitively "right" or "wrong."

1. Model Types: Mechanistic models focus on identifying parts and their interactions to explain phenomena, while dynamical models emphasize patterns and processes. The speaker argues that both types can be explanatory depending on the context and purpose of the investigation.

2. Adequacy for Purpose Account (Parker): This philosophical perspective suggests evaluating models based on their suitability for specific purposes, such as description, prediction, explanation, or understanding. The speaker highlights climate science as an example where various models serve different aims, and none is considered universally "true."

3. Model as Surrogate: The speaker distinguishes between a model (surrogate) and the thing being represented (target). A model is more than just a stand-in; it requires a theoretical hypothesis connecting the surrogate to its target, making it a representation that can be investigated for insights about the target.

4. Language as Model: The speaker suggests language is not typically considered a model in the traditional sense, as it does not employ surrogative reasoning like external models do. However, language can express arguments, which may share some characteristics with model-based reasoning.

5. Aesthetic Representations: The conversation touches on representational aspects of artistic creations such as books, movies, or paintings. These objects might be seen as representations only if there are minds to recognize and interact with them, following an inferentialist perspective.

6. Purpose-Driven Analysis: The speaker emphasizes the importance of understanding the purpose behind a model (or representation) to determine its suitability for a given context. By examining patterns instead of focusing solely on parts, researchers can uncover new insights and develop more comprehensive models.

7. Unificationist Account: This philosophical perspective posits that explanation involves recognizing instances as part of broader structures or patterns. The speaker suggests this account might provide a framework for understanding how different models (e.g., mechanistic vs dynamical) could be explanatory in their respective domains, depending on the patterns they reveal.

8. Model Transfer: The practice of applying models developed in one domain to another, potentially uncovering shared dynamics and processes. This transfer can lead to novel explanations and understanding across disciplines.

9. Ontic vs Pragmatic Explanation: Mechanistic models are often associated with ontic explanation, focusing on the parts and their interactions as essential for understanding a phenomenon. In contrast, pragmatic explanation might prioritize patterns and processes to achieve explanatory goals. The speaker suggests that both approaches can be valuable, depending on the research question and context.

10. Challenges in Model Evaluation: The speaker acknowledges the complexity of evaluating models, especially when they serve different purposes or make conflicting predictions. They advocate for a nuanced understanding of model evaluation, recognizing that no single model is universally "true" but may be more or less suitable for specific aims.

Overall, the conversation underscores the value of considering multiple perspectives and model types in scientific investigation. It highlights the importance of aligning models with research purposes and understanding their limitations to develop a more comprehensive grasp of complex phenomena.


### 20240628 - Navigational Mind： Understanding Our Paths Through Life as we Hold Paradox with Andrea Hiott

The user is discussing two key concepts: waymaking and navigability, which are used to understand and analyze the processes of making one's way through various spaces and landscapes. 

1. Waymaking: This term encapsulates the broader concept of how an entity (human or non-human) navigates its environment. It encompasses all the ongoing, dynamic, shared movements and interactions within a given space. Waymaking is not something that can be fully measured or assessed because it's inherently fluid, complex, and contextual. It involves all aspects of an entity's engagement with its surroundings, including sensory inputs (like sight, sound, touch), social interactions, personal history, and embodied position.

2. Navigability: This is a more specific concept that refers to the external representation or modeling of waymaking trajectories within particular landscapes or contexts. Navigability is about visualizing and understanding the possibilities for movement or action within given spaces – be it geographical, social, emotional, economic, etc. 

The user explains that different entities (humans, animals, systems) have distinct navigabilities based on their unique characteristics, histories, and embodied positions. For example, one's ability to navigate a city might be influenced by factors like economic status, social acceptance, physical abilities, or personal preferences.

Navigability is crucial because it allows us to understand and compare the affordances – or potential for action – available in different spaces for different entities. This understanding can reveal disparities and biases that might otherwise go unnoticed, enabling us to work towards more equitable designs of physical spaces, social systems, and conceptual frameworks.

The user emphasizes the importance of recognizing the multitude of ways in which entities navigate their worlds and how these navigabilities are shaped by a myriad of factors. By considering various types of navigabilities – such as economic, geographical, or social – we can develop a more nuanced understanding of accessibility, livability, and waymaking across diverse contexts.

In essence, while waymaking is the all-encompassing, inherently unmeasurable process of making one's way through life, navigability is our attempt to model or visualize aspects of this process externally, aiming to reveal patterns, biases, and possibilities for improvement.

Bildow's work contributes to broader discussions on heritage, sustainability, labor, ownership, and the importance of considering local practices and beliefs when shaping urban spaces. ### 20240702 - Research Diary Ramblings 5, Open-hearted Vulnerability towards improving ideas (high nerd score)

The text presents a novel perspective on cognition, suggesting it can be understood through the framework of "navigability." This approach views cognition not as a singular, reflective process confined to human brains but as a broader phenomenon that includes all entities capable of navigating their environments. Here's a detailed breakdown:

1. **Cognition as Navigability**: The author proposes that cognition is fundamentally about navigating landscapes, which can be physical (geographical), linguistic, conceptual, social, or virtual. This landscape could be anything from an office building to a social media platform, even one's own thoughts or body. 

2. **Multidimensional Continuums of Cognition**: Within this framework, cognition is not viewed as a single continuum but as multiple interconnected, overlapping dimensions. These dimensions vary based on the embodied subject (like different bodies) and the landscape in which navigation occurs (like different environments).

3. **Affordance Vector**: A crucial concept here is the "affordance vector," a trajectory from an entity towards a goal that doesn't rely on another entity for its relevance. For instance, a hammer or fence post isn't cognitive because their affordances (how they can be used) depend on a human user and thus don't form an independent affordance vector.

4. **Embodied Acts**: An act is considered cognitive if it's directed towards a goal in some landscape that can be parameterized or symbolized, with the affordance vector originating from the entity itself, not reliant on another for relevance. This definition allows for a clear distinction between cognitive and non-cognitive entities.

5. **Phenomenological Tradition**: The author grounds this framework in phenomenology, a philosophical movement focused on studying conscious experiences from the first-person perspective. By doing so, they aim to widen evidence gathering across traditional subjective-objective dichotomies and develop applications for understanding mental and environmental health.

6. **Implications**: This paradigm shift could impact various fields, including urban design (how we structure cities), transportation systems, social media platforms, and even our personal interactions with our bodies and thoughts. It also opens up new avenues for interdisciplinary research, merging phenomenology with cognitive science, neuroscience, and developmental biology.

7. **Future Directions**: The author plans to delve deeper into these concepts, explaining terms like 'body', 'affordance', 'trajectory', and 'landscape' in the context of their usage across different disciplines. They aim to explore how this framework connects with existing cognitive science, neuroscience, and developmental biology, particularly focusing on the hippocampus as a case study for memory and navigation.

In essence, this text presents an innovative way of understanding cognition that goes beyond traditional human-centric views to encompass all navigating entities within their environments. It's an attempt to bridge subjective experiences (phenomenology) with objective scientific investigation (cognitive science), potentially leading to new insights into mental health, environmental design, and our relationship with technology.
