### 20240725 - The Importance of Place with neuroscientist Richard Morris (Edinburgh) & Andrea Hiott

Richard Morris is a renowned neuroscientist known for his work on memory, particularly the hippocampus and its role in spatial navigation. His journey began with a PhD in experimental psychology at Sussex University, where he became interested in John O'Keefe's and Lynn Nadel's work on cognitive maps and place cells.

After his PhD, Morris took a detour from academia to work as a researcher for the BBC, focusing on science stories for live television programs. This experience piqued his interest in neuroscience once again, leading him back to academic research at the University of Edinburgh.

The turning point in Morris' career came when he was appointed to a department with no lab facilities. In search of space for his research, he ended up at the Gatty Marine Laboratory on the coast. There, inspired by observing swimming marine life, he developed the Morris Water Maze, an innovative tool for studying spatial learning and memory independent of sensory cues.

The water maze allowed Morris to investigate the hippocampus's role in navigation and spatial memory further. He found that damaging the hippocampus impaired rats' ability to navigate the maze, suggesting a causal relationship between the two. This discovery contributed significantly to our understanding of the hippocampus as a critical component of the brain's cognitive map.

Morris' work on the water maze has had lasting impacts on neuroscience. It paved the way for studying spatial memory and learning without relying on visual or auditory cues, which is crucial in understanding how animals (and potentially humans) maintain a sense of place independent of sensory input.

Moreover, Morris' research has played an essential role in uncovering the mechanisms underlying spatial memory and learning. By demonstrating the hippocampus's importance in these processes, his work has contributed to broader discussions on memory formation, consolidation, and the brain's ability to encode information about space.

The Morris Water Maze has also inspired further research into the neurobiological underpinnings of spatial memory, leading to discoveries like grid cells and border cells in the hippocampus, which provide a more detailed description of an animal's position within its environment.

In recent years, Morris has continued exploring the relationship between spatial memory and episodic memory, proposing that the rich spatial representations encoded by place cells might also serve as a framework for remembering events (what happened) in conjunction with their location (where it happened). He is currently investigating whether rats are capable of thinking about future destinations before navigating to them using advanced imaging techniques like calcium imaging, which allows the observation of hundreds of neurons simultaneously.

Overall, Richard Morris' work on spatial memory and navigation has significantly advanced our understanding of hippocampal function and its role in cognition. His discoveries have not only deepened scientific knowledge but also inspired new lines of inquiry, contributing to the broader goal of deciphering the mysteries of the human mind.

She argues that AI is not intelligent or conscious like humans but can mimic certain aspects of our lived experiences. Santa Catarina emphasizes that human intelligence encompasses complex elements such as intuition, instinct, imagination, belief, motivation, and the ability to reason about the whole of existence rather than just a fragment. She criticizes the reductionist approach in computer science, which views the brain as a computational device, denying our human identity, dignity, and essence.

The author advocates for a reconceptualization of the human-AI relationship, placing technology within the right context and dimension. Santa Catarina is concerned about the ethical implications of using AI to determine care or treatment based on a few variables, which may not capture an individual's unique experiences and knowledge.

She also warns against the oversimplification of human intelligence as a computational process and argues that we should value human potential in organizations rather than reducing people to tasks or atomized components. The author encourages businesses to embrace a global perspective, fostering cultural awareness, critical thinking, and the art of leadership.

Santa Catarina further discusses international relations as an example of human relationships at the macro level, focusing on self-preservation rather than self-interest. She emphasizes that people have a need for meaningful experiences and connections with others, which cannot be replicated by AI. In this context, technology should support human capabilities instead of replacing them.

The author also addresses the historical progression of societal relations and technological advancements, highlighting how the atomization of life has become problematic due to varying levels of understanding across different disciplines. Santa Catarina argues that while AI can be beneficial in certain aspects, it should not replace human agency and autonomy.

In summary, Maria Santa Catarina's Adaptive Resilience book highlights the importance of distinguishing between human and artificial intelligence, valuing human potential, and using technology thoughtfully and ethically to enhance our lives and the world around us. She encourages a global perspective, cultural awareness, critical thinking, and the development of meaningful relationships among people while leveraging AI for specific tasks, rather than replacing human capabilities or autonomy.

This exposure to open discussions about relationships and intimacy has influenced Lori's work.

The relationship between Lori and her mother was complex. Despite this, Lori felt a disconnect from her mother's more traditional approach to femininity, as she herself identified as staunchly feminist from an early age.

Lori's mother gave up horseback riding after getting married and adopted domestic responsibilities instead. This decision puzzled Lori, who saw it as a missed opportunity for her mother to pursue her passion for horses. The loss of both parents within two years added emotional turmoil to Lori's life.

The deaths prompted a period of intense grief and self-reflection. Lori found solace in motorcycle riding, which she had always wanted to do but was forbidden by her father. After his passing, she began taking lessons and eventually bought her own purple motorcycle for her 40th birthday. This new hobby became a stabilizing force during a challenging time.

Lori's films, such as "The Pilgrimage" and "A Motorcycle Saved My Life," explore themes of acceptance, self-love, and freedom. Her mother's influence, the complexities of her relationship with her parents, and her personal journey of grief and healing are reflected in her work. ### 20240906 - Pattern Language & Ways of Wholeness, on Christopher Alexander with David Seamon & Andrea Hiott

The conversation revolves around the concept of wholeness, place, and phenomenology as explored by geographer David Seaman. The discussion begins with Seaman's childhood experience on a plank overlooking a creek, which sparked his interest in understanding the interconnectedness between human beings and their environment. This led him to study geography, focusing on humanistic phenomenology, which emphasizes the first-person experience of place and its meaning for individuals.

Seaman's work is distinguished by his unique approach to phenomenology, which he describes as a "phenomenology of wholeness." He advocates for an understanding of wholeness that goes beyond traditional systems theory and instead focuses on the presencing of the whole within its parts. This concept is inspired by the work of Henry Bortoff, particularly his book "The Wholeness of Nature," which explores how phenomenology can reveal the underlying structure of natural forms.

Seaman argues that this understanding of wholeness allows for a more holistic approach to studying place and human experience in geography. He emphasizes the importance of holding both inside (individual) and outside (environmental) perspectives simultaneously, without reducing one to the other. This is a departure from conventional geographical research, which often focuses solely on the external environment.

The conversation then shifts to discussing Christopher Alexander's work, specifically his book "A Pattern Language." Seaman sees parallels between Alexander's approach and his own phenomenological perspective, particularly in Alexander's emphasis on movement, dynamic processes, and the importance of understanding place as an ongoing experience rather than a static entity.

Seaman introduces the concept of 'place release,' describing it as environmental serendipity—the idea that many significant life events occur due to chance encounters in specific places. He suggests this concept can help people recognize the profound impact that places have on their lives, fostering a sense of connection and appreciation for the role of place in human experience.

The discussion also touches upon the dichotomy between analytic relationality (the common approach that breaks things down into parts) and synergistic relationality (Seaman's preferred method, focusing on understanding whole systems through empathy and care). Seaman argues for a balance between these approaches, recognizing the value of both reductionist methods in certain contexts (such as surgery or dealing with damage) and holistic phenomenological methods that embrace complexity and interconnectedness.

Throughout the conversation, Seaman underscores the importance of place as an experience and the need to reconsider our understanding of human existence within this context. He emphasizes how his work and others' (such as Christopher Alexander's) contribute to a more nuanced appreciation for the role of place in shaping our lives, identity, and environmental humility.

The conversation concludes with Seaman recommending various key works for further exploration, including Henry Bortoff's "The Wholeness of Nature," Edward Casey's "Getting Back into Place," and his own publications on phenomenology and place. The speakers agree that understanding wholeness through a phenomenological lens offers valuable insights into the complex interplay between human beings and their environments, ultimately contributing to a more sustainable and appreciative relationship with the world around us.


### 20240915 - A New Age of the Web： From Printing Press to the Coming Spatial Experience with Dan Mapes

This conversation revolves around the evolution of human technology, from hunter-gatherer times to the digital age, and how these advancements have fundamentally altered our societies. Here are some key points discussed:

1. **Technology as Body Extension**: The initial technologies were extensions of the human body, such as spears for hunting or wheels for transportation. Later inventions like the printing press extended our capacity to record and disseminate knowledge. 

2. **Industrial Revolution**: This period marked a significant shift from an agrarian society to industrialization, characterized by factories, corporations, and a burgeoning middle class. It also introduced large-scale resource extraction and global trade.

3. **Computer Age (1945 onwards)**: The invention of the computer revolutionized information processing, starting with simple calculations in 1945. By 1970, computers were widely adopted by corporations and governments for data management. However, a major vulnerability was identified when it was realized that all these systems depended on one central hub, posing risks from single-point failures or attacks.

4. **Birth of the Internet (1969)**: The development of the internet as we know it began in 1969 with ARPANET, a project funded by the U.S. Department of Defense. Initially for communication between four universities, it later evolved into a global network that allows sharing text files and eventually fully formatted documents (web pages).

5. **World Wide Web (1980s-1990s)**: Tim Berners-Lee introduced the World Wide Web in 1989, enabling easy access to multimedia content over the internet using HTTP and HTML protocols. This marked a significant leap from plain text files to rich digital media accessible worldwide.

6. **Spatial Web (Present/Future)**: The current conversation shifts towards the concept of the Spatial Web – an evolution beyond the 2D web. Unlike the flat, linear nature of the World Wide Web, the Spatial Web promises a tridimensional, interactive space where data is visualized in three dimensions. It integrates augmented reality (AR), virtual reality (VR), and mixed reality (MR) technologies to create a more immersive digital experience.

7. **Active Inference AI**: This emerging field of artificial intelligence aims to create models that learn and adapt like living systems, following the Free Energy Principle. Instead of static, monolithic AIs, this approach envisions a web of interconnected, self-evolving AI 'tools' or 'agents,' each specialized in specific domains (like nutrition, history, or farming) and capable of evolving independently yet communicating with others to form collective intelligence.

8. **Decentralization and Abundance**: The Spatial Web is expected to foster a more decentralized, abundant digital ecosystem, allowing for hyper-local development and specialized AI tools that cater to various cultural, environmental, and individual needs without the need for massive, centralized infrastructure.

9. **Philosophical Perspectives**: The conversation also touches on philosophical themes such as embodied cognition (the idea that our cognitive processes are deeply rooted in our bodily interactions with the world), and how technological advancements reshape human understanding of self, society, and reality.

10. **Trust in Human Evolution**: The speaker expresses trust in humanity's ability to innovate and adapt, emphasizing that providing people with better tools can lead to unforeseen breakthroughs driving positive societal change. This perspective is reflected in the concept of the Spatial Web as a platform for distributed, evolving AI, mirroring the natural evolutionary processes observed in living systems.

### 20240923 - Among Superheroes： memories, maps & McGill with psychologist Lynn Nadel and Andrea Hiott Audio

The conversation between Lynn Nadell and an interviewer revolves around Lynn's early years as a neuroscience researcher, focusing on the hippocampus and its role in cognition and navigation. Here is a detailed summary of the key points:

1. Early interests and switch to psychology: Lynn initially pursued chemistry at McGill University but switched to biology after realizing it wasn't for him. He became interested in psychology, taking various courses, including an introductory course taught by Donald Hebb.

2. Montreal's neuroscience hub: Lynn describes the vibrant and politically engaged atmosphere of McGill University's Psychology Department during the 1950s and 60s, where he, John O'Keefe, Brenda Milner, and others were shaping the field.

3. Influential figures: Lynn mentions the influences of Edward C. Tolman, whose work on latent learning and cognitive maps inspired Lynn's later research. He also highlights Donald Hebb, who introduced the concept of "neurons that fire together wire together" (Hebbian learning).

4. Hippocampus research: Lynn explains his PhD focus on the hippocampus after observing a discrepancy between human and animal memory studies following surgeries on the hippocampus. He proposes that different parts of the hippocampus (dorsal vs ventral) may have distinct functions, which he later revisited in his research.

5. Connection to politics: Lynn and John O'Keefe shared political interests, bonding over their socialist leanings during a time when Montreal's Jewish community was progressive and socialist. However, they did not connect these politics directly to their scientific work.

6. Move to Prague: Lynn decided to pursue a postdoc in Prague after being inspired by Philip Liss, who had gone there for research before the Russian invasion of 1968. Lynn and his family moved to Eastern Europe, experiencing culture shock and adapting to life with limited language skills.

7. Collaboration with John O'Keefe: After returning from Prague following the invasion, Lynn reunited with John O'Keefe in London. They both secured lectureships at the University College London (UCL) and began collaborating on groundbreaking research on place cells and cognitive maps within the hippocampus.

8. The "play cell" discovery: When John O'Keefe discovered place cells, Lynn's prior work on the hippocampus allowed him to quickly recognize its potential implications for understanding spatial memory and navigation. Together, they began exploring a framework for cognitive neuroscience that would later incorporate predictive processing theory.

9. The book project: Inspired by their collaborative research, Lynn and John embarked on a six-year project to write a comprehensive monograph on the hippocampus and its role in cognition. They sought feedback from various experts across disciplines, ultimately producing a seminal work that shaped modern neuroscience.

Overall, Lynn Nadell's early career as a neuroscientist was marked by key influences like Edward C. Tolman and Donald Hebb, collaborations with fellow researchers such as John O'Keefe, and a pivotal move to Prague that ultimately facilitated his groundbreaking work on the hippocampus and cognitive maps.

### 20240923 - Audio of Intention and Elizabeth Anscombe with Mirko Prokop

In this conversation, two individuals discuss the concept of intentional action and its relation to perception, action, and cognition from an ecological perspective. They reference various philosophical and scientific works, including those by Ludwig von Bertalanffy, Evan Thompson, and Shaun Gallagher.

1. **Ecological Perspective on Perception and Action**: The conversation begins with the ecological perspective, which views perception and action as intertwined processes. In this framework, an organism's perception is not passive but active; it is shaped by the organism's history, body, and environment. This means that what we perceive is influenced by our past experiences and our ongoing interactions with the world.

2. **Affordances**: The concept of affordances plays a significant role in this discussion. Affordances refer to the possibilities for action provided by the environment. They are not properties of the environment itself but rather the relationship between the organism and its environment. For instance, a glass might afford drinking for a human but not for a bird.

3. **Intentional Action**: The conversation delves into the nature of intentional action. The individuals discuss whether intentions are necessary for perceiving affordances. They consider examples where an individual might not perceive relevant affordances due to their goals or intentions. For instance, if someone is thirsty and looking for a drink but there's no visible source of water (no affordance), they might still explore the environment based on their intention to find something to drink.

4. **Anscombe's Perspective**: The authors propose integrating Anscombe's understanding of intention with ecological psychology. They suggest that intentions can modulate the affordances we perceive without relying on internal representations of goals projected onto the environment. Instead, the intention to achieve a goal restricts which affordances are relevant or salient in a given situation.

5. **Interdisciplinary Approach**: The conversation emphasizes the importance of an interdisciplinary approach when exploring these concepts. Combining philosophical perspectives, such as Anscombe's account of intention, with scientific understandings of cognition and the mind-body relationship can yield richer insights.

6. **Beyond Dichotomies**: The individuals stress the value of moving beyond traditional dichotomies (e.g., mind vs. body, internal vs. external) when considering intentional action. They argue that a more nuanced understanding emerges when we recognize the complex interplay between an organism's history, body, environment, and goals.

In summary, this conversation explores the intricate relationship between perception, action, cognition, and intention from an ecological perspective. It highlights the role of affordances, intentions, and the importance of integrating philosophical insights with scientific understandings to develop a more comprehensive grasp of these concepts. The discussion also underscores the need for interdisciplinary approaches and moving beyond simplistic dichotomies when examining intentional action.


### 20240923 - Cognitive Scales and the Powers of Science Fiction with Michael Levin and Andrea Hiott Audio

In this conversation, Andrea and Michael Levin discuss their perspectives on cognition, consciousness, and the nature of intelligence. Here's a detailed summary and explanation of key points:

1. Early influences: Both scientists were inspired by science fiction as children, which helped them think beyond conventional dichotomies (e.g., machine vs. living organism). This early exposure to imaginative scenarios allowed them to consider a continuum between different entities exhibiting various degrees of complexity and agency.

2. Interest in embodied minds: Levin's research focuses on understanding diverse embodiments of mind, with an emphasis on how different biological systems can manifest intelligence and agency. Andrea shares a similar interest, aiming to create a philosophical framework that unites various scientific disciplines studying cognition.

3. Challenging dichotomies: Both researchers challenge the distinction between machines (artificial or natural) and living organisms, arguing that there is no strict boundary separating them. They believe in a spectrum of agency and intelligence across different scales and systems.

4. Measurement and tools: Levin suggests that the reason physicists tend to see machines rather than minds is due to using low-agency measurement tools (e.g., voltmeters, rulers). He proposes that high-agency tools could reveal more complex processes, like cognition occurring at various scales in different species.

5. Neurons and cells: Levin argues that neurons are not fundamentally different from other cells; they all share similar properties and can be seen as part of a collective intelligence driving morphogenesis—the process by which an organism's shape develops. This view challenges the traditional distinction between neural and non-neural cells.

6. Cognition as navigation: Levin introduces the concept of cognitive light cones, describing it as the scale of goals that an individual can pursue based on their cognitive abilities. He suggests that understanding cognition as navigation (or way-making) across various spaces (e.g., three-dimensional space, transcriptional space, or anatomical morphological space) could provide a more comprehensive perspective on intelligence in diverse systems.

7. Observer and agency: The conversation touches upon the relationship between the observer and what it means to be an agent. Levin proposes that being an agent involves having preferences, valence, and the ability to pursue goals within certain spaces (e.g., spatial, temporal, or physiological). This ability to navigate and adapt to different environments is central to his definition of agency.

8. Regeneration: Both scientists express interest in the concept of regeneration—the ability of an organism to regrow lost parts or even entire structures. They speculate on the possibility of entities regenerating without sharing the typical regularities associated with their developmental trajectories, hinting at potential future research directions in this area.

Overall, Andrea and Levin share a fascination with understanding intelligence and agency across diverse systems, challenging traditional boundaries between machines and living organisms. They emphasize the importance of employing appropriate measurement tools to reveal complex processes like cognition occurring at various scales in different species. Their conversation highlights the value of interdisciplinary approaches in exploring these fundamental questions about mind and intelligence.


### 20240923 - Curious Minds & Edge-working with Perry Zurn and Andrea Hiott Audio Only

Perry's discussion on Love & Philosophy revolves around the interconnectedness of love and curiosity, exploring how they inform our understanding and actions in life. Perry's work, along with his twin Danny, focuses on curiosity studies, particularly in their recent book "Curious Minds."

1. Curiosity as Love: Perry acknowledges an implicit connection between love and curiosity, noting that early Latin interpretations of curiosity involved desire, lust, and love. Today, this is reflected in our curiosity about subjects we genuinely love to know and think about.

2. Love as a Companion for Curiosity: Perry suggests that love can serve as a companion to curiosity, guiding it towards respectful forms of knowledge acquisition and inquiry. Without love or relational affection, curiosity might become more appropriative or objectifying.

3. The Love of Wisdom (Philosophy): Perry reflects on his own journey into philosophy, driven by the desire for more intellectual freedom and a space to ask questions. Philosophy, for him, is the love of wisdom – an inquisitive pursuit that expands one's worldview.

4. Curiosity as Connection or Practice: Perry redefines curiosity from its traditional acquisitional notion to a capacity or practice. He views curiosity socially, as something we do with and for others, emphasizing connections rather than isolated information gathering.

5. Edge Work and Curiosity: Perry discusses how curiosity can be seen as edge work – the act of building edges between nodes to create connections. This involves taking risks and exploring peripheries of existing knowledge networks.

6. Complexity Science in Curiosity Studies: Collaborating with his twin Danny, a physicist, Perry integrates complexity science into their curiosity studies. They use network theory to understand how the brain works in concert and the multiple systems involved in inquiry. This leads to a nuanced understanding of different styles or types of curiosity, like busybody, hunter, and dancer.

7. Walking as Metaphor for Curiosity: Perry uses walking metaphors to illustrate how we think and approach knowledge. Different walking styles represent diverse ways of being curious, depending on our individual training, patterns of thinking, and social inheritance.

8. Expanding the Definition of Curiosity: Perry challenges the traditional view that curiosity is a human-exclusive capacity by suggesting it's more than human – shared with animals, plants, and even the universe itself. He argues this perspective could have altered our relationships with other beings and the environment if considered from the outset.

9. Cracks as Metaphor for Future and Hope: Perry employs the concept of "cracks" to describe both destruction and creation in various contexts, including networks, geology, psychology, and sociology. This metaphor symbolizes hope and optimism – the idea that cracks allow light (new possibilities) to enter our lives, fostering growth and change.

10. TS Eliot's Love as a Framework for Curiosity: Perry uses TS Eliot's words of love to frame curiosity, emphasizing daring to disturb the universe – a theme that resonates with both love relationships and inquisitive pursuits. This connection highlights how curiosity can be seen as an extension of love's transformative power.

11. Dedication to Children: The book is dedicated to "all the children who question whether it needs to be this way," acknowledging the powerful, often unrecognized curiosity in children and their capacity for challenging societal norms. This dedication underscores the importance of nurturing and valuing children's inherent curiosity, which can lead to personal growth and societal transformation.

### 20240923 - From Ants to Active Inference with Daniel Ari Friedman and Andrea Hiott Audio Only

In this conversation, Daniel R.E. Friedman of the Active Inference Institute discusses his background in studying ants with Deborah Gordon and how it relates to understanding complex systems, individuality, and collective behavior. He explains that while working on gene expression analysis and neurotransmitter levels in foraging ants, he became interested in questions surrounding the nature of individuality within colonies and how we define "individual" versus "collective."

Daniel introduces Active Inference as a framework developed by Carl Friston, which describes cognition through perception, action, and minimizing free energy. He explains that this model involves an interface or Markov blanket that separates inbound (perception) and outbound (action) dependencies, with everything else being cognition. Daniel highlights the importance of scale-free and scale-friendly approaches in understanding systems across various scales.

The conversation touches upon the philosophical aspects of science, questioning how we define terms like "cognition," "individual," and "collective." Daniel emphasizes that Active Inference is a system-independent framework, allowing for applications across different species and scales. He discusses the importance of being aware of one's assumptions when applying this framework to various systems and the need for continuous investigation to understand cognition in diverse entities.

Throughout the conversation, Daniel shares his thoughts on how studying ants can provide insights into broader questions about complex systems, individuality, and collective behavior. He discusses the importance of being open-minded and flexible when defining terms like "cognition" across different scales and systems, ultimately aiming for intersubjectivity in scientific discourse. The conversation also touches on the Active Inference Institute's mission and its focus on fostering collaboration, openness, and understanding of cognition through active inference principles.

### 20240923 - I Am Because We Are： New approaches to sustainable heritage with Jonathan Doe Audio Podcast

The interview with Andrea explores her academic journey, focusing on her Master's project titled "Keta Lagoon: Uncovering Suppressed Heritage Practices for Sustainable Wetland Management." Born and raised in Ghana, Andrea pursued a Bachelor's degree in Philosophy with a minor in History. Afterward, she engaged in freelance tour guiding at UNESCO World Heritage sites, which sparked her interest in museum and heritage studies.

Andrea proceeded to study an MA in Museum and Heritage Studies at the University of Ghana, with a shift towards understanding UNESCO World Heritage, philosophy of nature, and the interaction between cultural history and ecological management in wetlands. This interest led her to explore the Keta Lagoon, a significant wetland area in Ghana.

The Keta Lagoon is crucial for understanding suppressed heritage practices due to its historical significance and cultural importance to local communities. Andrea's research uncovers how these heritage practices have been overlooked or marginalized, particularly within the framework of contemporary wetland management strategies influenced by international conventions like Ramsar.

The Ramsar Convention, established in 1971, aims to conserve and sustainably use wetlands for the benefit of humankind while maintaining ecological processes. However, Andrea's research reveals a disconnect between these international guidelines and local practices. This discrepancy arises due to historical suppression – colonialism's lasting impact on indigenous knowledge systems, leading to the exclusion of traditional practices in modern wetland management plans.

Andrea identifies specific examples of suppressed heritage practices, such as diviners and priests who hold unique cultural knowledge about the lagoon. These individuals embody traditions that could inform sustainable wetland management but are often overlooked or ignored in current practices. Andrea advocates for a more holistic approach to wetland management, one that incorporates local heritage and indigenous knowledge systems alongside modern scientific methods.

Her Master's project focuses on the Keta Lagoon fishing practice called "Atskita." This method employs sustainable techniques like creating temporary fish habitats using branches and nets, minimizing overfishing and environmental impact. Atskita also demonstrates the importance of time and temporality in local ownership concepts, as visible markers indicate who currently cares for a specific area within the lagoon commons.

The Keta Lagoon represents a broader issue: how historical suppression has led to the marginalization of traditional knowledge systems in contemporary wetland management practices. By reintroducing and valuing these suppressed heritage practices, Andrea proposes an inclusive approach that benefits both ecological sustainability and local communities. This research contributes significantly to discussions on decolonizing knowledge and developing more nuanced, context-sensitive approaches to environmental conservation.

6. **Historical Context of Mathematics**: The speaker points out that even seemingly objective fields like mathematics are subject to historical and cultural influences. For instance, Descartes' coordinate system fundamentally changed geometry, giving one point a distinguished role that wasn't part of the original discipline.

7. **Linear Thinking vs. Nested Open Mindset**: The speaker critiques linear thinking, which assumes a beginning, end, and single correct path. They argue for a "nested open mindset," recognizing that all we create perpetuates what's possible and that fundamental questions about change, time, and space remain unanswered despite advancements in science.

8. **Teaching and Learning**: The speaker reflects on their approach to teaching, emphasizing the importance of sharing experiences and stories without claiming expertise. They advocate for a non-psychological stance that allows students to critically evaluate different narratives and understand themselves as part of larger patterns rather than separate entities.

Throughout the conversation, the speaker draws connections between various disciplines, including cognitive science, Buddhism, philosophy, and history, emphasizing the interconnectedness of knowledge and the importance of challenging assumptions in scientific inquiry.

### 20240923 - Love is Mutual Transformative Change with Richard Watson and Andrea Hiott Audio Only

In this conversation, Richard Watson discusses his alternative perspective on evolution and adaptation, challenging the dominant view of Universal Darwinism. He argues that while natural selection is a powerful mechanism for biological adaptation, it doesn't fully account for complex phenomena like cooperation, multicellularity, and the emergence of higher levels of organization in living systems.

Watson introduces the concept of "mutual transformative change" as an alternative to competition-based natural selection. This process involves entities altering their relationships with one another, leading to new properties and behaviors that weren't present before. He suggests that this type of transformation can occur at various scales, from cells to ecosystems, and is not limited to evolutionary units (i.e., organisms capable of self-reproduction).

The conversation also explores the relationship between love, vulnerability, and cognition. Watson defines love as deeply vulnerable, mutual knowing, emphasizing its importance in understanding living systems. He contrasts this with a traditional scientific view that assumes objective, value-free observation and reductionist logic (science/logic).

Watson argues that the fear of loss of self drives the motivation to fit everything into a competitive, self-interested framework (natural selection) rather than embracing vulnerability and mutual connection. He suggests that love is a more fundamental principle in understanding the universe and living systems, as it involves creating new relationships, interactions, and levels of organization through relating, not just persisting.

The discussion touches on topics such as information integration, collective action, and the scale nestedness of living systems. Watson proposes that agency and cognition are primary characteristics of life, occurring at various scales regardless of whether entities are evolutionary units or not. He critiques the idea that agency is solely a product of evolution by natural selection, arguing instead that acting in ways that exhibit information integration and collective action is fundamental to living systems.

The conversation also delves into the tension between reductionist logic (focusing on individual parts) and holistic understanding (considering relationships and interactions). Watson suggests that love, as a principle of relating, offers a more comprehensive framework for understanding complex phenomena in living systems and the universe.

In summary, Richard Watson presents an alternative perspective on evolution and adaptation, emphasizing mutual transformative change over competition-based natural selection. He explores the role of love, vulnerability, and cognition in understanding living systems and challenges the reductionist logic that dominates scientific thought. By integrating these concepts, he offers a more holistic framework for examining life's complexity and emergent properties across various scales.

By changing how certain concepts are perceived or associated, communities can challenge and alter established patterns within their social environment.

Mal explains that these terms are often used differently across disciplines but generally refer to the capacity for an agent to integrate information and act upon it.

### 20240923 - Nature and Clarity with Mike Levin and Andrea Hiott Audio Only

In this conversation, Mike Levin discusses the intersection of nature, art, technology, and dichotomies with his interviewer. He emphasizes the importance of both nature and personal enjoyment of art in his life, describing activities like photography, gardening, and kayaking as meditative and thought-provoking experiences.

Levin challenges the binary distinction between 'natural' and 'technological,' arguing that all biology involves manipulation and hacking, which is a natural process itself. He criticizes the idea that nature is inherently good or privileged over human-engineered solutions because evolution doesn't optimize for what humans value (e.g., happiness, intelligence).

He expresses concern about people using 'natural' as an excuse to avoid making difficult decisions regarding environmental issues and technological advancements. Levin advocates for a more nuanced understanding of continuums rather than dichotomies when considering scales in ecology and technology, suggesting that human intervention can potentially make systems healthier if done thoughtfully.

The conversation then turns to the concept of 'anthrobots' – synthetic biological entities created through his lab's work – and how they relate to broader ideas about minds and agency. Levin explains his philosophical perspective that terms like 'robot' or 'machine' are engineering protocols, not claims about the system itself; rather, they indicate one's reference frame and interaction approach.

Levin discusses the idea of a "biobot," which he defines as a system amenable to control and manipulation. He highlights how different stances (or frames) for interacting with a system can facilitate or hinder understanding and problem-solving, using examples like orthopedic surgery and psychotherapy.

The discussion also touches on the idea of personalized medicine, where treatments are tailored to individual patients based on their unique biological makeup. Levin believes this approach will become increasingly feasible with advancements in sensors, AI, and our understanding of cellular communication. He envisions using one's own cells (from adult patients) to create these therapeutic constructs, eliminating the need for genetic manipulation or immune suppression.

Levin also reflects on the themes of competition, cooperation, and struggle within biological systems. He explains that being a successful agent involves maintaining homeostasis – preferring certain states and having stressors when deviating from them – but emphasizes that both competition and cooperation are valuable strategies for agents to navigate their environments.

The conversation concludes with Levin's views on suffering, death, and the role of human intervention in alleviating it. He acknowledges that many people suffer due to factors beyond their control (e.g., genetics, cosmic rays) and argues for improving our 'embodiment' through technology and science so we can focus on deeper philosophical questions.

Lastly, Levin discusses the concept of patterns and dimensions of thinking, hinting at a separate conversation about multi-dimensional thinking versus linear thinking when approaching skills development or problem-solving.

He emphasizes the importance of understanding place not just as an external object but as a lived experience intertwined with one's self.

Seaman traces his fascination with wholeness back to a childhood experience lying on a plank overlooking a creek, where he pondered how the world is always present around him, seemingly by "miracle." This question led him to geography and phenomenology as a way to understand this interconnectedness.

Phenomenology, which focuses on first-person experience and subjective interpretation of reality, was initially underrepresented in geography but found resonance with Seaman's research interests. He highlights how geography uniquely holds together the human and world aspects, unlike other disciplines that often sever them.

Seaman discusses Christopher Alexander's work, particularly his book "A Pattern Language," which offers a method for designing places by considering the whole rather than just individual parts. This approach aligns with Seaman's emphasis on synergistic relationality – understanding wholeness as interconnected relationships rather than analytical separation.

The conversation also touches upon the concept of place release, which refers to environmental serendipity – fortuitous encounters that shape our lives and experiences within a place. Seaman illustrates this with personal anecdotes and discusses how such chance occurrences can significantly impact one's life trajectory.

Seaman further delves into the idea of nestedness in places, from intimate experiences within a room to broader implications for our planet's well-being. He posits that understanding place as a phenomenological experience is crucial for individual and collective health.

Throughout the discussion, Seaman emphasizes the importance of cultivating environmental humility – recognizing our inherent connection to places and acknowledging their agency in shaping our lives. He also highlights the value of alternative approaches to understanding reality beyond analytical reductionism, which he argues have been historically undervalued in academia.

In response to questions about his motivation for this work, Seaman reveals that his lifelong quest stems from his desire to understand and accurately represent the real world around him. Despite encountering resistance within academia due to his unconventional approach, he remains committed to sharing these ideas, believing they are essential for fostering a more holistic understanding of our relationship with place.

Seaman concludes by recommending key readings on the topic, including works by Henry Bortoff ("The Wholeness of Nature"), Edward Relph ("Place and Placelessness"), Christopher Alexander ("A Pattern Language"), and himself ("Life Takes Place," "Phenomenological Perspectives on Place, Life Worlds, and Lived in Placement"). He also mentions the Environmental and Architectural Phenomenology journal as a valuable open-source resource.


### 20240923 - Powers of Focus with C. Thi Nguyen and Andrea Hiott Audio Only

In this conversation, philosopher C.T. Nguyen discusses games as an art form and their relation to love, agency, and life's gamification. Here are the key points:

1. Games and Art: Nguyen argues that games manipulate our agency by specifying goals and constraints, which makes them distinctive as an art form. Unlike other media like film or literature, where we might focus on narrative, dialogue, or character development, games uniquely offer a medium of agency.

2. Types of Games: Nguyen distinguishes between different types of games based on the level of agency they provide to players. Some games encourage intense focus and attention (like rock climbing or fly fishing), while others may not require as much verbal engagement (like board games or certain video games).

3. Gamification in Life: The conversation expands into how life has become increasingly gamified, with social media and other platforms setting pre-established goals and constraints for users, similar to game design. Nguyen raises concerns about the potential dangers of this gamification, particularly when users aren't aware they're participating in a structured system.

4. Trust and Vulnerability: Nguyen discusses trust as an essential mode of human interaction. He highlights that we constantly rely on trusting others, from the buildings we walk into to the professionals we depend upon for services. Games can be a way to practice trust in coordinated teamwork.

5. Artistic Experiences and Love: Nguyen posits love as an opposing force to games, emphasizing that love is non-disposable, non-fungible, and non-fluid—qualities absent from gameplay. However, he acknowledges the possibility of loving a game intensely, as one might deeply enjoy activities like rock climbing or writing.

6. The Power of Focus: Nguyen explores how games harness focus to create immersive experiences. By engaging in different games, we can learn to direct our attention toward various aspects of life, providing a broader perspective and a "refresh" for the mind. He also warns about the dangers of becoming overly habituated to quantified value systems within games or other digital platforms.

7. The Nature of Art: Throughout the conversation, Nguyen emphasizes that art is an open-ended experience—one in which we enter with an attitude of curiosity and willingness to engage with what the medium offers, rather than a predetermined agenda. This idea applies equally to games as it does to other forms of artistic expression like literature or cinema.

8. The Relationship Between Games and Life: Nguyen suggests that games can be tools for teaching valuable lessons about trust, focus, and vulnerability. However, he also cautions against the dangers of becoming too entangled in gamified systems without recognizing their influence on our behavior and perception of reality.

This engaging discussion explores the nuanced ways games function as an art form, intertwining with themes of love, agency, vulnerability, trust, focus, and life's increasing gamification. Nguyen offers a thoughtful perspective that both appreciates games' unique qualities and acknowledges their potential pitfalls within our modern world.

### 20240923 - Spiral Ladders of Love, Poetry and Reconciliation with Iain McGilchrist & Andrea Hiott Audio Podcast

In this conversation, Dr. Iain McGilchrist, a neuropsychiatrist and author of "The Master and His Emissary," discusses his ideas on the importance of asymmetry, the coincidence of opposites, and beyond dichotomy in understanding reality, human nature, and consciousness.

1. Asymmetry: McGilchrist emphasizes the significance of asymmetry in various contexts, including biology, physics, and human cognition. He argues that asymmetry is not merely a lack of symmetry but a fundamental principle that gives rise to complexity and richness. For instance, he points out that every living organism, from ancient creatures like the sea anemone to humans, exhibits asymmetry. In the human brain, this asymmetry manifests in distinct roles between the left and right hemispheres: the left hemisphere is more concerned with detail, analysis, and logic, while the right hemisphere deals with the bigger picture, context, and holistic understanding.

2. Coincidence of Opposites: McGilchrist advocates for recognizing that apparent opposites can coexist harmoniously within a unified whole. He illustrates this concept using examples from nature (such as whirlpools) and human consciousness, where resistance (opposition to flow) gives rise to new forms of beauty and complexity. This perspective is central to his argument against reducing complex phenomena to simplified binary oppositions.

3. Beyond Dichotomy: McGilchrist's work seeks to move beyond the limitations of dichotomous thinking that characterizes much of modern Western thought. He argues for embracing paradox and the interconnectedness of seemingly opposing concepts, allowing for a more nuanced understanding of reality. This is exemplified in his exploration of the relationship between the brain's hemispheres, which transcends simplistic left-brain/right-brain divisions.

4. Philosophical Implications: McGilchrist's ideas have significant implications for philosophy and our understanding of consciousness, reality, and human nature. He suggests that the left hemisphere's dominance in modern thought has led to a reductionist worldview that fails to capture the complexity and beauty of existence. By contrast, embracing asymmetry and the coincidence of opposites allows for a more holistic, nuanced appreciation of reality, including the interconnectedness of all things.

5. Literature and Art: McGilchrist's ideas are informed by literature, particularly the Romantics like William Blake, and he sees art as a means of exploring and expressing these complex concepts. He admires how poets and artists can convey profound truths through language that resists explicit definition, allowing for multiple layers of meaning to be simultaneously present.

6. Personal Journey: McGilchrist reflects on his own intellectual journey, which began with a love of literature and philosophy at an early age. He pursued these interests even after initially studying science in medical school, eventually discovering the mind-body relationship through neuroscience. His unique background allows him to approach complex topics from various angles, enriching his insights into the nature of consciousness and reality.

7. Balancing Attention: McGilchrist emphasizes the importance of cultivating different kinds of attention, particularly in an age dominated by left-hemisphere thinking (focused on detail, analysis, and utility). He encourages embracing right-hemisphere modes of attention, which involve a more holistic, contextual understanding of reality. This shift can foster empathy, connection with nature, and spiritual awareness.

8. Balancing Act: McGilchrist acknowledges the challenges of balancing left- and right-brain thinking in modern life, where competition, narcissism, and addiction to digital stimuli often dominate. He advocates for cultivating intuition, imagination, and a sense of interconnectedness with the world and others as essential components of a fulfilling human experience.

9. Poetry and Art: McGilchrist views poetry and art as powerful tools for exploring complex ideas and fostering a deeper appreciation of reality. He sees these forms as portals to alternative ways of experiencing the world, allowing us to transcend narrow perspectives and connect with universal truths.

10. Life's Purpose: McGilchrist reflects on the purpose of human life, suggesting that our primary function is not survival but responding to something greater than ourselves—values like goodness, beauty, and truth. By embracing these values and cultivating a holistic understanding of reality, humans can help bring more beauty, wisdom,

### 20240923 - What if we can model cognition？ And what is a model anyway？ Audio with Paul Kelly & Andrea Hiott

The debate between mechanistic and dynamical models centers around the question of what constitutes an explanatory model. The 3M principle, proposed by some mechanists, argues that a model must have at least one mapping to specific physical components (gears, levers, etc.) in the underlying mechanism to be considered explanatory. This principle excludes dynamical models, which do not make such posits, from being deemed explanatory.

However, there are several arguments challenging this strict interpretation of explanation:

1. Understanding as Explanation: Some philosophers argue that understanding something is a form of explanation. For instance, grasping why water pools on folded paper due to its shape constitutes an explanation, even if it doesn't meet the mechanistic standard set by 3M. This view implies that dynamical models, which help us understand complex systems, could be considered explanatory.

2. Dynamical Motifs in AI: In artificial neural networks, a dynamical motif has been proposed as an explanation for why trained networks perform better on related tasks than untrained ones. This explanation involves the network's ability to exploit or repurpose dynamical properties, such as attractors and prototypes, without requiring knowledge of specific components or interactions. While this explanation fails 3M, it seems implausible to deny its explanatory power.

3. Equilibrium Models: In economics and biology, equilibrium models successfully represent the dynamics of systems (e.g., predator-prey relationships or supply-demand interactions) by positing equilibrium points. These posits are similar to those found in dynamical systems theory, such as attractors and repellers. Extending 3M to these fields would exclude equilibrium models from being considered explanatory, which seems arbitrary and unjustified.

The debate ultimately raises questions about the necessity of choosing between mechanistic and dynamical models. While each approach has its strengths and weaknesses, it may be more practical and intellectually honest to employ various types of models depending on the specific research question and context, rather than insisting on a single, strict definition of explanation. This perspective allows for a more nuanced understanding of explanation across different scientific disciplines.


The conversation revolves around the philosophy of modeling, specifically focusing on climate science models, but extending to broader implications for various scientific disciplines and everyday life. The discussion centers on the idea that no single model is universally "true" or explanatory; instead, models should be evaluated based on their adequacy for specific purposes (adequacy for purpose account).

The participants discuss how climate science has converged on the understanding that individual models don't inherently possess truth value. Instead, they have a particular relationship with the target phenomena to varying degrees and in certain respects. This perspective aligns with Wendy Parker's work, who advocates for evaluating models based on their suitability for specific tasks (e.g., description, prediction, explanation, or understanding).

The conversation highlights the importance of recognizing different kinds of models and their purposes: describing phenomena, making predictions, explaining processes, and fostering understanding—each requiring distinct model types. In complex fields like climate science, where software simulations are involved, it's crucial to acknowledge that simplified mental representations may be necessary for comprehension, even if they don't capture the full complexity of the original phenomena.

The discussion also touches on the idea that scientific practices often involve model transfer—applying models from one domain to another. The challenge lies in determining whether such transferred models can still provide explanations or understanding rather than just descriptions. Dynamical systems theory might offer insights into how patterns and processes within different domains could be unified, allowing for explanatory power across disciplines.

The participants suggest that the debate between mechanistic and dynamic models is a case study illustrating broader philosophical assumptions about representation and explanation. They propose that focusing on patterns rather than parts might be beneficial in unifying various scientific approaches. The ultimate goal is to better understand how models can represent phenomena, explain processes, and foster genuine understanding across disciplines—with practical implications for fields like AI and criminal justice, where models inform decision-making with significant consequences.

Key points:
1. Models are not inherently "true" but have a particular relationship with the target phenomena, depending on their intended purpose (adequacy for purpose account).
2. Climate science models demonstrate this by being evaluated based on their suitability for description, prediction, explanation, or understanding.
3. Model transfer is common in scientific practice—applying models from one domain to another—and raises questions about maintaining explanatory power across disciplines.
4. Dynamical systems theory might help unify patterns and processes within different domains, enabling explanatory models across disciplines.
5. Focusing on patterns rather than parts could facilitate a more unified understanding of various scientific approaches.
6. The debate between mechanistic and dynamic models serves as an example of broader philosophical assumptions about representation and explanation in science.


### 20240923 - What is Intelligence？ Max Bennett on Brains, Evolution, and A.I. AUDIO Podcast

In this conversation, Max Bennett, author of "A Brief History of Intelligence: Evolution, AI, and the Five Breakthroughs That Made Our Brains," discusses his book's content and its implications for artificial intelligence (AI). Here's a detailed summary:

1. **Intelligence as a Pre-Brain Phenomenon**: Max emphasizes that intelligence is not exclusive to brains, but can be observed in simpler organisms like cells. The discrepancy between AI and human brain intelligence lies in understanding how the brain implements intelligence.

2. **Five Breakthroughs in Brain Evolution**:
   - **Steering (650 million years ago)**: The first bilaterians, like nematodes, moved around using a simple algorithm called "taxes navigation," which allowed them to find food without complex sensory organs. This marked the beginning of intelligence in animals with brains.
   - **Reinforcement (150 million years ago)**: The emergence of vertebrates brought about the cortex, enabling pattern recognition and arbitrary action sequences based on rewards or "valence."
   - **Simulation/Mentalization (150 million years ago)**: Mammals developed a unique ability to simulate and imagine alternative scenarios, leading to episodic memories, planning, and fine motor skills. Max discusses studies by David Redish that show rats modeling space and making choices based on simulated alternatives.
   - **Active Inference (150 million years ago)**: The neocortex models an animal's behavior, inferring intent, and driving it towards fulfilling the inferred goals—a process known as active inference. This concept explains phenomena like cognitive dissonance, where people overvalue experiences based on their internal self-model.
   - **Speaking/Language (200,000 to 150,000 years ago)**: Language emerged as a tool for sharing inner simulations and plans, enabling flexible problem-solving and cooperation between individuals. Max argues that language is not the foundation of intelligence but rather a tool built upon pre-existing mammalian intelligence.

3. **AI Implications**:
   - **Symbolic vs. Behavioral AI**: The debate between symbolic (rule-based) and behavioral (connectionist, like neural networks) approaches in AI mirrors historical discussions about nature-inspired vs. purely technological innovation. A balanced approach considers existence proofs from nature while acknowledging the limitations of direct copying.
   - **Continual Learning**: Current large language models face challenges with catastrophic forgetting—the inability to learn new information without losing previously acquired knowledge. Active inference and reimagining neural network learning algorithms may offer solutions, but much research is needed.

4. **Max Bennett's Work and Motivation**: Max's book presents an evolutionary perspective on brain intelligence, highlighting how various breakthroughs led to the unique cognitive abilities of mammals, including human language. His work emphasizes understanding underlying algorithms rather than specific implementation details or parts of the brain, aiming to reverse-engineer the brain and apply insights in AI development. The conversation hints at future discussions on love and philosophy beyond dichotomies, exploring interdisciplinary connections between science, philosophy, and human experiences.


### 20240925 - Is there any Positive Power in Polarity？ Rethinking opposition in design with Alastair Duckworth

In this conversation, two individuals discuss the concept of polarity, its role in design, and its implications for understanding life. The speaker, who has a background in religion and philosophy, is interested in how paradoxical or seemingly contradictory elements can create meaningful experiences and valuable designs. The guest, Alistair Andrea Hyatt, is an award-winning designer who sees polarity as essential for creating impactful brand campaigns and advertising.

1. **Polarity vs Dichotomy**: The speaker distinguishes between polarity and dichotomy. Polarity refers to the relationship of opposites with both distinction and union, while a dichotomy is a division or separation into two parts. For example, poles in a magnet are polarized because they have both separate identities (distinction) but also work together due to their underlying unity.

2. **Paradox in Design**: The guest explains how paradoxical elements in design capture attention and create value. He uses examples like the old Volkswagen Beetle ads, which turned a negative (being small) into a positive (efficiency, quirkiness). In these cases, the paradox lies within the subject itself, not added as an afterthought.

3. **Polarity in Branding and Advertising**: The guest believes that polarity is key to successful branding and advertising. By bringing together seemingly opposing ideas (e.g., freedom vs constraint, luxury vs simplicity), brands can create more compelling narratives and connect with audiences on a deeper level.

4. **Objectivity of Polarities**: The speaker argues that polarities have an objectivity to them, even though they may not be easily categorized as physical objects. They represent living principles that exist in everything and are present at different scales. For instance, freedom and constraint can be seen as dynamic processes rather than static concepts.

5. **Authenticity**: The conversation touches on the importance of authenticity in design and communication. The guest emphasizes that polarities should not be used merely to grab attention but rather to create meaningful connections with audiences by exploring genuine human experiences.

6. **Intention vs Authenticity**: While both are important, the speaker suggests that intention might be more crucial than authenticity when considering the purpose behind using polarities in design and communication. The intent should be to do good and create value for people, rather than simply pleasing a client or capturing attention without substance.

7. **Extremism**: Both speakers discuss extremism in different contexts. The guest advocates for positive forms of extremism, which involve embracing both ends of a spectrum and understanding their underlying unity. This way, they can constructively create each other instead of simply destroying one another.

8. **Holding Opposites Together**: Both speakers agree on the value of holding opposites together in the same space without collapsing them into a middle ground. This approach allows for more vibrant, engaging experiences and designs that resonate with audiences on a deeper level.

9. **Practice of Holding Paradoxes**: The guest views this practice as a cognitive exercise in design. By learning to hold seemingly contradictory ideas together, designers can create more interesting work and navigate subjective preferences more effectively.

10. **Communication and Understanding**: Both speakers emphasize the importance of understanding and communicating polarities accurately. Misusing terms like "polarization" or "extremism" without proper context can lead to misunderstandings or negative connotations.

In summary, this conversation explores how polarity, paradox, and the relationship between opposites can be valuable tools in design, branding, and understanding life. By embracing these concepts, individuals and organizations can create more meaningful connections with audiences while avoiding the pitfalls of superficial or disingenuous communication.

### 20241002 - Planta Sapiens and Rethinking Intelligence with Paco Calvo

Paco Calvo, a cognitive scientist and philosopher of biology, discusses plant intelligence and cognition with a focus on rethinking our understanding of these concepts. In his book "Planta Sapiens," Calvo argues that humans have deeply ingrained biases and assumptions about what constitutes intelligence and cognition, often based on human-centric views.

Calvo emphasizes the importance of considering plant behavior in its own right, without comparing it to human intelligence or using human-centric frameworks. He highlights that plants have their unique ways of perceiving, responding to, and interacting with their environment, which can be seen as forms of cognition and problem-solving.

One example Calvo provides is plant movement and growth. While humans typically associate intelligence with locomotion, plants employ alternative strategies such as growing towards or away from stimuli (e.g., tendrils coiling around supports). This growth-based movement can be viewed as a form of exploration and problem-solving, which challenges our conventional understanding of intelligence.

Calvo also discusses the historical context of this topic, referencing Charles Darwin's observations of plants in his bedroom while ill. Darwin noticed plants' intricate movements and behaviors without relying on time-lapse photography or other advanced tools, which underscores the value of attentive observation.

In terms of reaction to plant intelligence, Calvo acknowledges that some people find the concept fascinating while others are skeptical or even offended by it. This strong emotional response often stems from deeply ingrained assumptions about what constitutes intelligent behavior and life. Calvo argues that our prejudices against plant intelligence may run deeper than we realize, influenced not only by Western scientific reductionism but also by cultural biases and traditional knowledge systems.

Calvo suggests that rethinking cognition should start from a bottom-up perspective, focusing on shared regularities in the natural world rather than linear hierarchies. He emphasizes that intelligence is not confined to neural tissue or human-like forms but can emerge through various biological processes and interactions with the environment.

While the Dalai Lama acknowledges the possibility of AI sentience, he drew a line at attributing sentience to plants due to their sessile nature and lack of locomotion – a viewpoint Calvo considers limiting and rooted in human-centric biases.

Calvo concludes by emphasizing that rethinking intelligence requires questioning our fundamental assumptions, expanding our understanding of cognition beyond human experiences, and embracing a more inclusive and nuanced perspective on life's diverse forms of intelligence.

### 20241009 - The Synchrony of Fractals： Georg Northoff and Andrea Hiott discuss Spatiotemporal Neuroscience

Georg Northoff, a neuroscientist and philosopher, is known for his unique perspective on the relationship between mind, brain, and the world. He challenges traditional dualistic views of mind and body, advocating instead for a non-reductive neurophilosophy that combines science and philosophy.

Northoff's work revolves around the concept of subjectivity and the unique spatial temporal patterns of each individual. He argues that our mental states are deeply intertwined with our environment, shaping our perceptions, cognitions, emotions, and sense of self. This perspective is grounded in his research on patients with psychiatric disorders, which led him to question the standard mind-body dichotomy.

One of Northoff's key contributions is his theory of "world-brain dual-aspect monism," which posits that both the brain and the world exhibit aspects of subjectivity. This idea moves away from traditional dualistic views (mind vs. body) and reductive materialism (brain as the sole generator of mental states). Instead, it suggests that both the brain and the world have subjective properties, with the world's subjectivity manifesting in our conscious experiences of it.

Northoff's work also emphasizes the importance of considering cultural and geographical factors in understanding human cognition and consciousness. He argues that our spatial temporal patterns are influenced by our environment, which can lead to differences in mental states among individuals from various regions. This perspective challenges the idea that there is a universal, objective way of experiencing the world, highlighting instead the diversity and richness of human subjectivity.

To further explore these ideas, Northoff has turned to phenomenology, a philosophical tradition that focuses on subjective experience. By drawing on phenomenological insights, he aims to deepen our understanding of consciousness, selfhood, and the relationship between mind and world. This interdisciplinary approach allows him to challenge established assumptions in neuroscience and philosophy, offering a fresh perspective on age-old questions about the nature of reality and human experience.

In summary, Georg Northoff's work centers around challenging dualistic views of mind and body, advocating for a non-reductive neurophilosophy that integrates subjectivity into our understanding of both brain function and the external world. By emphasizing the importance of spatial temporal patterns, cultural influences, and phenomenological insights, Northoff offers a nuanced perspective on human consciousness, selfhood, and our relationship to the environment.

### 20241016 - Phenomenology, Cognitive Science, and Authenticity with Michael Wheeler

The conversation between Mirko Prokop and Michael Wheeler delves into phenomenology and its relationship with cognitive science, focusing particularly on Martin Heidegger's perspective. Phenomenology, traditionally, is concerned with first-person experience, aiming to accurately describe our experiences to understand the underlying conditions that shape them. This differs from the analytic tradition's approach, which often views introspection as unreliable due to potential biases in self-reporting.

In contrast, continental phenomenology, led by thinkers like Heidegger, Merleau-Ponty, and Husserl, also starts with first-person experience but extends its analysis beyond mere description. It seeks to understand the a priori conditions (transcendental structures) that determine why our experiences have their specific character. These conditions are historicized for Heidegger, meaning they can vary across cultures and historical periods, unlike Kant's timeless structures.

Heidegger's philosophy challenges Cartesian dualism, which separates mind (subject) from the world of objects. For Descartes, knowing the world involves forming representations within an essentially separate mind. In contrast, Heidegger argues that we are always already embedded in a meaningful world, and our relationship to it is fundamentally different.

Central to Heidegger's thought is his concept of "Dasein" (human existence or being) and the study of "equipment" (German: Zeug). Equipment refers to objects that are experienced as tools for human projects rather than isolated entities with properties. For example, a carpenter doesn't see their hammer as an object with mass, color, and material; instead, they experience it as equipment for carpentry.

Heidegger introduces the idea of "involvement networks" to describe how these tools are interconnected within our practical projects. When skillfully used, equipment becomes "transparent," meaning we don't consciously focus on it but rather on the tasks at hand. This transparency signifies a seamless integration between user and tool in a skilled, problem-free interaction.

When equipment malfunctions or breaks, users must deal with it as an independent object, entering the state of "unreadiness to hand." In this condition, the entire network of involvement becomes apparent, affecting not just the broken tool but also related aspects like career and self-perception.

A key aspect of Heidegger's thought is the "ready-to-hand" mode of engagement with equipment, which he considers more fundamental than detached, analytical modes (present-at-hand). However, Wheeler acknowledges that while this skilled interaction is primary for Heidegger, it might not be common in everyday life. The spectrum between ready-to-hand and present-at-hand includes the "unready-to-hand," where users are still engaged but face difficulties or gaps with their tools.

In summary, this conversation explores how phenomenology, particularly Heidegger's interpretation, offers a unique perspective on human cognition and our relationship with the world. By examining first-person experiences and the conditions shaping them, it challenges traditional dualistic views, highlighting instead the interconnectedness of humans and their tools within meaningful contexts.

### 20241022 - The Source of Consciousness is Feeling. With neuropsychologist Mark Solms.

The narrator, a neuroscientist, recounts his lifelong fascination with the brain and consciousness, sparked by a childhood trauma involving his older brother's severe head injury at age four. This event led to profound questions about the relationship between mind and body, mortality, and the nature of selfhood.

As a young boy, he noticed changes in his brother's behavior and cognitive abilities post-injury, realizing that the brain was connected to personality and identity. The incident instilled in him a sense of existential dread about mortality and the transient nature of life. Simultaneously, it ignited an intellectual curiosity about the mind-body problem and the mystery of how subjective experience arises from physical processes.

As he grew older, this fascination persisted despite societal pressures discouraging such inquiry. In university, he encountered behaviorism—a school of thought that emphasized studying observable behaviors rather than internal mental states—which further complicated his pursuit of understanding consciousness.

Despite this, the narrator continued to explore the subject, eventually specializing in neuropsychology and conducting groundbreaking research on dreams and their relation to brain damage. His findings revealed that certain frontal lobe injuries could result in complete cessation of dreaming, implicating a specific brainstem dopamine system—the mesocortical mesolimbic pathway—as crucial for dream generation.

This research brought him back to the fundamental nature of feeling as a core aspect of consciousness, challenging the prevailing cortex-centric models of mind and cognition. He argues that feeling is not merely a byproduct of sensory input but an intrinsic, affective component integral to all forms of consciousness.

The narrator's journey reflects a broader tension within scientific inquiry regarding the study of subjective experience. He contends that this subjectivity—this "spring" of human awareness—has been misunderstood and underestimated, leading to oversimplified models of consciousness rooted in external stimuli rather than internal feeling states. His work aims to rectify this by applying tools from statistical mechanics and the free energy principle to explain how feelings emerge as a means of navigating and maintaining homeostasis within biological systems, ultimately shaping our understanding of what it means to be conscious.
