### 20241027 - Rethinking Systems with Love： The Economics of Tomorrow with Paddy LeFlufy

In this discussion, Patty Lafloufee talks about her book "Building Tomorrow" and her perspective on love as a motivating force for positive change. She defines love as wanting what's best for the object of one's affection, which in her case is humanity. This definition led her to view her writing and life work as driven by her love for humanity.

Patty explains that her travels and experiences meeting diverse cultures significantly influenced this perspective. She compares this process to falling in love with humanity, rather than being an inherent inclination she always had. This realization sparked her dedication to redesigning society for the betterment of all humans.

The book itself focuses on six organizational technologies that can transform the current economic system and address planetary problems like climate change. These technologies include:

1. The Doughnut Model: This replaces economic growth as a goal with meeting human needs within planetary boundaries, ensuring everyone has access to life's basics while staying within environmental limits.
2. Circular Economy: A framework that aims to eliminate waste by cycling materials endlessly through the economy instead of viewing them as disposable.
3. Future Guardian Model (FGM): This redefines businesses, shifting their legal duty from profit maximization to fulfilling a stated purpose while balancing and protecting interests of stakeholder groups like employees, customers, partners, investors, community, and environment.
4. B Corps: A certification for companies that balance profit with positive social and environmental impact.
5. Regenerative Organizations: Networks connecting local organizations globally under shared ethos and rules, sharing resources and collaborating on common goals.
6. Sovereign Currency: An alternative monetary system where money is created by governments rather than private banks, potentially reducing the need for continuous growth to pay back loans with interest.

Throughout the conversation, Patty emphasizes the importance of understanding and implementing these organizational technologies incrementally, starting with small steps that can lead to a holistic new economic system. She highlights various examples of people, communities, and businesses already putting these ideas into practice and encourages readers to do the same in their own lives.

The conversation also delves into the concept of love as a driving force for positive change. Patty relates her motivation for writing the book to her love for humanity, viewing it as an intimate relationship where she aims to improve the quality of life for all humans. She suggests that this approach involves balancing idealism with practical analysis and recognizing the need for continuous improvement and adaptation in addressing global challenges.

In summary, Patty Lafloufee's book "Building Tomorrow" presents six organizational technologies aimed at transforming our current economic system to better address environmental issues and improve quality of life for all humans. These technologies are grounded in her personal definition of love as an intrinsic motivation to act on behalf of humanity, and she encourages readers to engage with these ideas by implementing them in their own lives and communities. The conversation highlights the importance of balancing idealism with practical analysis and continuous improvement when striving for a more loving, sustainable world.


### 20241030 - #36： Feeling into the Source of Self with neuropsychologist Mark Solms

Mark Soames, a South African psychoanalyst and neuropsychologist, discusses his journey into understanding consciousness and the role of feelings in cognition. His interest was sparked at age four when his older brother suffered a severe brain injury after falling from a roof, leading to a fractured skull and brain hemorrhage. This event profoundly affected Mark, raising questions about the nature of mind and body, consciousness, and the self.

Following this incident, Mark became preoccupied with understanding what constitutes sentient being or consciousness, driven by a desire to find meaning beyond the inevitability of death. He felt guilty when excelling at school while his brother struggled academically due to the injury, leading him to compromise by pursuing a career that would allow him to help others like his brother.

At university, Mark discovered that studying the mind-body relationship was not encouraged in scientific circles, as the focus was on objectivity and excluding subjective experiences. He studied neuropsychology but found it disappointing due to the separation of mind and body during research. This led him to investigate dreaming as a way to study subjective mental phenomena while avoiding the controversy surrounding dream interpretation.

In his doctoral research, Mark made significant discoveries related to the brain mechanisms of sleep and wakefulness, specifically focusing on REM sleep and dreams. He noticed that certain brainstem damage led to a complete loss of dreaming in patients, indicating that dreams originated from specific structures within the brain. This research ultimately connected feelings and emotions with the brain's reward system or "seeking" system.

Mark's findings challenged the prevailing cortical model of consciousness by demonstrating that the essence of consciousness is feeling, not just perception. Feeling arises from the innermost core of the brainstem and sustains wakefulness. The reticular activating system in the brainstem plays a crucial role in generating raw feelings and is prerequisite for any form of consciousness.

In his book, Mark Soames proposes that selfhood is not reflective but rather based on being – feeling. Consciousness emerges from the interaction between the body's needs and its environment through homeostasis or minimizing free energy. Feelings serve as a value system guiding our actions to maintain survival and well-being, with bad feelings indicating movement away from preferred states and good feelings signaling progress in the right direction.

Mark argues that understanding consciousness requires acknowledging the causal power of feelings within the framework of statistical mechanics and free energy principles. He aims to bridge the gap between subjective and objective perspectives, seeking an underlying ontological explanation for the interplay between mind and body using mathematical models.

In summary, Mark Soames' journey into understanding consciousness began with a traumatic childhood event that sparked his curiosity about the nature of mind, body, and sentience. Through groundbreaking research on dreaming and brain mechanisms, he uncovered the crucial role feelings play in shaping consciousness and cognition. By applying statistical mechanics principles like free energy, Soames seeks to explain subjective experiences within an objective scientific framework, ultimately bridging the divide between mind and body.


### 20241103 - Entanglement with Alva Noë： Striving with Problems and the Balm of Philosophy

The conversation between the host and philosopher Alva Noe revolves around themes such as human perception, self-organization, self-awareness, and work/life balance. Here's a detailed summary and explanation of key points discussed:

1. Organization and Self-Awareness:
   - Noe discusses his lifelong fascination with human habits and regularity, which can be traced back to childhood when he began keeping a diary at age 10 or 11. This interest in documenting life experiences reflects an awareness of oneself as a process, rather than a static entity.
   - Noe emphasizes that we are constantly writing ourselves through our actions and decisions, which can be seen as a form of self-organization and self-creation.

2. Representation:
   - The host and Noe explore the concept of representation, moving beyond the traditional understanding of internal representations in the mind to encompass language, tools, and other means by which we create and share meaning with others.
   - Noe acknowledges that the term "representation" can be problematic due to its association with objectifying or simplifying complex processes, but he finds it necessary for understanding how humans engage with their world.

3. Work as a Fundamental Mode of Being:
   - Noe describes work (including intellectual and creative pursuits) as a core aspect of human existence, emphasizing that striving towards self-understanding and relating to others is central to what it means to be alive.
   - He draws a connection between this idea of work and love, positing that both involve an ongoing effort to understand ourselves and our relationships with the world around us.

4. Presence and Being Present:
   - Noe discusses the idea that being present (or mindful) may not be as effortless or graceful as it's often portrayed; rather, it requires work and practice. He likens this to the concept of presence in dance, where one must actively engage with their movements to truly be there.

5. Love as a Life Process:
   - Noe views love as an ongoing, transformative process that shapes us and reshapes us throughout our lives. He emphasizes the difficulty of maintaining intimacy and deep connection with others as fundamental aspects of human existence.

6. Philosophy vs. Science:
   - Noe argues for the distinct nature of philosophy and science, stating that while both can engage with similar questions, their methodologies and goals differ significantly. Unlike science, philosophy does not rely on fixed standards or experimental measurements to validate its claims.
   - He highlights how art and philosophy share the ability to provoke introspection and challenge our assumptions about the world, making them essential components of human inquiry that cannot be reduced to scientific methodologies.

7. The Aesthetic Problem of the Self:
   - Noe posits that humans are "aesthetic problems" to themselves because we cannot fully see or understand ourselves as fixed entities; instead, our self-concept is always richly indeterminate and open to interpretation. This perspective informs his skepticism towards neuroscience's attempts to reduce complex human experiences (like consciousness) to measurable neural substrates.

In summary, this conversation with Alva Noe explores the interconnected themes of self-awareness, representation, work/life balance, and love through a philosophical lens that emphasizes process, relationality, and the importance of ongoing engagement with the world around us. The discussion underscores Noe's commitment to understanding human life as an ever-evolving, dynamic endeavor shaped by our efforts to make sense of ourselves and others through various modes of representation and expression.


The conversation between the interviewer and philosopher Ian Chambers-Stodden revolves around several themes, primarily focusing on perception, embodiment, and the nature of seeing as it relates to broader human experiences. Here's a detailed summary and explanation of key points:

1. Perception as embodied process: Both interviewer and Chambers-Stodden agree that perception is not confined to visual modality or even the brain itself, but extends to the whole body and its interactions with the environment. The act of seeing can be likened to hugging someone or climbing a mountain – a more immersive experience than a passive process of light hitting the retina and creating an internal picture.

2. Challenging the notion of vision: Chambers-Stodden argues that our traditional understanding of vision, based on optical principles, is incomplete or even incorrect. He suggests that we should rethink what vision means by examining historical examples like Renaissance perspective paintings and their limitations in representing reality.

3. Disability and perception: The interviewer raises the issue of how people who are blind or visually impaired perceive the world. Chambers-Stodden emphasizes that such individuals do not lack vision but see differently, experiencing a rich world full of shapes, sizes, spatial extents, and potentially colors distinct from what we typically associate with sight.

4. Style as a lens for understanding human experience: Chambers-Stodden posits that humans are creatures of style rather than mere habits. This means our unique ways of doing things – whether speaking, walking, dancing, writing, or even listening to music at concerts – shape our identities and create spaces for social interaction and self-expression.

5. Critiquing the visual modality: Chambers-Stodden critiques the idea that vision operates through separate modalities (touch, sight, hearing, etc.). Instead, he suggests these categories are socially constructed and that our experiences should be understood as an indivisible whole.

6. Art as a means to challenge perception: The interviewer connects this discussion to Chambers-Stodden's earlier work "Action and Perception," highlighting how artists like Cézanne defy traditional notions of visual representation, demonstrating that seeing is more about encountering the world anew rather than reproducing established views.

7. Expanding the scope of perception: The conversation touches upon the possibility of experiencing the world beyond conventional vision through art, performance, and philosophical exploration. Chambers-Stodden aims to challenge established ideas about sight and create a more expansive understanding of human perceptual capacities.

8. Encouraging paradox and rethinking dichotomies: Both participants agree on the value of embracing contradictions and holding multiple perspectives simultaneously, rather than forcing choices between opposing viewpoints. This approach fosters a richer understanding of human experience by acknowledging complexities and nuances in perception, identity, reason, and more.

9. Philosophy as a liberating force: Chambers-Stodden expresses concern over the devaluation of philosophy, art, and acting in contemporary society, emphasizing their importance in fostering critical thinking and questioning dominant paradigms. He advocates for an understanding that encompasses various modes of reasoning, including aesthetic, ethical, and political dimensions, rather than reducing all to scientific or logical frameworks alone.

10. Personal narrative: Throughout the conversation, Chambers-Stodden shares his personal experiences with philosophy and art, explaining how reading certain texts at a young age sparked an enduring passion for exploring human identity, perception, and existence through these lenses. This emphasis on individual journeys underscores his belief in the transformative power of philosophical inquiry and creative expression.


### 20241105 - Discussing Representation in Neuroscience with Lynn Nadel & Andrea Hiott

In this conversation, two individuals discuss the topic of representation in cognitive neuroscience, focusing on how the brain models or represents the external world to guide behavior. They explore various historical perspectives and current debates surrounding this concept.

1. Historical context: The speakers mention that students today often lack knowledge about the history of research in fields like the hippocampus, emphasizing the importance of understanding the evolution of ideas over time. One speaker recalls a class they taught 10 years ago, noting that many of the core concepts haven't changed significantly.

2. Representation and the brain: The speakers delve into the mystery of how the physical brain gives rise to subjective mental experiences, such as self-awareness and thoughts that don't feel physical. They discuss the mind-body problem and the challenge of reconciling our bodily experience with cognitive representations.

3. The role of language: While language is a powerful tool for describing thoughts, it often falls short in capturing their true nature. The speakers acknowledge that thoughts are not confined to words or a single lingua franca, implying a more complex form of representation in the brain.

4. Types of reality: Cognitive neuroscience aims to understand different aspects of reality, including current experiences (thoughts and perceptions), past memories, and future predictions. The challenge lies in deciphering how the brain translates sensory inputs into meaningful representations that guide behavior and support survival.

5. Topographic maps: The conversation highlights the concept of topographic maps in the brain, which organize information spatially, with nearby neurons processing related stimuli. Examples include the visual cortex's retinotopic organization and somatosensory cortex's body-part representation. These maps provide computational advantages but do not fully address the problem of representation.

6. Embodied cognition: The speakers touch on embodied cognition theory, which posits that the mind and body are tightly coupled, and cognitive processes arise from interactions between the organism and its environment rather than being confined to a single decoder within the brain.

7. Grid cells and population coding: Recent research suggests that grid cell populations in the hippocampus may represent spatial information using a high-dimensional code, with excitatory neurons capturing specific features of sensory input and inhibitory neurons reflecting context or modulation. These findings imply prewired structures in the brain that constrain representation before specific experiences shape their function.

8. Inside-out vs. outside-in approaches: The speakers discuss the ongoing debate between inside-out (brain generates representations independently of external factors) and outside-in (representations emerge from interactions with the environment) perspectives. They propose a hybrid approach that acknowledges both aspects, recognizing the brain's inherent structure and its responsiveness to experience.

9. Controversy surrounding Eichenbaum's relational memory theory: The conversation critiques Eichenbaum's notion of the hippocampus as a unique relational device, arguing that this idea is vacuous because every part of the brain engages in associative processing and extracting statistical regularities from input patterns.

10. Future research directions: The speakers emphasize the need for continued exploration into how the brain's structure and interactions with the environment give rise to meaningful representations that support cognition, behavior, and self-awareness. They suggest that a middle ground between inside-out and outside-in perspectives may hold the key to understanding this complex process.

In summary, this conversation revolves around the representation problem in cognitive neuroscience, discussing historical contexts, various theories, and current debates. The speakers explore the role of topographic maps, embodied cognition, grid cells, and population coding while critiquing specific theories like Eichenbaum's relational memory hypothesis. They propose a hybrid approach that acknowledges both prewired brain structures and responsiveness to environmental factors in generating meaningful representations.


### 20241107 - #37： Our Entanglement： Striving with problems (in a good way) with philosopher Alva Noë

In this conversation, philosopher Alva Noë discusses various themes with his interviewer, including organization, self-awareness, love, and the nature of perception. Here's a detailed summary and explanation of some key points:

1. **Organization**: Noë emphasizes that when he uses the term "organization," he's not referring to business or corporate structures but rather the way we habitually organize ourselves in the world. He sees habits as a form of integration and organization, which can be oppressive if one is stuck or ignorant about them.

2. **Self-awareness**: Noë believes that achieving self-awareness is hard work; it's not an automatic process but requires constant effort to become aware of oneself in relation to others and the world. He argues that we are always problems to ourselves, as we can't see ourselves clearly and must strive to understand who we are and what we're doing.

3. **Love**: Love, for Noë, is a life process that constantly reshapes us. It's a challenging endeavor to hold another person in view and maintain intimacy, as it requires being present and attentive—something he considers difficult and requiring effort.

4. **Perception**: In his book "Action and Perception," Noë challenges the notion that perception happens within the brain, arguing instead that perception is an action involving the whole body. He believes seeing is more like hugging someone or climbing a mountain, emphasizing that our bodies are integral to perception.

5. **Art and Philosophy**: Noë sees art and philosophy as entangled, with each offering opportunities for reorganization of ourselves and our understanding of the world. Artworks challenge us to see differently, while philosophy invites us to question our assumptions and realize that many things we take for granted aren't stable.

6. **Neuroscience**: Noë is skeptical of neuroscience's attempts to "sciencify" philosophy or art, arguing that human experience is indeterminate and richly complex, making it challenging to study using traditional scientific methods. He advocates for a more nuanced approach that acknowledges the limitations of our current understanding.

7. **Performance**: Noë introduces the concept of performance as a way to understand our constant becoming in social spaces. Performance implies doing something in the presence of others, allowing us to connect and reorganize ourselves while taking risks safely. He sees this idea linked to love, where a child can play freely in the presence of their mother without knowing she's there.

8. **Paradox**: Noë emphasizes the importance of holding paradoxes rather than trying to choose between opposing ideas. This approach allows us to see the richness of various possibilities and better understand complex phenomena like consciousness, ethics, politics, and relationships.

9. **Reason**: Noë argues for a new conception of reason that acknowledges its disputable nature in many domains of human life. He suggests that reason isn't about choosing between emotions or feelings but involves recognizing the intersubjective significance of our experiences and values.

Throughout the conversation, Noë stresses the importance of being present, paying attention, and engaging with the world to truly understand ourselves and others better. He encourages us to embrace complexity and paradox rather than seeking simplistic answers or reductionist explanations.

### 20241112 - Could individual consciousness be a dissociation of the source？ with Bernardo Kastrup

The interviewee, Bernardo Kastrup, discusses his philosophical journey from physicalism to analytic idealism. Initially working at CERN, he attempted to create a conscious computer but realized that physicalism couldn't account for consciousness, leading him to question his materialist worldview.

Kastrup's interest in Jung began at age 14 when he encountered the I Ching and Jung's preface, which discussed synchronicity as an alternative to causality. This sparked his curiosity about hidden assumptions and led him to examine physicalism critically. He found that physicalism was riddled with unexamined assumptions and empirically untenable.

Jung's concept of synchronicity provided a rationalist account for oracles, which Kastrup initially dismissed as ridiculous. Jung's approach demonstrated that seemingly illogical ideas could be given substance through empirical verification, teaching Kastrup to scrutinize his own assumptions.

Kastrup eventually abandoned physicalism and adopted analytic idealism, which posits that everything is mental or experiential. However, not all mental states belong to personal experience; the external world consists of dissociated mental states that we perceive as matter. Under analytic idealism, our perceptions are like a dashboard displaying information about the world, which is itself composed of experiential states.

Kastrup emphasizes the importance of flexibility in language and conceptual models, acknowledging that different ways of expressing ideas can help people discern underlying concepts. He also discusses the nature of dissociation, explaining that it is a cognitive phenomenon rather than a fundamental separation between individuals and the world.

Living analytic idealism has significantly impacted Kastrup's life, heightening his empathy and forcing him to develop coping mechanisms for dealing with intense emotional experiences. It has also reintroduced the fear of death into his consciousness, as he now recognizes that his subjectivity will continue beyond physical existence.

In summary, Kastrup's philosophical evolution from physicalism to analytic idealism was driven by his realization that physicalism couldn't account for consciousness. Inspired by Jung and the concept of synchronicity, he developed a critical approach to assumptions and adopted an idealist worldview. This shift has profoundly influenced his understanding of reality, empathy, and mortality.

### 20241119 - Holding paradox： Can we enter a new way of thinking？

Andrea Hyatt presents a compelling philosophical perspective that encourages moving beyond traditional binary thinking (dichotomies or opposing polarities) to navigate the complex challenges of our modern world, both globally and individually. She argues that this outdated way of thinking doesn't suffice in addressing today's multifaceted issues, and it often leaves individuals feeling lost or misunderstood due to its rigid structure.

Binary thinking, while useful historically, imposes a false dichotomy suggesting that every choice has an opposite and equally valid counterpart – a 'right' and 'wrong', 'good' or 'bad'. This oversimplification doesn't capture the nuanced reality of life's complexities. Hyatt contends that this mindset can lead to polarizing narratives, causing divisions and fostering an "us vs them" mentality in our personal lives, societies, and even scientific/philosophical discourse.

To counteract this, she proposes the adoption of paradoxical thinking: embracing the coexistence of seemingly opposing ideas within a broader context. This approach encourages us to hold multiple perspectives simultaneously rather than viewing them as mutually exclusive. By doing so, we can explore a more comprehensive understanding of reality, one that acknowledges the existence of truths, values, and consequences without forcing them into binary categories.

Hyatt's philosophy emphasizes the importance of shifting our collective mindset from oppositional thinking to a space where diverse viewpoints are recognized as valid and worthy of exploration. This entails:

1. Recognizing that not all aspects of life fit neatly into binary categories, such as 'true' vs 'false', or 'helpful' vs 'harmful'. These concepts exist on a spectrum rather than being absolute opposites.

2. Embracing the complexity and interconnectedness of reality by acknowledging that various factors can contribute to an issue without negating others. This understanding fosters more holistic problem-solving approaches.

3. Encouraging open dialogue and debate, where people discuss differing viewpoints with the aim of expanding their mutual understanding rather than winning an argument or forcing conformity to a particular narrative.

4. Cultivating self-awareness and humility, acknowledging that our personal perspectives are not absolute truths but rather subjective interpretations of reality.

5. Nurturing empathy and compassion in interactions with others, recognizing shared humanity beyond ideological differences.

In essence, Hyatt's philosophy is about evolving our collective thinking to embrace the paradoxical nature of life. By moving away from rigid binaries and towards a more nuanced understanding of reality, we can foster deeper connections with ourselves, others, and the world at large. This shift in perspective, while challenging and uncertain, holds immense potential for personal growth and societal transformation. Ultimately, it's about recognizing that love, as a concept encompassing care, respect, and understanding, requires us to transcend limiting dichotomies and open ourselves up to a broader, more inclusive way of experiencing life.


### 20241121 - #39： The Real World and its Many Models with philosopher Michela Massimi

Michaela Massimi, a philosopher of science, discusses her work on Perspectival Realism and its connection to philosophy, physics, and the plurality of models. She grew up in Italy, where philosophy was compulsory in high school, sparking her interest in both philosophy and physics from a young age. Massimi's passion for understanding the big questions of physics led her to study the Bohr-Einstein debate on physical reality during her Laurea dissertation.

Massimi's career took her through various stages, including encountering Kantian philosophy in her postdoc and PhD studies. She struggled with the dichotomy between realism and anti-realism, particularly when considering pluralism in science. This tension led her to explore the idea of situated knowledge from feminist epistemology as a way to reconcile realism and pluralism.

In Perspectival Realism, Massimi proposes an alternative view of models and reality. She argues that models are inferential blueprints used by communities to explore and make reliable inferences about phenomena without needing to pinpoint essential properties or one-to-one mappings with the target system. The book's central concepts include stable events (basic realist commitments), modally robust phenomena, and natural kinds with a human face (open-ended groupings of inferred phenomena).

Massimi's work challenges traditional views on essentialism in science, instead emphasizing that our knowledge is open to revision as we uncover new phenomena. This perspective encourages the recognition of diverse ways of knowing and access to scientific knowledge for different communities. She believes this shift can lead to a more inclusive understanding of science and how its benefits are shared.

Throughout her career, Massimi has been influenced by various writers such as Jorge Luis Borges and Italo Calvino. Their works, particularly Calvino's Invisible Cities, helped shape her ideas on perspective, plurality, and the construction of reality through human imagination and experiences.

Massimi's personal background also plays a significant role in her work, as she draws from her family's local knowledge and her own experiences to counter the notion that scientific knowledge is solely the domain of well-educated experts. This biographical element highlights the importance of community, both local and academic, in shaping her philosophical views.

### 20241121 - Holding Paradox： Beyond Dichotomies #holdingparadox

Andrea Hyatt is introducing a philosophical concept that challenges traditional binary thinking—the idea that opposites are mutually exclusive and exhaustive. This binary way of thinking, while useful in many contexts, is increasingly perceived as insufficient for addressing complex global issues and personal dilemmas. 

Hyatt argues that real-world phenomena like truth, goodness, and morality aren't strictly binaries—they're nuanced and multifaceted. This realization, she suggests, has led to a sense of disorientation for many individuals who are struggling with rigid definitions and the pressure to choose definitive 'right' or 'wrong' paths in life.

To counter this, Hyatt proposes Paradoxical Thinking – a method that embraces contradictions rather than resolving them into opposites. This involves acknowledging the coexistence of seemingly opposite concepts within a broader context. The goal is not to negate the reality of these binaries but to see them as part of a larger, more complex whole.

This new way of thinking isn't about dismissing the existence of harmful actions or valid perspectives; it's about recognizing that our understanding of 'truth' and 'goodness' is more nuanced than simple dichotomies allow. 

Hyatt further explains this by referencing various fields: science, philosophy, psychology, and personal life. She believes that many are seeking similar shifts in their approach to understanding themselves, their relationships, societal structures, and the world at large. This new perspective could lead to a richer, more sensory-engaged existence with profound implications for our sense of self and interconnectedness.

She encourages dialogue and collective exploration of this concept, emphasizing that it's a journey—not a destination. It involves navigating the discomfort and uncertainty inherent in abandoning familiar binary frameworks. 

The ultimate aim is to foster a shared understanding that allows for the coexistence of contradictory ideas within a wider context, thereby enriching our collective comprehension of reality. This philosophy aligns with the concept of love, as traditionally understood in its broadest sense—an expansive, inclusive, and non-oppositional state of being.

In essence, Andrea Hyatt's proposal is about embracing complexity, fostering dialogue, and collectively reimagining how we understand ourselves and the world through a lens that transcends simplistic binaries.


### 20241123 - Rethinking Models of Reality with philosopher Michela Massimi and Andrea Hiott

Michaela Goodman, the author of "Perspectival Realism," discusses her journey into philosophy and how she found her community within the field. Born in Italy, she encountered philosophy early on in high school and was immediately captivated by the subject, particularly the ideas of Plato and Kant. Despite her passion for physics, she chose to pursue a degree in philosophy due to her profound interest in its big questions.

Goodman's academic path was marked by a struggle between science and philosophy, as the Italian education system did not allow for combining the two subjects. However, she found ways to subvert this limitation by auditing physics classes and delving into scientific literature during her free time. This love for both fields eventually led her to focus on the philosophy of physics and history of science during her PhD.

Her book "Perspectival Realism" presents a unique approach to understanding reality, challenging traditional realist vs. anti-realist debates in the philosophy of science. Goodman argues that models, or "inferential blueprints," serve as tools for communities to make reliable inferences about reality through an exploratory process, without needing to attribute essential properties to target systems.

Key concepts in her work include:

1. Stable events: These are phenomena that have remained consistent across various models and are considered inherent aspects of nature, such as the repulsion of electric charges by magnetic fields. They serve as constraints for possible inferences made through modeling practices.

2. Moderately robust phenomena: Goodman posits these as complex natural occurrences, like global warming or the stability of specific isotopes, that have been repeatedly confirmed using multiple models and techniques. These represent the intersection points where different paths in the "garden" of modeling practices meet.

3. Natural kinds with a human face: Goodman rejects essentialism, arguing that natural kinds are simply concepts used for grouping phenomena based on shared characteristics. They're historically identified and can be revised as new phenomena emerge. This view allows for the inclusion of local knowledge, experiences, and know-how in scientific practices.

By emphasizing reliability within perspectival modeling, Goodman seeks to create a space where situated practices of various communities are recognized and valued. This approach challenges traditional notions of authority in science, paving the way for more inclusive and diverse participation in knowledge production and benefit sharing.

Goodman's personal life experiences have significantly influenced her philosophical work. She draws parallels between modeling practices in science and literary techniques like those found in Italo Calvino's "Invisible Cities." Her writing process during the COVID-19 pandemic further underscores the interconnectedness of personal life, academic pursuits, and philosophical exploration.

Overall, Goodman's work offers a fresh perspective on realism in science, emphasizing the importance of diverse communities and situated practices while challenging essentialist views of natural kinds. By integrating her own experiences with meticulous research and engaging narratives, she encourages readers to reconsider how we approach reality, knowledge, and participation in scientific endeavors.

### 20241219 - Environment is Not in the Head： Why everyone is talking about 'affordances' with Harry Heft & Andrea

The conversation between the host and Harry Heft revolves around the concept of affordances, as introduced by James J. Gibson in ecological psychology. Affordances are relational properties of the environment defined relative to an active organism, representing opportunities for behavior.

Harry Heft explains his intellectual journey, which began with environmental psychology during the time of Earth Day activism. He was initially interested in understanding how environments could be made optimal for child development. However, he became frustrated with the dominant approaches within environmental psychology at the time: defining environments physically and subjectively experiencing them. This led him to James Gibson's work, which provided a direct theory of perception, emphasizing that we experience the environment directly through affordances rather than indirectly via mental representations.

Heft describes Gibson's notion of a medium for vision, where reflected light in our surroundings carries information about objects and their properties, allowing us to perceive them directly without mental intermediaries. He highlights the importance of understanding affordances in ecological contexts, such as designing spaces that accommodate human capabilities (e.g., seats or ledges of appropriate height).

The conversation also delves into the historical roots of Gibson's ideas, which can be traced back to William James' radical empiricism—the rejection of dualistic epistemologies and the belief that immediate experience points both to the experiencer and the experienced object. Heft argues for viewing perception and action as a continuous process rather than distinct categories, challenging traditional philosophical and scientific frameworks.

The interviewees discuss the misuse of affordances in fields like computer design and interaction design, which has diluted its original meaning derived from Gibson's ecological psychology. Heft emphasizes the importance of maintaining a consistent definition for affordances within specific contexts to preserve their scientific value.

Finally, they touch on the topic of navigation and wayfinding in relation to affordances. Instead of relying on cognitive maps, the hosts propose that our perceptual experience and active memory allow us to navigate the world dynamically over time without needing an internal mental representation. They stress the significance of being careful with terminology and avoiding metaphorical leaps between different domains (e.g., physical vs. thought spaces). Ultimately, they advocate for a more nuanced understanding of memory as active engagement with learned skills rather than conscious recall.


The conversation revolves around the evolution of psychologist James J. Gibson's ideas about perception, specifically his transition from "The Perception of the Visual World" (1950) to "The Senses Considered as Perceptual Systems" (1966).

In his early book, Gibson primarily focused on visual perception as a static process tied to the retinal image. His later work, however, shifted towards an active, dynamic view of perception, considering individuals moving in their environment. This change was significant because it moved away from viewing perception as a passive reception of sensory information and instead emphasized the role of the active organism in shaping perceptual experiences.

The psychological community initially reacted negatively to Gibson's second book, dismissing it as quirky or even erratic due to its departure from his earlier work. However, over time, this later body of work began to be recognized for its profound insights. Mainstream psychologists continued to cite and build upon Gibson's initial ideas from the 1950 book, even as he moved away from them in subsequent publications.

The conversation also touches on the parallel experience of Ulrich Neisser, another prominent psychologist who initially subscribed to information processing theory but later embraced a more ecological approach after encountering Gibson's work. Both Gibson and Neisser demonstrated courage in revising their ideas, despite the potential backlash from their respective communities.

The discussion then shifts to Harry Heft's own journey in understanding how to make environments better for children, rooted in his undergraduate studies during Earth Day (1970). The early formative years of childhood and brain plasticity were central to his interests, with the goal of changing children's environments to support their development. Heft emphasizes the importance of teaching and raising awareness about environmental changes as acts of love and commitment to making a positive impact on future generations.

The conversation concludes by acknowledging the significance of these ideas in shaping our understanding of perception, affordances, and our relationship with the environment over time. Both Gibson's evolutionary thought and Heft's dedication to teaching and environmental awareness demonstrate the power of re-evaluating established ideas and the enduring impact of acts of love in academia and beyond.


### 20241220 - Why everyone is talking about 'affordances' with Harry Heft and Andrea Hiott： Driving Gibson

Harry Heft's exploration of affordances and ecological psychology, particularly in relation to the work of James J. Gibson, revolves around the idea of a relational approach to perception and action that transcends dualistic thinking. Here are some key points from the discussion:

1. **Relational Thinking vs Dualism**: Heft argues against traditional dualistic perspectives in psychology, which often view perception as either subjective (in the mind) or objective (in the world). Instead, he advocates for a relational approach that considers both aspects simultaneously.

2. **Affordances**: These are properties of the environment defined relative to an active organism. They represent opportunities for action and are not inherent to objects themselves but rather emerge from the interaction between the object and the perceiver's capabilities. For example, a cup affords drinking to humans but not to other animals due to differences in manual dexterity.

3. **Ecological Psychology**: Unlike environmental psychology, which can encompass various approaches, ecological psychology is a theoretical framework developed by Gibson that emphasizes direct perception—perceiving the world without intermediary mental representations. This perspective posits that the environment has intrinsic structure that organisms can directly detect and respond to.

4. **Navigating and Wayfinding**: Heft's work on navigation and wayfinding underscores the dynamic nature of perception and cognition as continuous processes rather than separate, discrete events. Navigation is not just about finding a route but also involves actively exploring and understanding the environment through movement.

5. **Cognitive Maps**: While rejecting the notion of cognitive maps as internal representations within the mind, Heft acknowledges that mental activities like planning routes or visualizing environments can be thought of as forms of cognitive mapping. These are distinct from the physical act of navigating and should be clearly distinguished to avoid conflation.

6. **Historical Context**: Heft's fascination with affordances and ecological psychology began during his graduate studies when he was introduced to Gibson's work by Eleanor Gibson, who asked him to drive her husband to teach a course. This practical engagement allowed Heft to delve deeply into Gibson's ideas about perception and action, particularly concerning navigation and wayfinding.

7. **Critique of Traditional Psychology**: The initial reception of Gibson's later works, such as "The Ecological Approach to Visual Perception," was mixed within the psychological community due to its departure from earlier theories rooted in vision psychology and information processing. However, over time, many recognized its significance in advancing ecological psychology.

8. **Influence of William James**: Heft traces a philosophical lineage from William James's radical empiricism to Gibson's ecological psychology. James's rejection of dualistic epistemologies and his emphasis on immediate experience pointing towards both the subject and object align with Gibson's ideas about direct perception and affordances as relational properties.

9. **Language and Precision**: Heft stresses the importance of using language precisely in scientific discourse to avoid ambiguity or misinterpretation. This includes defining terms like 'affordance' clearly, especially as it has been adopted by various fields beyond ecological psychology (e.g., design, computer science), sometimes with altered meanings.

10. **Continuity and Change**: Heft's perspective on perception, cognition, and memory emphasizes continuity rather than distinct categories or processes. Movement through the world, whether physically or cognitively (e.g., reading a book), involves engaging with ongoing structures that unfold over time, challenging dualistic notions of thought versus action, mind versus body.

This summary encapsulates Heft's nuanced views on affordances and their relevance to understanding perception, cognition, and human-environment interactions within an ecological framework, while also acknowledging historical influences and the challenges posed by language in scientific communication.


The text appears to be a transcription of a conversation between two individuals discussing the work and philosophy of psychologist James J. Gibson. Here's a detailed summary and explanation:

1. **Gibson's Early Research Interests**: The conversation begins by discussing Gibson's early research interests in perception, specifically visual perception as one moves through time (the "jerk transformation"). This concept was explored in his 1930s book about driving and during his work for the Air Force in the 1940s, focusing on pilots' perception of the ground during landing.

This interest stemmed from the understanding that early years are formative for humans, a concept supported by brain plasticity research. Gibson wanted to explore how children's environments influence their development.

4. **Gibson's Teaching Philosophy**: A significant part of the conversation is dedicated to Gibson's teaching philosophy and career. He spent 42 years teaching at an undergraduate college, using his role as a platform to inform students about environmental changes, including climate change. The speaker views this as an act of love - sharing knowledge that empowers students to be conscientious citizens.

5. **Affordances and Perception**: The speakers also discuss the concept of affordances, which Gibson defined as opportunities for action provided by the environment. They agree that humans can change their affordances through altering the environment and by paying attention to it, a process linked to the idea of niche construction in biology.

6. **Meaning and Perception**: Towards the end, they reflect on how understanding perceptual experience and placing attention on it can lead to a different lived experience of the world. This ties back to Gibson's early work and his later focus on children and environmental awareness.

7. **Appreciation for Gibson's Work**: The conversation ends with both individuals expressing their appreciation for Gibson's work and its impact, acknowledging that while his papers are significant, his daily acts of teaching and sharing knowledge with students were perhaps the most meaningful contributions to society.

This text showcases a deep dive into James J. Gibson's career, research interests, and philosophies, highlighting how his early work on perception evolved over time and significantly influenced his later focus on children and environmental education. It also underscores the speakers' admiration for Gibson's dedication to sharing knowledge and fostering environmental awareness among students.

### 20241225 - #44： The Flow of Life： An Unusual Winter Walk with Karl Friston and his Free Energy Principle

In this podcast episode, host Love discusses various philosophical and scientific concepts with renowned neuroscientist Karl Friston. The conversation revolves around Friston's theories of active inference, free energy principle, and their implications for understanding life, consciousness, and cognition.

1. **Flow in Active Inference**: Friston explains that flow can be understood as a state of being in tune with one's environment by gracefully following an attracting set or manifold consistent with the kind of thing one is. This attracting set has low volume, representing only a small number of possible states that align with life and consciousness.

2. **Theory of Mind**: The concept arises when dealing with entities similar to oneself. Friston discusses how, in such cases, a theory of mind is necessary to understand and predict the behavior of these entities. This understanding is based on inference, measurement, or computation.

3. **Paradox in Free Energy Principle**: The free energy principle suggests both minimizing and maximizing energy/entropy simultaneously. Friston clarifies that this apparent contradiction can be resolved by viewing it as a process of balancing the evidence for one's internal model of the world with the surprise or uncertainty of sensory inputs.

4. **Minimax Problem**: The free energy principle is framed as a minimax problem, where one minimizes variational free energy (energy - entropy) to minimize sensory surprise and maximizes variational entropy (entropy of beliefs) to keep options open and maintain flexibility in understanding the world.

5. **Fractal/Multi-scale Perspectives**: Friston emphasizes the importance of multi-scale or scale-free approaches when analyzing various systems, from single cells to communities. The free energy principle can be applied across different scales while considering the context provided by the scale above and the scale below.

6. **Hierarchies in Cognition**: Friston introduces hierarchies as a crucial aspect of cognitive functioning. He distinguishes between 'low-road' and 'high-road' processing, where low-road processes are rapid, automatic, and often subconscious, while high-road processes are slower, more controlled, and deliberate.

7. **Bayesian Statistics**: Friston highlights Bayesian statistics as a tool that allows for subjectivity by setting priors according to one's beliefs or perspectives. This flexibility in modeling helps overcome the limitations imposed by binary thinking.

8. **Keeping Mind Open**: The conversation emphasizes the importance of keeping one's mind open and finding the simplest explanation that generalizes across various scenarios, rather than committing to a single, discrete viewpoint.

The discussion between Love and Friston delves into complex scientific and philosophical ideas, exploring their implications for understanding life, consciousness, and cognition while challenging binary ways of thinking. The free energy principle emerges as a unifying framework that helps explain various phenomena across different scales and levels of analysis.

They propose a more fractal or multi-scale model, where levels of abstraction coexist without any single privileged level of explanation.

1. **Fractal/Multi-Scale Hierarchy**: The discussion revolves around the brain's structure being better described as nested and self-similar, rather than linear. This means that understanding the brain involves multiple levels of abstraction, each constraining the others, without a single definitive explanation. 

2. **Concentric Spheres/Onions Model**: The speakers use analogies to illustrate this concept. They propose the brain's structure as similar to concentric spheres or layers of an onion, with sensory and motor functions at the outer layers and deeper, more abstract representations towards the center. Each layer represents a different level of abstraction and time course.

3. **Sparsity and Causal Structure**: This hierarchy is characterized by sparse connections between adjacent levels, creating a complex web of causal relationships. The idea is that there's circular causality only between subjacent or adjacent levels, not across the entire hierarchy.

4. **Temporal Dynamics**: The speakers emphasize the temporal aspect of this model, where deeper layers represent longer-lasting context and higher layers represent faster-changing content. This mirrors the self-similarity seen in fractals, where patterns repeat at different scales.

5. **Limitations of Observation**: They highlight the limitations of human observation due to our position on a specific scale. We can't observe phenomena beyond certain scales because they change too quickly (quantum level) or last for too short a time (large-scale cosmic events). 6. **Model vs Reality**: There's a discussion about the potential confusion between models and reality, emphasizing that the brain entails a generative model, but this doesn't mean it 'uses' a model in the same way humans do. Instead, our understanding of the brain is inferred through observation and mathematical constructs like free energy principles.

This could metaphorically apply to societal interactions where we fail to understand or connect with others due to perceived differences in implicit generative models.

9. **Flow and Perspective Shifting**: Lastly, there's a reflection on the idea of being 'in the flow' as requiring the ability to shift perspectives and suspend sensory attenuation to truly listen and understand others. This is suggested as crucial for avoiding societal segregation and fostering connection.

Overall, this conversation underscores the complexity of understanding the brain (and by extension, reality) through a fractal or multi-scale model that accommodates various levels of abstraction and temporal dynamics. It also highlights the importance of perspective-taking, empathy, and open-mindedness in navigating our social world.


### 20241227 - Flow, the Free Energy Principle & other Potentials of the Universe with Karl Friston & Andrea Hiott

In this conversation between the host and neuroscientist Carl Friston, they discuss various topics related to Friston's work on active inference and the free energy principle. Here's a detailed summary and explanation of key points:

1. **Career Journey and Influence**:
   - Friston emphasizes the importance of "doing what you're told" in his career, which led him to study various fields like mathematics, physics, medicine, and psychiatry to understand the brain.
   - His interest in understanding others, particularly those with schizophrenia, influenced his later work on disconnection hypothesis, focusing on value-dependent learning and synaptic plasticity.

2. **Brain Imaging and Software Inventions**:
   - Friston played a significant role in developing statistical parametric mapping (SPM) and voxel-based morphometry (VBM), revolutionizing brain imaging techniques.
   - These tools allowed researchers to visualize brain activity, understand functional specialization, and study connectivity, marking a paradigm shift from neuropsychology and electrophysiology to cognitive neuroscience.

3. **Unconscious Inference and Free Energy Principle**:
   - Friston discusses the concept of unconscious inference, which is not about propositional, declarative conclusions but rather the process of reaching a conclusion based on evidence (in this case, sensory input).
   - He explains that everything can be seen as a form of inference or measurement, and life is a flow driven by maximizing evidence for an implicit model of the world.

4. **Thermostat vs. Life**:
   - The conversation highlights the distinction between living entities (like humans) and non-living systems (like thermostats). Living entities have affordances – the ability to generate consequences of their actions based on a generative model – which thermostats lack.
   - This difference is crucial in understanding life as an itinerant, space-filling attracting set that constantly evolves and minimizes free energy to maintain self-organization, avoiding equilibrium steady states (death).

5. **Entropy and Free Energy**:
   - Entropy is explained as a description of a probability distribution, with different entropies corresponding to sensory exchanges or Bayesian beliefs about causes of sensory input.
   - The free energy principle aims to minimize sensory entropy by avoiding surprising sensory samples while maximizing the entropy of Bayesian beliefs, creating a duality with James's maximum entropy principle and Occam's principle.

6. **Fractals, Scale Invariance, and Hierarchies**:
   - Friston emphasizes scale invariance, meaning that the free energy principle applies across various scales (from cells to societies) without loss of the fundamental pattern.
   - He discusses hierarchies not as linear class systems but as nested, multi-scale structures, allowing for a more nuanced understanding of complex systems like the brain.

7. **Avoiding Binary Thinking**:
   - The conversation underscores the importance of avoiding binary thinking (e.g., either/or explanations) in favor of maintaining openness and maximizing entropy to find simpler, more generalizable explanations for understanding the world.

In summary, this dialogue explores Friston's career trajectory, brain imaging contributions, unconscious inference concept, distinctions between living entities and non-living systems, entropy's role in free energy minimization, scale invariance, and the importance of avoiding binary thinking to foster a more comprehensive understanding of complex systems.

### 20241227 - The Self in Oscillation, A Poet's Life with John Koethe and Andrea Hiott

T. is a poet-philosopher from San Diego (born 1945), who studied at Princeton University, became friends with notable poets like John Ashbery and Frank O'Hara, earned a PhD in Philosophy from Harvard, and taught philosophy for many years before retiring. 3. **Romanticism and Modernism**: John sees himself as a romantic poet, despite his deflationary approach influenced by modernists like Eliot and Pound. He believes that both romanticism and modernism share obsessions but differ in their affirmative vs. disillusioned stance.

4. **Influences**: John was significantly influenced by the New York School of Poets, particularly Ashbery, Schuyler, and Kenneth Koch. He attended poetry readings featuring these poets during his time at Princeton, which deepened his connection to their work.

This theme resonates throughout John's poetry and philosophy.

He finds this situation similar to the oscillation between perspectives in philosophy and poetry.

This is achieved through a focus on place, architecture, and the passage of time within personal experiences.

11. **New Book**: John mentions an upcoming book called "Cemeteries and Galaxies," which explores themes of physics, mathematics, and astrophysics alongside poetry, continuing his investigation into oscillation between perspectives and embracing paradoxes.


### 20250101 - Beyond the Blind Spot with Philosopher Evan Thompson

In this conversation, Evan Thompson discusses his book "The Blind Spot" with the host of a philosophy-oriented podcast. The book explores what Thompson calls the "blind spot," a metaphorical term for a fundamental misunderstanding in scientific thought regarding human experience and its relationship to nature.

Thompson identifies this blind spot as arising from a historical shift in modern science, where abstract models and mathematical abstractions have been mistakenly equated with objective reality. This substitution leads to a metaphysical hierarchy that diminishes the significance of human experience and subjectivity within scientific understanding.

The origin of this crisis, according to Thompson, can be traced back to Edmund Husserl's "Crisis of European Sciences," where he pointed out the surreptitious substitution of method for reality in science. This substitution manifests as a bifurcation of nature—the division into subjective (mental) and objective (physical), with human experience relegated to the latter.

The parable of temperature illustrates this blind spot, showing how our lived experiences of hot and cold have been abstracted into mathematical constructs like average molecular kinetic energy. While these abstractions are valuable for understanding nature, they can lead us astray if we forget their origin in human experience.

Thompson emphasizes the importance of recognizing the relational nature of reality and the irreducible complexity that arises from this interconnectedness. He suggests practices such as mindful awareness, cognitive humility, and spiritual exercises to help us embrace uncertainty and recognize our role within scientific inquiry.

The conversation also delves into the historical context of Thompson's upbringing and intellectual influences. Growing up amidst interdisciplinary thinkers like his father William Irwin Thompson, Gregory Bateson, Francisco Varela, Lynn Margulis, James Lovelock, and others, allowed him to appreciate diverse perspectives on nature, science, and human experience.

Thompson highlights the challenges in maintaining this holistic perspective within contemporary society, particularly the political and cultural shifts towards conservatism and neoliberalism since the late 70s/early 80s, which have stifled experimental discussions on nature and human experience. He advocates for a collaborative effort to reassess our value framework, emphasizing ecological network perspectives that encompass art, science, and spirituality to address the climate crisis.

In essence, Thompson's work aims to help individuals recognize their blind spots by fostering a more nuanced understanding of nature, human experience, and scientific inquiry—a process rooted in love for knowledge and appreciation for interconnectedness.


### 20250102 - Moving Beyond the Blind Spot： Seeing what we don't see with Evan Thompson and Andrea Hiott

In the conversation between Evan Thompson and the host, they discuss Thompson's book "The Blind Spot," which explores what he calls the "scientific blind spot" – a disconnect between our scientific understanding of the world and our lived, subjective experiences. This blind spot arises from mistaking abstracted models and idealized theories for the concrete reality of nature, including human experience itself.

1. The Meaning Crisis: Thompson identifies the "crisis" as a meaning crisis, which stems from a worldview embedded within science that elevates objectification over subjective experience. This crisis manifests most tangibly in the climate crisis but isn't a threat to the planet's existence per se – it poses a danger to humanity and other species.

2. Surreptitious Substitution: The German philosopher Edmund Husserl introduced the concept of "surreptitious substitution," where we mistakenly equate scientific models with the nature they're meant to describe, treating these abstract tools as if they represent reality itself. This abstraction of phenomena through idealized mathematical descriptions hides our reliance on human experience as a foundation for all knowledge.

3. Blind Spot Metaphor: Thompson uses "blind spot" as a metaphor for the unseen ways science overlooks its dependence on human subjectivity. Our visual blind spots are a physical illustration of this idea – areas in our field of vision that we can't perceive without moving our eyes, highlighting how certain things remain hidden from our awareness until actively sought out.

4. The Parallel of Temperature: Thompson presents the parable of temperature to demonstrate how scientific abstraction has occluded human experience's primacy. Initially, we understand hot and cold through embodied bodily sensations. Then, as science develops, it creates tools (like thermometers) that quantify these experiences, eventually defining temperature as average molecular kinetic energy. The blind spot occurs when this mathematical construct is mistakenly regarded as more fundamental than our lived experience of heat and cold.

5. Bifurcation of Nature: This concept refers to dividing nature into objective, mathematizable properties and subjective mental experiences. By bifurcating nature in this way, we mistakenly view human consciousness as less fundamental than physical phenomena, creating a hierarchy that undermines the importance of lived experience.

6. Paradox: Thompson and his co-authors argue that science's narratives (such as evolution and cosmology) position humans as a tiny, contingent part of nature while simultaneously acknowledging our role as storytellers within those narratives. This duality creates an apparent paradox – humans are both insignificant and central to scientific stories about the world.

7. Relational Thinking: To address these challenges, Thompson suggests shifting from traditional dichotomous modes of thought (e.g., mind vs. body, subjective vs. objective) towards frameworks that emphasize relationality and complexity. This involves recognizing our inclusion within the networks we study and acknowledging how our observations, measurements, and interventions impact those systems.

8. Practices for Change: Thompson implies that embracing certain practices or spiritual exercises could help scientists maintain a more integrated understanding of their role in the scientific process. These practices might include cultivating cognitive humility, tolerating uncertainty, and remembering the context-dependent nature of all claims about reality.

9. Connection to Ancient Philosophies: Thompson acknowledges that ancient philosophical traditions (like Buddhism and Greek philosophy) often emphasized holistic development, integrating knowledge with character, attitude, and way of life. Modern philosophy has lost this perspective, focusing instead on critical analysis disconnected from personal growth.

10. Cultural Impact: Thompson identifies the breakdown in our cultural relationship with science as contributing to current crises – both individual and environmental. The polarization between scientific triumphalism and denialism, alongside the marginalization of arts and humanities within education, undermines a balanced approach to understanding and engaging with reality.

Thompson's work in "The Blind Spot" encourages readers to critically examine their assumptions about science and subjectivity while advocating for an integrated worldview that recognizes the inseparability of human experience from our understanding of nature.


The conversation revolves around the nature of reality, consciousness, and our understanding of it through scientific models and technological advancements. The speakers discuss the concept of 'blind spots' - areas where we unintentionally misunderstand or overlook aspects due to our cognitive frameworks.

1. **Atoms and Electrons**: They acknowledge that atoms and electrons are real, established through various technologies like microscopes and particle accelerators. This background influenced their approach to studying consciousness, cognition, and our relationship with the natural world.
