### 20250115 - #46： Rhythms of Nature & Love： Hegel's Concept of Life with philosopher Karen Ng

In this conversation between Andrea Sotiriou and Karen Ng, they delve into Georg Wilhelm Friedrich Hegel's philosophy, focusing on his concept of life and its implications for understanding self-consciousness and the relationship between mind and nature. Here are some key points:

1. **Hegel as a Rationalist**: Karen Ng explains that despite common perceptions, Hegel can be seen as a rationalist who expands the understanding of reason to include living entities and their processes. This expansion of reason goes beyond disembodied, abstract thought and embraces the embodied, lived experience of cognition.

2. **Life and Cognition**: The conversation highlights Hegel's exploration of life as a model for understanding self-consciousness (subject-object) and cognition. Unlike traditional views that separate mind and body or subject and object, Hegel sees life as constitutive of self-consciousness, emphasizing the embodied nature of thought and understanding.

3. **Critique of Mechanism**: Hegel challenges the mechanistic view of living beings by demonstrating that cognition is an intrinsic aspect of life, not something added externally. This idea moves away from treating living entities as mere machines with pre-determined functions and instead acknowledges their dynamic, self-organizing processes.

4. **Proposiveness**: Karen Ng introduces the concept of proposiveness, which Hegel inherited from Kant's Third Critique. Proposiveness refers to the capacity of living beings to set goals or purposes according to their own activity (internal proposiveness) rather than being externally imposed. This aligns with the idea that life is a process and not a static end-state, emphasizing continuous becoming over finality.

5. **Speculative Identity Thesis**: A central theme in Ng's work is Hegel's speculative identity thesis, which asserts that self-consciousness is self-conscious life. This radical claim challenges dualistic thinking by insisting that the mind is not separate from nature or living processes but is an integral part of them.

6. **Historical Context**: The discussion touches on how Hegel's ideas fit into broader philosophical currents, particularly his response to Descartes and Kant. While Descartes famously separated mind (res cogitans) from body (res extensa), Hegel sought a more unified view where life and thought are inextricably linked.

7. **Implications for Contemporary Philosophy**: The conversation concludes by exploring the relevance of Hegel's ideas today, especially concerning the computational theory of mind and environmental concerns. By reconnecting mind and nature through his emphasis on life as constitutive of self-consciousness, Hegel offers an alternative framework that could enrich contemporary debates about consciousness, technology, and our relationship with the natural world.

In essence, this dialogue underscores how Hegel's philosophy, particularly his views on life as a model for understanding self-consciousness and cognition, challenges traditional dualisms and offers a more integrated view of mind, body, and nature. This perspective has contemporary implications, suggesting alternative ways to think about consciousness, technology, and our relationship with the environment.


In this dialogue, the participants delve into the philosophical perspective of Georg Wilhelm Friedrich Hegel, particularly his views on love and its connection to life, self-consciousness, and recognition. 

1. **Hegel's Early Views on Love**: The early Hegel viewed love as deeply interconnected with life. For him, both life and love had an "infinite structure," meaning they infinitely return to themselves. This notion is akin to the idea of self-return or self-reference, where something fundamentally remains itself while also changing or evolving.

2. **Friendship and Romanticism**: During his time in Tübingen with friends like Hölderlin and Schelling, love was a significant theme for Hegel. Despite later philosophical shifts, this romantic influence persists in his mature work.

3. **Love as Free Concept**: A key point raised is the interpretation of "free love" in Hegel's philosophy. This isn't to be understood in a contemporary sense (i.e., sexual freedom), but rather as a form of unrestricted conceptual thought. Here, 'free' signifies something that is not fixed or confined; instead, it’s open to all perspectives, much like love itself, which involves seeing the other as fully alive from every point of view.

4. **Love and Self-Consciousness**: Love is linked to self-consciousness for Hegel, implying a recognition of another's life or existence from various perspectives. This ties into his broader idea that self-consciousness itself involves stepping beyond one’s own perspective to encompass others.

5. **Love as Recognition**: Hegel's concept of love extends to recognizing the living nature of things, including natural phenomena. It's about sensing something as alive and dynamic, not static or inanimate. This echoes his belief that true understanding comes from relational processes, where love serves as a potent example.

6. **Criticisms and Misinterpretations**: The discussion also touches on potential misconceptions of Hegel's philosophy, particularly the idea that his conceptual thinking aims to fix or limit things. Instead, participants argue that for Hegel, grasping concepts should be more like loving—an act of fully appreciating something from multiple perspectives without reducing it to a single, fixed viewpoint.

7. **Philosophy's Role**: Lastly, there’s an underlying theme about the role of philosophy and science. Both are seen as ways to engage robustly with reality, aiming to understand and appreciate things as fully alive and dynamic, much like love does.

In summary, this dialogue explores how Hegel's philosophy intertwines love, life, self-consciousness, and recognition, highlighting the importance of perspective-taking and relational processes in understanding existence. It also addresses common misinterpretations of his conceptual thinking, emphasizing its open-ended, dynamic nature rather than a tendency towards fixity or limitation.


### 20250116 - Patterns in the Movement： Hegel's Concept of Life & Problem of Contradiction with Karen Ng & A Hiott

In this conversation between Andrea E. Speer (Andrea) and Karen Ng, they delve into Georg Wilhelm Friedrich Hegel's philosophy, focusing on his concept of life. Here are key points discussed:

1. **Hegel as a Rationalist**: Despite common misconceptions that Hegel is an irrationalist or mystical thinker, Karen Ng argues that he is indeed a rationalist with a subtle and expansive way of thinking about reason permeating the living natural world.

2. **Life and Rationality**: The conversation highlights how Hegel connects life and rationality, challenging the modern dualistic separation between them. This connection is explored through Hegel's idea of self-consciousness as a subject-object phenomenon, where living beings are both subjects with perspectives on the world and objects that can be perceived by other subjects.

3. **Proposiveness**: Karen Ng discusses Immanuel Kant's concept of proposiveness (internal and external) to understand the organization of nature. Internal proposiveness refers to living organisms setting their own goals based on their intrinsic unity and self-organization, while external proposiveness suggests that natural forms have ends or purposes given by an external source, like a designer god.

4. **Hegel's Speculative Identity Thesis**: Ng explains how Hegel builds upon Kant's internal purposiveness to propose a speculative identity thesis, which argues for life as a process with ongoing, non-finalistic goals. This idea of purpose is not tied to an endpoint but rather emphasizes the continuous becoming and self-actualization inherent in living beings.

5. **Life as Meaning**: Ng discusses Hegel's view that meaning and intelligibility are fundamentally connected with life itself. She explains how, for Hegel, sense-making, reproduction, and ethical life emerge from the intrinsic properties of living beings.

6. **Overcoming Dualisms**: Both discussants highlight how Hegel's philosophy aims to transcend dualistic ways of thinking (e.g., mind vs. body or subjective vs. objective) by revealing interconnectedness and organic unity in life processes. This approach contrasts with the mechanistic models that were prevalent during his time, emphasizing instead the distinctiveness and autonomy of living beings.

7. **Kant's Copernican Turn**: Ng discusses Kant's Copernican turn—shifting from a view where objects conform to our knowledge to acknowledging the subjective role in shaping what we perceive as knowledgeable—as crucial for understanding Hegel's philosophy, which further develops and refines this perspective.

8. **Hegel's Relevance**: Despite the complexity of Hegel's writing style, his ideas remain pertinent today, especially in discussions about the nature of life, self-consciousness, and the relationship between subjective and objective aspects of reality. The conversation underscores how Hegel's speculative identity thesis offers a nuanced understanding that transcends rigid dualisms.


The conversation revolves around the philosophical ideas of G.W.F. Hegel, particularly focusing on his views of life, self-consciousness, and their interconnectedness. The speaker, a scholar specializing in Hegel's philosophy, shares personal anecdotes about encountering Hegel's work and how it has influenced their perspective on life and nature.

One central concept discussed is the "speculative identity thesis," which posits that self-consciousness is self-conscious life. This idea challenges traditional dualisms, such as the Cartesian mind-body split, by asserting that thinking and cognition are inherently embodied and rooted in living processes.

The speaker highlights Hegel's emphasis on the living body's role in understanding and making sense of the environment, arguing that this perspective is revolutionary in the history of philosophy. Despite being written over 200 years ago, Hegel's ideas remain pertinent today, offering an alternative to contemporary computational theories of mind, which reduce mental processes to machine-like operations.

The discussion also touches upon related philosophers and their contributions, including Fichte, Schelling, Kant, Descartes, and more modern thinkers like Evan Thompson and Peter Godfrey Smith. The speakers agree on the value of revisiting German Idealism for contemporary philosophical inquiries, especially regarding the connection between life, mind, and nature.

The conversation concludes by reflecting on the transformative power of Hegel's ideas and their potential to reshape our relationship with nature amidst pressing environmental issues. The speakers also touch upon themes of love as a manifestation of recognition and understanding in Hegel's philosophy, emphasizing its significance in the context of self-consciousness.

Throughout the conversation, there is a shared appreciation for Hegel's challenging yet rewarding philosophical language and methods, which demand a nuanced and dedicated approach to fully grasp their depth and implications.


### 20250124 - #47： Love Beyond Subject and Object with therapist & neuroscientist Mark Solms

In this conversation, neuroscientist Mark Solms discusses the nature of love, consciousness, and subjectivity. He begins by explaining that love is not a simple feeling but a composite state of mind involving several emotional dispositions or drives. These include lust (sexual desire), seeking (curiosity and exploration), panic/grief (attachment), care (nurturing), and play (social dynamics). Romantic love, in his view, requires the integration of these various components to be successful.

Solms emphasizes that feeling is fundamental to our nature, as it allows us to perceive and respond to our needs and environment. He distinguishes between basic states of mind like feelings and more complex ones involving self-awareness (first and second persons). The latter emerges when feelings extend onto objects and we can reflect on ourselves as having these feelings, which is crucial for the development of empathy and love.

Regarding narcissism, Solms explains that it represents an initial state where self-love dominates, with a lack of recognition for others' subjectivity and needs. To progress from narcissism to object love (romantic or otherwise), one must integrate the libidinal drive (sexual desire) with attachment drives (care, panic/grief). This integration entails vulnerability and acknowledging that the beloved has their own needs and perspective.

In discussing consciousness, Solms clarifies that he interprets it as basal, raw feeling—the first-person experience of being an object with subjective experiences emerging later. He argues for a scientific approach to understanding subjectivity within the broader framework of natural science, countering dualistic views that separate consciousness from the physical world.

When questioned about his work and influences, Solms mentions his translations and revisions of Sigmund Freud's works, highlighting Freud's project of integrating subjective experience into natural science. He also references neurologist Oliver Sacks for inspiring him to consider the importance of patients' subjective experiences in understanding brain function.

Solms identifies himself as a dual-aspect monist, believing that the physical and mental aspects of reality are two sides of the same coin. He argues that our objective perceptions are subjective representations shaped by our sensory systems, and a unifying language is needed to bridge these perspectives. Ultimately, he advocates for an ongoing exploration and refinement of our understanding of consciousness and subjectivity within the scientific realm.


### 20250125 - Research Diary #8： Hegel and the inspiration of 'aufheben' #aufhebung #philosophy #practice

Andrea, in her diary entry, explores the German term "Aufheben" (pronounced A-U-F-H-E-B-E-N), a word used by philosopher Georg Wilhelm Friedrich Hegel. The term is multifaceted and can be translated into English as 'sublation,' which carries several nuanced meanings.

1. **Lifting Up or Suspending**: Aufheben signifies both lifting up or elevating (Aufheben), and suspending (Heben). This dual nature reflects the concept's intricacy and its ability to encompass seemingly contradictory actions.

2. **Transcendence and Cancellation**: It can also mean transcending (overcoming or moving beyond) or cancelling out (eliminating). The word hints at a dynamic process of superseding, where something is not merely discarded but rather transformed or integrated into something new.

3. **Continuous Movement**: Aufheben embodies the idea of movement and change, without necessarily returning to an original state. Andrea likens this to a spiral—a metaphor often used in cognitive science, philosophy, and neuroscience to describe non-circular, progressive processes that evolve while retaining elements from their past states. This movement isn't redundant; it results in new configurations with each iteration.

4. **Paradox and Contradiction**: Aufheben encapsulates the philosophical notion of holding contradictory ideas simultaneously, akin to F. Scott Fitzgerald's quote about entertaining two opposing thoughts at once. It embodies the human experience of grappling with dualities like right and wrong or up and down, recognizing that these distinctions are fluid and interconnected.

5. **Connection to Taoism**: Andrea draws parallels between Aufheben and concepts found in Taoism, such as the yin-yang symbol, which illustrates the coexistence of seemingly contrary forces in a dynamic, cyclical relationship. This reflects her view that Aufheben embodies a broader philosophical thread seen across various disciplines and cultures.

6. **Hegelian Dialectic**: While not explicitly stated, the concept of Aufheben is closely tied to Hegel's dialectical method—the process involving thesis, antithesis, and synthesis. However, Andrea emphasizes that Hegel's description of this process is nuanced and cannot be fully captured in static language; one must engage with his works as a practice or method of inquiry.

In essence, Aufheben represents for Andrea a philosophical tool to navigate the complexities of human existence—an approach that acknowledges and integrates contradictions, fostering an understanding of life's perpetual movement and interconnectedness. She views this concept as central to her own exploration of paradox, love, and the human condition, drawing inspiration from literature, poetry, and diverse philosophical traditions.


### 20250127 - #48： In Defense of the Human Being with Thomas Fuchs

In this conversation between Andrea Siniawski and philosopher Thomas Fuchs, they discuss the topic of embodiment and conviviality as presented in Fuchs' book "In Defense of the Human Being." The central theme revolves around the human being's relationship with technology, self-contempt, and glorification, often oscillating between these two extremes.

Fuchs argues that modern society is moving away from a traditional understanding of humans as embodied beings interacting with others in the world. Instead, this view is challenged by developments like artificial intelligence (AI), transhumanism, and virtualization, which question our humanity's uniqueness.

The conversation begins by addressing why defending the human being is necessary. Fuchs highlights three primary fields where traditional notions of humans are being questioned: AI, transhumanism, and virtualization. He emphasizes that he wants to defend a new kind of embodied humanism, one that acknowledges our constitutive relation with the environment and other living beings.

Andrea and Fuchs then discuss the instability present in our relation to AI, where we oscillate between viewing ourselves as superior (grandiose) or inferior (self-disdain). This instability is rooted in modernity, which has left us metaphysically homeless, leading to constant striving for progress and empowerment.

Fuchs introduces the concept of "inner bodily resonance," where life itself is an experience, and being alive is a continuous feeling that permeates all actions, perceptions, thoughts, and emotions. This feeling is grounded in our organism's constant interaction with the world, making us fully embodied beings rather than separate entities within our bodies.

The conversation explores the difference between dualistic and holistic perspectives on embodiment. Dualism posits the body as an object or instrument for the mind or soul to use, whereas a more embodied perspective recognizes that the feeling of being alive is deeply embedded in our organism.

Fuchs emphasizes the importance of understanding our thoughts as emerging from this vital bodily feeling rather than identifying with them as separate entities. This insight helps counteract the tendency to confuse our technology, including AI, with ourselves, perpetuating an unhealthy relationship between humans and their creations.

The idea of conviviality is introduced as a way to appreciate life's continuity and shared living. It stems from the Latin prefix "con" (with or together) and the root "viv," meaning life. Conviviality suggests an awareness of our shared existence with others, acknowledging that we are part of a larger web of life rather than isolated individuals.

The discussion also touches on the historical evolution of the mind's concept, moving away from dualism towards recognizing that our thoughts and experiences arise from our embodied existence. This understanding can help counteract the confusion between our thoughts and our true selves.

In essence, Fuchs' work encourages a return to an embodied perspective on humanity, embracing our organic nature and interconnectedness with others and the world around us. By fostering conviviality, we can find a more balanced, humble, and authentic relationship with ourselves and technology, avoiding both self-contempt and grandiosity.


### 20250128 - Mark Solms on Love and Philosophy

Mark Solms, a neuroscientist and neuropsychologist, discussed the nature of love, consciousness, and subjectivity with Andrew Galambos on the podcast "Love and Philosophy." Here's a detailed summary and explanation of their conversation:

1. Love as a feeling and a composite state: Solms views love as a complex emotional experience that involves multiple basic emotions or drives. He distinguishes romantic love from other forms, such as familial love or the love of knowledge. In his view, romantic love consists of several components: lust (sexual attraction), attachment (panic/grief – anxiety about loss), care (nurturing instinct), play (social engagement and power dynamics), rage (frustration management), and seeking (curiosity and exploration).

2. Integration of emotions in love: Successful romantic relationships require integrating these emotional drives, as they can sometimes conflict with each other. For example, attachment feelings may clash with rage or seeking, necessitating the balancing of needs and desires for a harmonious relationship.

3. Fear in love: Solms acknowledges that fear (anxiety about losing a loved one) is an essential aspect of romantic attachment, and it's a price one must be willing to pay for proper connection. However, he clarifies that fear should not be confused with generalized anxiety or the fear of harm, which are distinct emotional states.

4. Love as a fundamental part of human nature: Solms agrees with philosopher Jonathan Lear's view that love is a basic force in human nature. Feeling, he argues, is integral to our existence and consciousness. Feelings signal the mind about whether needs are being met or not, driving us to take voluntary actions to improve our situation.

5. The development of love: Solms discusses how the capacity for love emerges through mental development, involving a shift from narcissism (self-love) to object love. This transition requires recognizing interdependence with others and acknowledging their needs and perspectives – a process that involves vulnerability.

6. Consciousness as feeling: Solms presents his perspective on consciousness, arguing that it originates from the experience of feelings. He posits that there are two levels of consciousness – basic, raw feelings and second-order consciousness, where one can reflect upon those feelings about objects.

7. Subjectivity in neuroscience: Solms emphasizes the importance of integrating subjective experiences into natural science, particularly neuroscience. He believes that understanding how the brain creates a sense of self and subjectivity is crucial for a comprehensive grasp of its functioning. This integration involves recognizing the unique perspective each person has on their own consciousness while also accounting for shared physical reality.

8. Dual aspect monism: Solms identifies as a dual aspect monist, believing that subjective and objective aspects of reality are interconnected and underpinned by common underlying realities inferred from perceptions. He sees the mind-body problem as a paradox to be held together rather than resolved into distinct categories.

In essence, Solms' view on love emphasizes its complexity and multifaceted nature, highlighting that it involves various drives and emotions. His perspective on consciousness underscores the primacy of feeling as the basis for mental states and subjective experiences, ultimately connecting these ideas with his commitment to integrating subjectivity into neuroscience.


### 20250131 - #49： Empowerment in Robotics： Solo Brainstorm & AI Bonus Conversation with Dari Trendafilov

Dari Trendelov, a researcher in swarm robotics and artificial intelligence, discussed empowerment as an information-theoretic quantity used to measure an agent's potential to influence its environment. In the context of swarm robotics, empowerment is applied to predict and optimize collective performance without centralized control.

Empowerment is defined by channel capacity – the maximum mutual information between an agent's actions and subsequent sensory observations. This measure reflects the level of control or predictability in the perception-action loop between the agent (robot) and its environment. The more correlated actions and perceptions are, the higher the empowerment, indicating that the agent can effectively manipulate its surroundings to achieve its goals.

In human-computer interaction (HCI), Dari initially worked on improving user interface usability by finding a more rigorous measure of effectiveness than traditional usability testing methods. During his PhD research in HCI, he discovered empowerment as a potential solution because it could quantify affordances—the possibilities for action offered by the environment to an embodied agent—in a principled way.

Empowerment is flexible and can be applied to various tasks or scenarios since it depends on the model's specification of both agents and environments. Measured in bits, empowerment provides a benchmark for understanding the range of possible outcomes for a given problem. However, its values cannot be directly compared across different models because they are relative to their respective contexts.

In HCI, empowerment helps understand and improve user satisfaction by encapsulating multiple factors contributing to uncertainty in human-device interactions, such as sensor imprecision, communication delays, key sizes, and other disturbances. By measuring these factors collectively using the empowerment metric, designers can optimize the interface for better performance without explicitly considering each individual factor separately.

When applying empowerment to swarm robotics, it helps understand how individual agents' intrinsic motivations influence the overall collective intelligence of the group. The measure allows researchers to predict and control the level of redundancy within the swarm system, ensuring efficient problem-solving while minimizing costs (e.g., sensor range, communication range).

Though empowerment is not directly used to create intelligence in robots, it serves as a tool for understanding and modeling collective behavior, including aspects of "intelligence" found in natural swarms like bird flocks or ant colonies. It offers insights into how individual agents' actions contribute to the overall group's performance and helps identify optimal system parameters for various tasks.

In Dari's research, the most exciting moments come from bridging different scientific fields and applying empowerment across seemingly disparate paradigms. He sees empowerment as a versatile building block that can be utilized in both natural sciences and humanities to uncover foundational problem-solving processes present in various entities, including humans expressing love.


### 20250201 - Life nested within life： In Defence of the Human Being with philosopher & psychiatrist Thomas Fuchs

In "In Defense of the Human Being," philosopher Mark Cohen argues that the traditional understanding of humans as embodied beings interacting with others is being challenged by developments in artificial intelligence, transhumanism, and virtualization. These advancements question our distinctiveness from machines we create and blur the line between reality and simulation.

Cohen defends a new form of humanism that acknowledges embodiment as central to understanding humanity. He distinguishes two ways of thinking about the body: dualistic (viewing it as an object or instrument) and embodied (recognizing oneself as fully inhabiting the body). The latter, according to Cohen, is characterized by a vital feeling of being alive that accompanies all experiences, thoughts, feelings, and actions.

Cohen contends that modernity has led humans to become metaphysically homeless, trying to compensate through empowerment and progress. This results in a narcissistic grandiosity (self-admiration) oscillating with self-contempt (self-disdain). In the context of AI, this manifests as feeling both superior to and inferior compared to our creations, leading to instability and confusion about our place in the world.

Cohen suggests that humans must overcome their narcissistic image and adopt a more modest self-image. This would involve recognizing our limitations, accepting our vulnerabilities (such as mortality), and appreciating our embodied existence as the source of freedom, richness, and wealth in human life.

The book explores themes like embodiment, intercorporeality (the bodily interaction between individuals), and conviviality (shared embodied experience). Cohen argues that these concepts are crucial for understanding our primary relation to others and that losing touch with them can lead to loneliness and confusion.

Cohen also discusses the concept of love as an expression of a loving engagement, emphasizing that recognizing and understanding other human beings is only possible through this kind of relationship. This perspective on knowledge contrasts with traditional detached scientific approaches, advocating for a more embodied, engaged way of relating to both the natural world and fellow humans.

Finally, Cohen highlights the importance of balancing linear time (constant acceleration) with cyclical, repetitive forms of experience that align better with our basic living processes. By nurturing this balance in interactions with others, we can cultivate awareness of our bodily presence and help each other regain a sense of being fully present.


### 20250205 - Solo Brainstorm then Empowerment in Robotics with Dari Trendafilov #hci #informationtheory

In this conversation, Andrea and Dari discuss the concept of Empowerment in the context of swarm robotics and artificial intelligence. Empowerment is an information-theoretic measure that quantifies an agent's potential to influence its environment. It is used as a tool to understand and optimize the perception-action loop between an agent (such as a robot) and its environment.

Dari, who works in swarm robotics and artificial intelligence, explains that Empowerment helps predict swarm performance and design decentralized decision-making controllers for autonomous swarms. It is applied in various scenarios like collective perception tasks and site selection problems.

Empowerment is defined as the channel capacity between an agent's actions and subsequent sensory observations. This measure maximizes mutual information or correlation between actions and perceptions, indicating how well an agent can control its environment through the perception-action loop.

The conversation also touches on the relationship between Empowerment and intrinsic motivation. In this context, intrinsic motivation refers to drivers that are inherent to the agent itself, independent of external forces. Empowerment, as a measure of control over an environment, is driven by intrinsic motivations since it encourages agents to maximize their influence without relying on external guidance or reward systems.

Andrea, with a background in human-computer interaction (HCI), initially sought measures to improve the usability of mobile devices. She discovered Empowerment as a potential tool to quantify affordances—the possibilities for action offered by an environment—in HCI systems. Empowerment's flexibility allows it to be applied across various scenarios and models, providing a benchmark for evaluating control within specific contexts like user interfaces or robot swarms.

The discussion then expands to the application of Empowerment in understanding human-computer interactions. By modeling uncertainties (noise, disturbances) within the interaction process, Empowerment offers a single, computable measure of user satisfaction or system performance. This allows designers to optimize system parameters like keyboard size, communication range, and sensor capabilities without extensive experimentation.

Dari highlights that Empowerment is not intended to create intelligence in robotic swarms; instead, it serves as an analytical measure to understand and predict the quality of processes involving agents interacting with their environment. This understanding can guide designers in building systems that behave intelligently within specific constraints.

The conversation concludes by exploring the broader implications of Empowerment across various fields, including humanities. Dari expresses excitement in applying Empowerment to disparate scientific and philosophical domains, emphasizing its potential as a unifying concept that transcends traditional subject-object dichotomies.

### 20250213 - #50： Love and Liberation in Times of Crisis with philosopher Rupert Read (and Wittgenstein)

Rupert Reed is a philosopher, public intellectual, and activist who has experienced various crises throughout his life. In his youth, he witnessed the UK's three-day week crisis with power cuts, which instilled in him a sense of societal fragility. Later, in his thirties, Reed faced a severe episode of anxiety and depression that significantly influenced his philosophical thinking and writing.

Reed's work often revolves around themes of crisis, liberation, love, and the therapeutic potential of philosophy. His book "Wittgenstein's Liberatory Philosophy" emphasizes that true freedom lies in being liberated from ideological preconceptions and thought traps, ultimately leading to relational autonomy. This approach stems from Wittgenstein's ideas about the importance of language games and the rejection of private languages.

Reed believes that philosophy can help individuals deal with difficult emotions by uncovering entrenched thought patterns, such as those experienced during anxiety and depression. He argues that understanding freedom as relational rather than individualistic is crucial for overcoming societal and civilizational crises.

These practices have helped him navigate difficult emotions and thought patterns by fostering mindfulness, compassion, and connection with others. He also draws inspiration from philosophers like Nietzsche, Wittgenstein, and Foucault, emphasizing their shared passion for truth and wisdom.

Reed's current focus is on the climate crisis and ecological degradation. He co-founded Extinction Rebellion (XR), an international movement that uses nonviolent civil disobedience to compel action against the breakdown of ecosystems, and later led the Climate Majority Project, which aims to build a broad coalition of support for climate solutions.

Reed advocates for truth-telling, emotional resilience, and inner work as crucial aspects of facing crises like the climate emergency. He encourages people to engage in difficult conversations without anger or blame, fostering depolarization across society. In essence, Rupert Reed's life and work demonstrate the transformative power of philosophy, spiritual practices, and a commitment to truth and relational autonomy in navigating personal crises and engaging with societal issues such as climate change.

1. **Through Topianism**: Kingsnorth introduces the concept of "through topianism," which he defines as navigating through the challenges ahead, rather than striving for a utopia or even a traditional dystopia. This perspective is inspired by Ursula Le Guin's work and emphasizes genuine intergenerational solidarity. 6. **Invitation to Participate**: Kingsnorth encourages listeners to explore the Climate Majority Project and consider their role in addressing climate change using their unique skills and resources. He emphasizes that this initiative is designed to be adaptable and applicable anywhere, regardless of location or existing climate activism.

In summary, Paul Kingsnorth presents a nuanced approach to environmental activism through his concept of "through topianism." This perspective encourages intergenerational solidarity, recognizing the role of difficult emotions in driving action, and invites broad participation in addressing climate change using individual strengths. His work emphasizes the importance of balancing these elements while remaining hopeful and committed to positive change.

### 20250227 - #51： Body Knowledge with philosophers Shay Welch and Elena Cuffari

Discomfort, they suggest, is not solely an individual's experience but can be a signifier of oppressive structures within society or academia. They advocate for embracing this discomfort as a means to challenge the status quo and work towards more inclusive frameworks in philosophy.

The conversation concludes by acknowledging the richness of lived experiences, the importance of diverse perspectives, and the need to move beyond binary thinking (i.e., right/wrong, normal/abnormal) when engaging with new ideas or approaches within philosophy. They invite listeners to join their ongoing exploration and dialogue about these themes, emphasizing that no one is inherently 'wrong' but rather inviting a shared journey towards understanding and growth.


### 20250227 - What are the Es of Cognition？ Andrea Hiott 4E is Embodied, Embedded, Enactive, Extended, Ecological

Andrea is providing an overview of 4E cognition (also known as "embodied, embedded, enactive, extended") and its implications. Here's a detailed breakdown:

1. **Embodied**: This aspect emphasizes that cognition is not confined to the brain but involves the entire body. It suggests that our physical experiences and interactions with the world play a crucial role in how we perceive, understand, and process information. Our bodily sensations, movements, and physiological states are integral to our mental processes. 

2. **Embedded**: This facet underscores the idea that cognition is not isolated within an individual but is deeply interconnected with the environment. Our minds are not separate from the world; instead, they emerge from ongoing interactions with our surroundings. Every encounter and engagement with the environment contributes to our cognitive processes.

3. **Enactive**: Enaction posits that cognition is an active, dynamic process rather than a passive representation of the world. It involves the continuous creation and maintenance of meaning through our interactions with the environment. Our mental life isn't just about reflecting the world; it's also about actively shaping and influencing our experiences.

4. **Extended**: The extended aspect of 4E cognition suggests that our cognitive processes aren't limited to our brains but can involve external tools, technologies, and even other people. This idea is inspired by the concept of the 'extended mind,' proposed by philosophers Andy Clark and David Chalmers. For example, using a notebook to remember tasks or a computer for calculations could be seen as extensions of our cognitive abilities.

Andrea stresses that 4E cognition is a holistic view that takes the body and environment seriously in understanding mental processes. It contrasts with computationalism, which views thinking as a form of internal representation within the brain. While she acknowledges the value of computational models, she argues that they don't capture the full complexity of human cognition, particularly its physical and relational aspects.

She recommends further reading, including "The Embodied Mind" by Evan Thompson, Francisco Varela, and Eleanor Rosch, and works by Andy Clark and David Chalmers to explore these ideas in more depth. She also suggests watching videos and engaging with various resources she'll provide to gain a comprehensive understanding of 4E cognition.


### 20250305 - #52： We Are Not Machines! Agency & Science beyond Computationalism with Fotis and Yogi (Johannes ...

In this conversation, Johannes Jäger, also known as Yogi, discusses his philosophical views on science, technology, human agency, and love. Here's a detailed summary of the key points he makes:

1. **Critique of Modernity**: Yogi argues that modernity, with its focus on mechanistic and computational models of the world, has led to an attitude problem in humanity. We've become passive consumers, mistaking machines for living beings and extending this misconception into various fields, including AI.

2. **Machine View Misconception**: He contends that we're treating AI as if it were sentient or conscious, which is a mistake. AI systems are merely sophisticated algorithms designed to detect correlations and make predictions; they lack true agency, self-manufacture, and embodiment—key aspects of living organisms.

3. **Agency and Embodiment**: Yogi emphasizes the importance of understanding what constitutes an agent or living being. True agency is rooted in autopoiesis (self-manufacturing) and embodied cognition, which AI lacks. Living beings are self-constructing entities that build their hardware and programming environment as they exist.

4. **The Problem with Current AI**: Yogi criticizes the current AI research landscape, arguing that it is driven by a misguided business model and computationalist assumptions about agency and consciousness. He claims this leads to overhyped theories, such as Mike Levin's and Carl Friston's free energy principle, which ignore the crucial difference between living systems and non-living ones.

5. **The Need for a New Philosophy of Science**: Yogi advocates for a paradigm shift in science that recognizes the limitations of computational models and focuses on understanding the organization and self-manufacture of living systems. This new perspective would yield a more accurate and ethical approach to technology, allowing humans to interact with it in a more responsible manner.

6. **Love as Connection**: Yogi discusses love within the context of human connections, emphasizing that true love necessitates acknowledging and addressing painful truths about others and ourselves. He advocates for tough love—not sparing people from difficult realities when necessary—to foster growth and better relationships in society.

7. **The Relationship between Agency, Love, and Longevity**: Yogi explores how our relationship with the world and ourselves is intertwined with normativity and love. He argues against a focus on longevity or eternal life, suggesting that such desires reflect a distorted view of humanity's place in the world. Instead, he advocates for appreciating our interconnectedness with living beings, embracing relational processes as they evolve and develop, and accepting our fallibility as part of being human.

8. **Meta-modernism and Tough Love**: Yogi introduces meta-modernism as a philosophical approach that emphasizes relations over objects and possession. He suggests that in critical times for humanity, we must practice tough love by acknowledging and addressing painful truths about ourselves and others, even if it means challenging those in power or refusing love to fundamentally evil or deluded individuals. Ultimately, Yogi argues, this approach is necessary for humanity's survival and the creation of a sustainable future.

In summary, Johannes Jäger (Yogi) presents a critique of modern scientific views that equate living organisms with machines or computational systems. He argues for a new paradigm in science that recognizes the unique characteristics of living beings—self-manufacture and embodiment—and emphasizes relational processes, love, and tough love as essential elements for human growth and societal progress. Yogi's philosophy advocates for an ethical approach to technology and fostering connections with the world and other living beings, rather than pursuing unrealistic notions of eternal life or perfect control over nature.


### 20250306 - Body Knowledge with Shay Welch, Elena Cuffari and Andrea Hiott #philosophy #embodied

In this conversation, Shay Welsh and Elena Kufari discuss their experiences and perspectives on embodied cognition, marginalized body epistemologies, and the challenges they face in academia. They emphasize that embodied cognition has its roots in feminist and Indigenous philosophies but often remains unrecognized or marginalized within mainstream academic circles.

Shay Welsh, a professor at Spelman College, organized a conference titled "Marginalized Body Epistemologies and 4E Cognition" to create a space for women and people of color working in this field. The conference focused on first-person perspectives, participatory sense-making, and the role of the body as a source of knowledge rather than just an object acted upon. Welsh highlighted that while these ideas have been explored by marginalized groups for centuries, they are often excluded from mainstream philosophy discussions.

Elena Kufari, a professor at Franklin and Marshall College, discussed her work on linguistic autonomy in interactions with AI and non-human agents and gesture methodology. She also mentioned her interest in exploring human-human interaction dynamics in oppressive situations to better understand participatory sense-making.

The conversation touches upon the challenges of integrating embodied cognition into traditional academic settings, where there is often resistance from established power structures that favor Eurocentric philosophical traditions. The discussion also delves into the concept of "normal" and how it is socially constructed. However, societal norms can still dictate what is deemed acceptable or standard, often marginalizing those who don't fit into these prescribed categories.

The speakers emphasize the importance of co-responsibility in understanding how individuals contribute to oppressive systems and the necessity to examine one's own discomfort when interacting with others. They advocate for embracing discomfort as a means of growth, recognizing that it often signals resistance against societal expectations.

In conclusion, Shay Welsh and Elena Kufari advocate for a more inclusive approach to embodied cognition, which acknowledges the diverse experiences of individuals and their unique perspectives on knowledge acquisition. They encourage embracing discomfort as a catalyst for change, fostering an environment that welcomes various epistemologies and ontologies, ultimately enriching our understanding of the world and ourselves.


### 20250308 - We are not machines! with Fotis and Yogi (Johannes Jäger)

Johannes Jäger, known as Yogi online, is a natural philosopher and biologist who aims to challenge the modern worldview that treats the world and living beings as machines. He advocates for a shift towards understanding humanity's relationship with the world based on experience rather than computation or control.

Yogi argues against the mechanistic materialism that underpins much of contemporary science, industry, and society, which views the universe as a machine governed by deterministic laws, where everything can be predicted and controlled. This perspective, he claims, is not only an outdated view but also dangerous because it fosters hubris, leading to unintended consequences when applied to complex systems like ecosystems or human societies.

Yogi's proposed alternative is a metamodern philosophy of science and worldview that acknowledges the complexity and unpredictability inherent in living systems. He emphasizes the importance of autopoiesis, or self-manufacturing, as a fundamental characteristic of agency. Unlike algorithms or artificial intelligence (AI), living beings produce their parts and assemble them in ways that allow for continued existence and emergent behavior not easily predicted by formal models.

This distinction between living systems and machines has profound implications for how we understand cognition, consciousness, and technology. Yogi argues against the notion of AI as an agent or possessor of agency, claiming that such thinking misrepresents the true nature of intelligence and leads to unrealistic expectations about what machines can achieve. Instead, he advocates for a more modest appreciation of AI as algorithmic mimicry – tools that simulate certain cognitive abilities but do not truly possess them.

Central to Yogi's perspective is the idea that human beings are embedded in their environments and have unique relationships with living systems. He calls for a return to animistic or traditional cultural perspectives that recognize this interconnectedness, fostering appreciation for nature and other living beings rather than treating them as resources to be controlled or manipulated.

Yogi sees the current obsession with technological immortality – extending human life indefinitely through advanced medical and genetic technologies – as a manifestation of an unhealthy self-regard. He argues that no one genuinely desires eternal life, as such an extension would only exacerbate the sense of insufficiency inherent to our mortal condition. Instead, he encourages embracing our finitude and understanding ourselves as part of larger relational processes within the world.

In terms of societal relationships, Yogi advocates for a more realistic assessment of our interactions with others. He suggests that we need to stop naively believing in the transformative power of dialogue or niceness alone and instead acknowledge the necessity of sometimes being selective about which relationships and individuals we invest in, particularly when confronted with those who are fundamentally deluded or evil.

Overall, Yogi's philosophical project seeks to cultivate a more nuanced understanding of humanity's place within the world, emphasizing our relational nature and the importance of appreciating the limits of our knowledge and control. He calls for a rethinking of our fundamental assumptions about reality, cognition, and technology, which he believes are essential to navigating the challenges posed by an increasingly complex and interconnected world.

### 20250322 - #54： Project Love： What We Make & How it Makes Us with service designer Clive Grinyer

Project Love is an initiative led by designer Clive Grinier that aims to explore how design can foster greater love between individuals and across society. The project was commissioned by the FETSA Institute, a US philanthropic organization, with the goal of creating tangible actions, artifacts, rituals, and interactions that promote love in various contexts—individual relationships, community, society, nature, and spirituality.

Grinier, who is the head of service design at the Royal College of Art, assembled a diverse team of designers, including recent graduates, tutors, and external experts from fields such as AI, finance, economics, and inclusive design. The team defined love using a framework that includes:

1. **I**: Individual self-love and well-being
2. **We**: Relationships between two people (romantic or platonic)
3. **Us**: Community and societal love, focusing on neighborhood acceptance and understanding
4. **Nature**: Appreciation of the natural world and our place in it
5. **Spiritualism**: Exploring how religions and cultures express and act upon love

The team spent three months researching and developing concepts before presenting their ideas at the Royal Society of Arts. The project resulted in numerous innovative designs, such as:

- **25 Degree Bench**: A bench designed with a 25-degree angle at each end to encourage conversation between people sitting on it. It can be used both physically and digitally (with QR codes for storytelling).
- **Nature Frame**: An empty frame placed in parks, which encourages users to appreciate nature by framing natural scenes differently. This project also includes educational games about local ecosystems.
- **Compliment Cards**: Physical cards to give compliments to others, along with an AI program that monitors emails and social media to provide tips on being kinder in digital communications.
- **Love After Death Monument**: A secular gravestone placed in natural beauty spots for scattering ashes after death, featuring a digital platform for sharing stories and memories about the deceased.
- **Interconnected Touch App**: An app that creates a visual representation of people touching each other's screens anonymously on public transport or large surfaces, symbolizing human connection in an impersonal urban environment.

The project has received significant attention, with media coverage and partnerships emerging from King's College London to develop wellbeing tools based on these concepts. Grinier hopes that Project Love will inspire a revolution in thinking about design and its potential to create positive social change by integrating love as a core principle.

The project also emphasizes the importance of understanding design as an embodied, ongoing relationship with the world rather than merely visuals or fashion-oriented creations. By fostering this mindset, Grinier aims to challenge preconceptions about design and encourage people to engage more critically with the objects, technologies, and spaces they interact with daily. Ultimately, Project Love seeks to demonstrate that design can play a significant role in shaping our environment for greater love, empathy, and compassionate human connections.


### 20250322 - Project Love： If a Thing Loves with Clive Grinyer and Andrea Hiott

Project Love is an initiative that explores how design can foster empathy, compassion, and love. Initiated by Clive Grinyer, a service design lecturer at the Royal College of Art (RCA), Project Love was commissioned by FETSA, a U.S.-based philanthropic organization interested in examining the topic of love through tangible actions. The project's primary goal is to redefine and make love more accessible and meaningful in the 21st century, focusing on three dimensions: self, community (us), and society (them).

1. Self: Project Love explores personal well-being and self-regard, emphasizing the importance of befriending oneself before attempting to befriend others – a concept rooted in Aristotle's teachings. This aspect delves into understanding love from an individual perspective and fostering emotional growth.

2. Community (us): Focusing on neighborhood relationships, Project Love aims to bridge gaps among neighbors who might live near each other without knowing one another. Clive Grinyer shares a personal experience of organizing a street party during the coronation of King Charles III, which brought together diverse religious and cultural groups in his local area. This aspect highlights the power of community engagement and inclusivity.

3. Society (them): Project Love tackles societal issues such as conflict, immigration, and division by attempting to detoxify society through love-centered initiatives. It explores how design can contribute to healing and understanding in larger contexts, encouraging empathy and connection among strangers and diverse communities.

Throughout the project, a team of designers from RCA and other institutions worked together to create tangible artifacts, rituals, and interactions that could promote love across these dimensions. They developed concepts like:

- 25-degree benches: Designed with angled seats to encourage conversation between strangers. A digital version of the bench allows people to share stories of love at specific locations.
- Compliment cards and AI tools: Encouraging meaningful compliments in workplaces or personal relationships, using both physical cards and an AI program that analyzes emails and social media for kindness opportunities.
- Secular gravestones (SELF): Memorializing loved ones in natural landscapes with a digital component to share messages, voices, and stories.
- Empty frames in parks: Encouraging people to appreciate nature differently by framing scenic views, leading to new ways of experiencing the environment.
- Multicultural events: Gatherings that celebrate diverse communities, fostering understanding through shared experiences like poetry recitation and storytelling.

The project resulted in an exhibition at the Royal Society of Arts and a recognition from Tim Brown (Chair of IDEO) that it could be "the thing after design thinking." Project Love has also led to discussions around developing a manifesto, principles, and training opportunities for designers.

Clive Grinyer emphasizes the importance of redefining love in design to extend beyond romantic notions, embracing broader concepts like compassion, tolerance, and empathy. He encourages people to understand that design is an ongoing process that encompasses every aspect of our lives – from the objects we use to the digital spaces we navigate – and should be guided by human-centered principles, empathy, and a focus on creating positive impacts in society.

Project Love aims to inspire a new era in design thinking, moving away from traditional methods that merely improve functionality or aesthetics toward designing for deeper human connections, emotional well-being, and social cohesion. The project highlights the potential of design to address complex societal challenges by fostering empathy, compassion, and love – ultimately creating a more harmonious world.


### 20250330 - #55： How Shall we Live？ with anthropologist and lineologist Tim Ingold

Tim Ingold is a British anthropologist, philosopher, and author known for his unique perspective on human cognition, movement, and wayfaring (moving through the world). He challenges traditional notions of embodiment, agency, and cognition by emphasizing the continuity between thinking and moving. Ingold argues that cognition should be understood within the context of skilled perception and attention rather than as a separate entity.

1. Embodiment: Ingold is wary of the term "embodied" because it often implies a separation or sinking of skill into the body, which he finds problematic. He prefers terms like "animate" to emphasize the liveliness and dynamism of human activity.
2. Cognition vs. Locomotion: Ingold objects to the opposition between cognition and locomotion, arguing that they are not distinct entities but rather different aspects of a continuous process. He suggests that attention is a fundamental mode of our being, always attending to something in the world from the start.
3. Process over Outcome: Ingold prioritizes process over outcome, emphasizing dwelling (being in the world) over building (creating separate objects within it). This perspective highlights the importance of understanding human activity as a continuous engagement with the environment.
4. Teaching and Education: Ingold views teaching as an essential part of anthropology and a way to redefine education. He argues that introducing new people into a world we love (amor mundi) is fundamental to education, as it fosters respect for the world and encourages engagement with its inherent complexity.
5. Digital Technology: Ingold is skeptical about the long-term sustainability of digital technology due to its environmental impacts. He believes that focusing on traditional skills and processes will be crucial for future generations.
6. Attention and Thoughtfulness: Ingold emphasizes the importance of attention and thoughtfulness in human cognition. He argues that responsibility requires responsiveness, which in turn necessitates educated attention developed through interaction with others and the world around us.
7. Lineology and Wayfaring: Ingold's concept of lineology explores the continuous paths we follow throughout our lives, from birth to death. By examining these lines, we can better understand our fundamental modes of movement and engagement with the world.

In summary, Tim Ingold's work challenges conventional wisdom about human cognition, embodiment, and agency by emphasizing the continuity between thinking and moving. He advocates for a redefined understanding of education that fosters respect and engagement with the world, prioritizes process over outcome, and acknowledges the importance of attention and thoughtfulness in our interactions with the environment.

### 20250404 - How shall we live？ Weaving lives with anthropologist and lineologist Tim Ingold & Andrea Hiott

Tim Ingold, an emeritus professor of anthropology at the University of Aberdeen, has developed a unique perspective on life, nature, culture, and human existence that diverges from traditional understandings. His approach emphasizes the interconnectedness of all living beings and the importance of relationships in shaping our understanding of the world.

Ingold's ideas revolve around the concept of "lines" – not just physical lines but also metaphorical ones representing the paths we create as we live, learn, and interact with others. This includes footpaths created by Sami people in Finland, which symbolize individual and collective journeys, as well as more abstract lines formed through reading books, having conversations, and creating art.

Key influences on Ingold's thinking include his father, a mycologist who studied fungi, and his experiences with Sami culture. Fungi, with their extensive mycelial networks, exemplify the idea of lines as interconnected pathways. The Sami people's concept of individuals being tied to their footpaths resonated with Ingold, reinforcing his view that humans are fundamentally connected to the paths we forge in life.

Playing the cello has also played a significant role in shaping Ingold's thought process. As a gestural instrument, it requires pulling lines from the instrument, which mirrors how he views knowledge as something drawn or extracted rather than passively received. This perspective is reflected in his writing, where he emphasizes the act of creating and extending lines through human activity and relationships.

Central to Ingold's philosophy is a critique of the modern dichotomy between nature and culture. He argues that these terms have been misunderstood and proposes returning to their original meanings: "nature" from nassere, meaning birth or generation, and "culture" from colere, meaning cultivation or caretaking. By redefining them in this way, Ingold emphasizes humanity's role as stewards of life and the environment – a responsibility that extends beyond humans to include all living beings.

Ingold is also critical of the concept of embodiment, arguing that it often conflates bodily activity with a mystical envelopment or sinking of skills into the body. Instead, he advocates for a clearer distinction between physical action and skill acquisition. Furthermore, Ingold expresses skepticism about digital technology's long-term relevance, believing it is transient compared to thousands of years of human practices and skills that should be preserved and passed down through generations.

In summary, Tim Ingold offers a holistic perspective on life, nature, culture, and human existence. His ideas emphasize interconnectedness, relationships, and the creation of lines as paths – literal and metaphorical – through which humans engage with the world. By reimagining traditional concepts like nature and culture, Ingold underscores humanity's role as caretakers of life and encourages us to cherish and extend the lines we create in our lives. His philosophy is deeply rooted in personal experiences, including his father's mycological work, encounters with Sami culture, and cello playing, which have collectively shaped his unique worldview.


In this conversation, the speaker discusses various influences on his approach to understanding the world and human experience, particularly in relation to writing notation, mycology, Sami culture, and cello playing. Here's a detailed breakdown:

1. **Mycology**: The speaker draws parallels between the linear structure of fungal mycelium and his writing style. Fungi grow in a network of lines, which has inspired him to adopt a similarly linear approach in his work.

2. **Sami Culture**: The Sami people's concept of individuals being connected to their personal footpaths resonated with the speaker. This idea of a person being defined by their path aligns with his perspective on identity and experience.

3. **Cello Playing**: The speaker attributes his gestural approach to writing to his long-time practice of playing the cello. He describes how learning music on this instrument involves pulling sounds from it, which has influenced his perception of writing as a similarly tactile, embodied act.

The speaker also expresses reservations about the concept of "embodiment," particularly the conflation of being bodily with something sinking into or becoming part of the body. He prefers terms like "animate" to describe the liveliness of human activity.

In discussing cognition, he argues against separating it from other movements and processes, suggesting that cognition is better understood as a form of skilled perception, or attention. He proposes starting with attention rather than treating it as a subsidiary aspect of thinking.

The speaker emphasizes the importance of process over outcome, using examples like dwelling (a process) versus building (an outcome). This perspective underscores his belief that understanding our movements and wayfaring—how we navigate through life—is fundamental to grasping human experience and how we should live.

He sees education as crucial in rekindling love for the world, emphasizing attentiveness, responsibility, and respect for the present world as essential aspects of human fulfillment and sustainability.

In essence, the speaker's approach to understanding the world is rooted in process-oriented thinking, drawing from diverse influences like nature, culture, and personal experiences. He advocates for a more holistic view of cognition as intertwined with movement and attention, emphasizing love for the world as a guiding principle for human existence and education.


### 20250413 - #56： Radical Incompleteness & Windows of Ritournelle with poet philosopher Bayo Akomolafe

In this conversation between Andrea Hyatt and Bayo Okomolafe, they discuss the themes of binaries, dichotomy, love, and care, drawing from Okomolafe's personal experiences and philosophical work. Here is a detailed summary and explanation:

1. Binaries as modulated ways to organize the world:
   - Okomolafe sees binaries (e.g., light vs. darkness, man vs. woman) as attempts to simplify complexity for convenience and intelligibility in modernity.
   - He argues that this simplification leaves out processual relational tensions within categories, leaving gaps or "cracks" where new worlds and possibilities can emerge.

2. Care and structure:
   - Okomolafe discusses the importance of care as a form of structuring to navigate complexity.
   - He explains how categories can become carceral (oppressive) if not carefully managed, emphasizing that even the act of categorizing itself can inadvertently cause harm.

3. Ritornello – the concept of repetition and cracks within binaries:
   - Okomolafe introduces the idea of ritornello, a refrain or repeating story, as a metaphor for understanding binaries' limitations and potential for emergence.
   - He suggests that within categories (rituals/cultures), there are always cracks where new possibilities and connections can occur, such as his experience with his daughter being stuck in a room.

4. Relationality and multiplicity:
   - Okomolafe emphasizes the importance of understanding things not as separate entities but in relation to one another.
   - He argues for embracing multiplicities and complexity, using examples from sensory overload, trauma, and the idea that categories are not bad but can become oppressive if not critically examined.

5. Personal growth and understanding:
   - Okomolafe shares his personal journey of trying to understand and connect with his late father through faith, philosophy, and the acceptance of life's messiness.
   - He explains how his search for connection ultimately led him to embrace a more processual, agential realism perspective that acknowledges multiplicities within reality.

6. Love as radical incompleteness:
   - Okomolafe defines love through the lens of radical incompleteness and cracks within structures (ritornello).
   - He sees love as emerging from these gaps, shaping embodied spaces while acknowledging ongoing risks in differing fields.

7. Care and moral holding spaces:
   - Okomolafe discusses how care can sometimes become stifling or a trap if not balanced with an awareness of its limitations.
   - He argues that one should question the "shoulds" and embrace the complexities of life, including contradictions and feelings, to foster true connection and understanding.

8. The role of disruption in personal growth:
   - Okomolafe reflects on how disruptions (e.g., his daughter being locked in a room) can lead to profound connections and new ways of thinking.
   - He suggests that such experiences help crack open rigid structures, allowing for the emergence of alternative possibilities and understandings.

In summary, Bayo Okomolafe's philosophy revolves around challenging binaries and rigid categorizations to reveal underlying complexities and potentials for connection within our world. By acknowledging life's inherent messiness and the presence of cracks (both literal and metaphorical), he invites readers to explore relationality, multiplicities, and the transformative power of love as a force that bridges gaps and shapes embodied spaces.


### 20250417 - #Love is the radical incompleteness of everything with Bayo Akomolafe and Andrea Hiott

The conversation between Andrea Hyatt and Bayo Okomolafe revolves around the themes of binaries, categories, care, love, and the complexities of human existence. Here's a detailed summary and explanation of key points discussed:

1. **Binaries and Categories:**
   - Binaries are simplified ways to understand complexity, but they can be limiting and exclusionary (ontospillage).
   - Categorizing the world into binaries ignores relational tensions and processes that shape reality.
   - Even though we may find solace in structure and categorization (retornello), it's essential to recognize their limitations.

2. **Care as a Double-Edged Sword:**
   - Care can be both nurturing and restrictive; it can provide comfort but also lead to confinement or "carceral" situations.
   - Cultures, rituals, and societal norms (categorizations) can function as holding spaces for individuals while also potentially imprisoning them.

3. **Cracks and Love:**
   - Cracks in binaries represent opportunities for new perspectives and connections. They symbolize love's radical incompleteness and insufficiency, which allows for growth and transformation.
   - Love is decoloniality and the process of crafting embodied spaces despite risks, differing structures, and ongoing change (intimacy between field and structure).

5. **Love as a Transformative Force:**
   - Love is seen as a radical force that embraces incompleteness, which enables growth, connection, and transformation across various aspects of life (familial, romantic, and philosophical).
   - Love encourages ongoing shape-shifting and exploration beyond established structures or ideologies.

6. **The Role of Experimentation:**
   - Experimentation is essential for navigating disturbing moments and uncertain times, fostering parapolitics (political actions outside traditional systems) and post-activism (new forms of engagement beyond conventional activism).
   - By embracing the crack—an imperfect, incomplete space that challenges norms—we can create opportunities for change within flailing care paradigms.

In summary, Andrea Hyatt and Bayo Okomolafe's conversation explores how binaries and categories shape our understanding of reality while acknowledging their limitations. They delve into the complexities of love, care, and personal growth, emphasizing that embracing incompleteness and exploring cracks within established systems allows for profound transformation and connection across various aspects of life. The conversation between the host and Kevin Kelly, a renowned futurist, technologist, and author, delves into various aspects of Kelly's life, career, and philosophies. Here is a detailed summary and explanation of key points discussed:

1. Early encounters with computers:
   - At the age of 13, Kevin Kelly accompanied his father to an early computer exhibition in Atlantic City (around 1965). The experience was unremarkable because computers were massive cabinets without screens or keyboards, primarily used for complex calculations.

2. Influence of science fiction:
   - Science fiction significantly influenced Kelly's understanding and perception of technology. He believes that if scientific literature acknowledged the impact of science fiction, we would discover a more substantial role for speculative fiction in shaping technological research and development.

3. The humanizing aspect of computers:
   - Kelly's interest in computers reignited when they started connecting people through telephones and the internet. He found this fusion more "human" as it facilitated communication, community-building, and access to hard-to-find information.

   - Inspired by the Whole Earth Catalog, Kelly sought a job there during his high school years. His submissions were initially rejected, but he persisted in contributing content, particularly focusing on budget travel.

   - Kelly emphasizes the importance of patience in identifying emerging frontiers, noticing new languages, and following passions. He believes that being wrong is essential to growth, encouraging individuals to learn from their mistakes.

### 20250510 - How Online Became a Country with one of the fathers of the internet Kevin Kelly & Andrea Hiott

The conversation between the interviewer and Kevin Kelly revolves around various aspects of his life, career, philosophies, and insights on technology, humanity, and creativity. Here's a detailed summary:

1. **Early Exposure to Computers**: Kevin Kelly had an early encounter with computers as a teenager in the 1960s when his father took him to a computer show in Atlantic City. He describes the experience as uninteresting since the computers were large cabinets without screens or keyboards, and interactions were done via punch cards. Despite this initial lack of interest, science fiction had already piqued his imagination about future possibilities.

2. **Influence of Science Fiction**: Kelly emphasizes the significant role science fiction played in shaping his vision of technology's potential. He believes that if scientific literature required citing science fiction influences, we'd uncover the genre's profound impact on research and development.

3. **Computer Revolution**: For Kelly, the true revolution began when computers started interacting with telephones, leading to the internet era. This convergence humanized technology for him by creating a sense of connection between people across distances, enabling communities and information sharing.

4. **Whole Earth Catalog and Stuart Brand**: The Whole Earth Catalog, founded by Stuart Brand, had a significant impact on Kelly's life. As a teenager, he was fascinated by its focus on recycling, sustainability, and systems thinking – themes that resonated with his own interests. Years later, when he discovered Lonely Planet guides through the catalog, it led to his involvement in travel writing and eventually Wired magazine.

5. **Travel and Perspective**: Kelly highlights the transformative power of travel, especially for young people. He advocates for subsidizing youth travel to broaden their horizons and challenge cultural assumptions. Travel helps individuals perceive the world from different perspectives, fostering creativity, adaptability, and personal growth.

6. **Habits and Mindset**: Throughout his life, Kelly has cultivated habits and mindsets that promote flexibility and continuous learning. He values the ability to switch perceptions and embrace new ideas, which he associates with both reading science fiction as a child and traveling as an adult.

7. **Advice for Living**: In his book "The Advantage of Being Disadvantaged," Kelly shares practical advice based on his experiences. Some key takeaways include:

   - Embrace being the only one rather than trying to be the best, which means staying true to your unique path and passions.
   - Cherish time wealth over monetary wealth, understanding that having control of your time is a form of true richness.
   - Pursue real-time conference reporting or similar projects to explore emerging fields and establish yourself as an expert.

8. **Career Evolution**: Kelly's career evolved organically through a series of events driven by his passions, including founding the Well (an early online community), attending the Artificial Life Conference in Los Alamos, writing about artificial life, and eventually editing Wired magazine. His ability to adapt and follow his interests led him to become influential in shaping narratives around technology, science, and culture.

9. **Writing as a Tool for Discovery**: Kelly writes to explore and clarify his thoughts on various topics, using the process as a means of self-discovery. This approach has allowed him to stay ahead of trends and contribute significantly to discussions surrounding artificial life, complexity, and the internet's early days.

Overall, Kevin Kelly's narrative underscores the importance of curiosity, adaptability, and following one's unique passions in shaping a fulfilling life and career. His experiences illustrate how science fiction, travel, and an open-minded approach to learning can lead to profound insights and contributions to emerging fields.

Embracing growth through process and movement is another central theme, where constant improvement leads to a greater sense of purpose.

7. Hill Climbing vs Hill Making:
Kevin introduces the concept of "hill climbing" (optimizing existing structures) versus "hill making" (creating new territories or opportunities). The idea is that true innovation and impact come from the latter, as opposed to merely improving upon existing systems or ideas.

8. Love:
In the context of their conversation, love emerges as a key element for fostering responsible growth and maintaining balance in life, helping individuals avoid getting trapped in pursuits that lead to misery despite external success.
