### 20250518 - Complexity Coding, Residuality Theory, the Hyperliminal： being flexible and rigid at once with Barry

Barry O'Reilly is a software architect, author, and former mathematician who has brought complexity theory into the field of software architecture. He argues that traditional linear thinking, which is crucial for creating computers, is detrimental when applied to human systems and business environments, leading to rigid and brittle software solutions.

O'Reilly introduces the concept of "residuality" as a solution to this problem. Residuality theory suggests that instead of trying to capture a static snapshot of a dynamic business environment in code, we should embrace uncertainty and focus on creating flexible, adaptable systems. This involves modeling software based on potential changes rather than fixed states, allowing the system to survive unexpected shifts in its environment.

Historically, software development has been driven by linear thinking, leading to a persistent "software crisis" characterized by projects that fail to deliver on time or don't work as expected. O'Reilly believes this crisis stems from an unyielding attempt to maintain stillness and certainty in a world that is inherently fluid and uncertain.

His approach draws from process philosophy, which acknowledges the constant change and movement of reality, rather than trying to impose rigid structures on it. By applying these principles to software architecture, O'Reilly aims to create systems that can adapt and thrive in the face of uncertainty, ultimately leading to more resilient and effective software solutions.

This shift in perspective challenges traditional software engineering practices and requires a rethinking of how we model and build software systems. It also touches on broader philosophical concepts such as complexity theory, process philosophy, and the nature of human cognition, offering a fresh, interdisciplinary approach to solving long-standing problems in the field of software development.


In this conversation, the speaker discusses their journey into the philosophical underpinnings of software architecture, tracing influences from ancient Greek philosophers like Parmenides and Plato, through Ludwig Wittgenstein, Bertrand Russell, John von Neumann, Alan Turing, and Karl Popper, to modern process philosophy. This intellectual pursuit began with Popper's critique of Plato's need to "hold things still" and expanded into a deep dive into various philosophical schools of thought.

The speaker highlights the paradox faced by software architects: balancing linear, logical thinking necessary for coding with the complexity and unpredictability inherent in human systems. This necessitates embracing uncertainty while still maintaining control over software design. The speaker emphasizes the importance of understanding when to apply which thought process - rigid logic or fluid flexibility - depending on the context.

The conversation then delves into the philosophical shift that has occurred, moving away from a Newtonian mindset focused on certainty and control towards embracing uncertainty as a fundamental aspect of software design. This approach acknowledges the inherent complexity and unpredictability of human systems and leverages this uncertainty to create resilient, adaptable software architectures.

The speaker introduces the concept of "hyperliminality," describing it as a state where architects must navigate between rigid, structured software design and the fluid, complex world of human systems. This constant back-and-forth is challenging but necessary for creating software that can function effectively within unpredictable environments.

An illustrative example provided is the use of "stress testing" in software design - intentionally introducing absurd or seemingly random scenarios (like giant lizards attacking a city) to understand how a system responds and adapts under extreme conditions. This method reveals unexpected vulnerabilities and resilience, enabling architects to create more robust systems that can withstand unforeseen disruptions.

The speaker also discusses the importance of self-reflection and forgiveness in the profession, encouraging architects to understand their own decision-making processes better and to empathize with stakeholders who may not possess the same technical acumen. This introspection fosters a more compassionate, less stressful approach to software architecture.

The conversation concludes by touching on themes of love and connection in this philosophical journey, emphasizing how delving into these ideas can lead to a richer understanding not just of software but also of one's own thought processes and relationships with others. The speaker underscores the value of their work in bridging the gap between technical expertise and broader human experience, ultimately enriching both aspects of life.

The speaker's journey exemplifies the potential for interdisciplinary learning to enhance professional practice. By integrating philosophical insights into software architecture, they've developed a more nuanced, adaptable approach that acknowledges and works with the inherent uncertainties of human systems. This perspective not only improves software design but also fosters a more balanced, empathetic professional mindset.


### 20250523 - Residuality Theory, Hyperliminality & the Architect's Paradox with Barry O'Reilly & Andrea Hiott

Barry O'Reilly is a software architect and author who has introduced residuality theory into the field of software design. This theory stems from his exploration of complexity sciences and process philosophy, offering an alternative to traditional linear thinking that underlies most software development practices. Here's a detailed explanation:

1. **Linear Thinking vs Human Systems**: O'Reilly argues that software engineers are trained in linear thinking – logical problem-solving, one step at a time, always striving for correctness and clarity. While this approach was essential to invent the computer, it's ineffective when dealing with human systems due to their inherent uncertainty and fluidity.

2. **The Architect's Paradox**: The paradox lies in the application of linear thinking to complex human environments. Software developers build rigid, brittle models that quickly break as the reality moves and changes. This leads to constant software updates, poor quality, and an ongoing crisis within the industry.

3. **Residuality Theory**: Residuality theory proposes a shift from static representations of reality to embracing uncertainty and change. Instead of rigid, object-oriented models (common in software design), it suggests thinking about systems as collections of potential 'trouser legs' or scenarios – different ways the system might evolve based on various factors.

   - **Residues**: These are snapshots of the system at specific times from a particular perspective. When reality shifts, residues help maintain functionality by adapting the architecture to new conditions without breaking it entirely.
   
   - **Multiple Realities**: Rather than one definitive model, residuality theory acknowledges multiple possible realities and prepares software for various scenarios. This shift moves from Platonic ideals (single, perfect representations) to Bergson's multiplicity – systems capable of adapting across a range of conditions.

4. **Philosophical Underpinnings**: Residuality theory draws from process philosophy and complexity science. It challenges the substance philosophy prevalent in object-oriented software design (treating everything as objects) by embracing the fluid, process-driven nature of reality.

5. **Benefits**: By thinking this way about systems, software architects can create structures more likely to survive unexpected changes or events not accounted for in initial specifications. This results in resilient software capable of adapting and functioning off-spec – a crucial aspect of an architect's role.

6. **Innovation**: While complexity theory has been applied to computing before, O'Reilly's residuality theory is novel within enterprise software, bringing deep philosophical concepts (like those from Kauffman networks and process philosophy) into the realm of coding practices. It challenges conventional wisdom and offers a fresh perspective on designing adaptive, robust software systems for complex human environments.


In this conversation, software architect Simon Brown discusses his book "The Architect's Paradox" and his philosophical approach to software design and architecture. Here are the key points:

1. **Frustration with the Software Industry**: Brown was frustrated with the state of the software industry, feeling that linear thinking and traditional engineering paradigms were insufficient for addressing complex real-world problems. He wanted a better way to understand why some developers struggled while others succeeded instinctively.

2. **Paradox in Software Engineering**: Brown identified a paradox in software engineering – the need to use linear thinking (for code execution) and nonlinear, flexible thinking (for dealing with complex systems and changing requirements). He felt that the industry lacked a language or methodology to handle this paradox effectively.

3. **Philosophical Influences**: To understand this paradox better, Brown delved into philosophy, exploring thinkers like Plato, Aristotle, Kant, Wittgenstein, and others. He discovered that many of the assumptions underlying software engineering (e.g., permanence, cause-and-effect) have philosophical roots, particularly in the work of Ludwig Wittgenstein and Bertrand Russell.

4. **Residuality**: Brown's concept of "residuality" aims to bridge this paradox by embracing uncertainty at the core of software design. Instead of trying to eliminate uncertainty (as traditional methods attempt), residuality acknowledges it and uses it to create robust, adaptive systems. This approach involves subjecting a system to various stresses (including absurd scenarios like giant lizards) to reveal its resilience and discover emergent properties.

5. **Hyperliminality**: Brown introduced the term "hyperliminality" to describe the state of software architects who must navigate between rigid, linear systems (required for code execution) and complex, changing real-world environments. Architects must constantly shift perspectives and adapt their thinking to accommodate this liminal space.

6. **Playfulness and Philosophy**: Brown emphasizes the importance of playfulness in software design, encouraging architects to explore absurd scenarios and use humor to foster creativity and resilience in their systems. This approach helps architects build more adaptable software that can handle unforeseen circumstances.

7. **Love and Forgiveness**: Brown discusses the role of love and forgiveness in software design, encouraging architects to look inward, understand their decision-making processes better, and extend forgiveness to stakeholders whose behavior may be driven by the same uncertainties that the architects themselves grapple with. This helps reduce bitterness and frustration, leading to more open, collaborative relationships within development teams.

8. **Accessing Philosophy Through Software**: Brown's book aims to make philosophy accessible to software professionals by connecting abstract concepts to relatable software-specific examples. This approach helps readers understand and apply philosophical ideas in their work more effectively than traditional philosophical texts might allow.

9. **Balancing Linear and Nonlinear Thinking**: Ultimately, Brown advocates for a balanced approach that embraces both linear (for code execution) and nonlinear (for dealing with complex systems) ways of thinking. This dual perspective enables architects to create more robust, adaptable software that can thrive in the uncertain real world.

In summary, Simon Brown's philosophical approach to software architecture emphasizes embracing uncertainty, playfulness, and a balanced blend of linear and nonlinear thinking. By exploring the roots of software engineering assumptions in philosophy, he offers a novel perspective on designing adaptable systems capable of navigating complex, ever-changing real-world environments.

Boma's interest in inner transformation and sustainability drove her to pursue a PhD focused on the relationship between inner and outer transformation. She found it challenging to navigate the academic world, as the topic of inner transformation was not yet established in sustainability science. Her entry point into relational philosophy was through indigenous wisdom, which resonated with her.

She also drew inspiration from Karen Barad's work on entangled relationships and process philosophers like Alfred North Whitehead. Boma emphasizes the importance of understanding that inner and outer transformation are interconnected aspects of a relational worldview. She aims to develop transformative educational processes to teach people how to shift from mechanistic to relational thinking.

In her work, Boma addresses the knowledge-action gap, where individuals know what is good for them or the planet but struggle to act accordingly. This dilemma arises due to complexities in measuring immeasurable aspects of interconnected systems. She seeks to bridge this gap through her Institute for Practical Ecophilosophy, which promotes living philosophically as a means for personal and planetary health.

The institute's philosophy gym concept aims to create collaborative spaces where individuals can engage in sense-making sessions about topics they care deeply about, fostering collective understanding and problem-solving. Boma is currently working on a European Union-funded project that will develop philosophy gyms for small and medium enterprises (SMEs), helping them navigate ethical decision-making within complex systems.

Boma's approach to measuring the success of these philosophy gyms involves considering "warm data" – qualitative, experiential metrics that capture the essence of personal growth and community engagement. She also explores dynamical measurement, acknowledging that assessments should not be rigid but adaptable to changes in circumstances and perspectives.

Throughout her journey, Boma has grappled with balancing various disciplines, avoiding oversimplification, and maintaining authenticity while engaging diverse audiences. She emphasizes the importance of holding paradoxes and embracing ambiguity in our understanding of complex systems and interconnected relationships.

In summary, Jessica Boma's work revolves around bridging inner transformation and outer change through a relational worldview. By fostering collaborative spaces for sense-making and addressing the knowledge-action gap, she aims to promote living philosophically as a means for personal and planetary health. Her Institute for Practical Ecophilosophy and innovative concepts like the philosophy gym exemplify her commitment to creating practical applications of relational philosophy in diverse contexts, from individual lives to corporate decision-making.

### 20250709 - The Lure of Convenience with philosopher of technology Sabina Leonelli and Fotis Tsiroukis

In this conversation, Sabina Leonelli discusses her work as a philosopher of science and technology, focusing on the intersection between humanities, philosophy, history, and contemporary data-intensive biological practices. She emphasizes the importance of understanding how scientific knowledge is produced, reliable, and integrated into society for its betterment rather than becoming an end in itself.

Leonelli's approach to philosophy of science is characterized by a hands-on, collaborative method that combines conceptual and empirical engagement with scientific practices. This perspective stems from her lifelong fascination with figures who transcended disciplinary boundaries, such as natural philosophers, and her belief in the necessity of understanding the human role in the world by examining our interrelation with nature.

Key influences in her intellectual journey include Azok Chang, who introduced her to complementary science – the idea that history can supplement contemporary scientific understanding by considering paths not taken; and Mary Morgan, who sparked her interest in social sciences. Leonelli's work often involves tracing links between continental philosophical traditions and naturalistic perspectives with current developments in philosophy of science.

Leonelli highlights two significant issues in contemporary society: the obsession with control and convenience. The first issue revolves around the utopian desire for complete human mastery over the environment, which is unattainable due to our limited decision-making abilities and historical precedents showing the impossibility of such control. The second issue pertains to the lure of convenience in managing complex life situations, leading people to seek effortless solutions through technology, often at the expense of engaging deeply with the world.

Leonelli argues that this combination is dangerous because it fosters a tendency towards total control and minimal personal engagement, which can create victims by disempowering individuals in their ability to navigate life's complexities. She contends that social media exemplifies this trend as the "biggest Panem et Circenses" strategy, entertaining citizens while distracting them from questioning authority and seeking better lives.

In terms of her work, Leonelli explores various topics:

1. The history and philosophy of organisms used in research, particularly why biologists study seemingly unrelated creatures like worms to understand human biology. She collaborates with Rachel Ankeny on this topic, examining the implications of these choices for biological understanding and textbook content.
2. Data-driven approaches in science, focusing on how data analysis is often mistakenly viewed as an objective process free from choice or subjectivity. She argues against this misconception, highlighting the importance of acknowledging researchers' assumptions, methodological choices, and collaborations in generating reliable scientific knowledge.
3. The role of tacit knowledge and know-how in scientific research, particularly in biology, which often goes unrecognized or undervalued in favor of quantitative methods. She explores the implications of this exclusion for scientific progress and understanding.
4. The impact of social context on scientific developments, emphasizing the importance of considering scientists' values, interests, constraints, and institutional contexts when assessing research outcomes and directions.

Leonelli's work challenges entrenched hierarchies among different modules of knowledge, advocating for a more nuanced appreciation of qualitative methods alongside quantitative ones in addressing complex issues like global emergencies (e.g., the COVID-19 pandemic). She encourages a holistic approach to scientific inquiry that acknowledges various forms of expertise and the interplay between them, ultimately fostering better-integrated, more effective solutions to pressing problems.

In conclusion, Leonelli's philosophy of science aims to bridge disciplinary boundaries by promoting a collaborative, empirical approach that recognizes the subjectivity inherent in scientific practices and the importance of engaging with the world in all its complexity. This perspective offers valuable insights for rethinking how we approach scientific research, technological innovation, and societal challenges.


### 20250716 - #65 Play & Meaningful Uncertainty： Active Inference and the Intellectual Sweet Spot with Julian K...

Julian Kiverstein, a senior researcher in the psychiatry department at Amsterdam University Medical Center, discusses play, its relation to active inference, and how it connects with phenomenology, embodied cognition, and well-being. He emphasizes that play involves a balance between uncertainty and certainty—not too complex to understand yet not too simple with no learning potential.

Kiverstein's interest in play arose from his work on predictive processing and active inference, which led him to explore the epistemic aspects of play, such as curiosity and the resolution of uncertainty. He collaborates closely with Mark Miller, examining emotions and cognition, particularly valence (the positive or negative feeling accompanying an emotion).

Their work suggests that play is intrinsically motivating due to its ability to offer a balance between challenging one's understanding of the world while maintaining a sense of safety. This balance allows individuals to resolve uncertainties, thereby improving their cognitive skills and overall well-being. Kiverstein describes play as a form of niche construction where agents modify their environment to create an optimal level of surprise or challenge.

In this context, "surprise" refers to the right amount of uncertainty for learning and growth. Play spaces are intimate places held by others (e.g., parents, therapists) that enable exploration without danger. They provide a safe zone where individuals can develop their skills, engage with affordances (opportunities for action), and cultivate meaning in life.

Kiverstein highlights the importance of play for survival and flourishing by referencing Stuart Brown's research on play's role in preventing mental illness, as observed in animals and humans who lacked play opportunities during childhood. This connection implies that play may help avoid rigid patterns or attractors (habitual ways of engaging with the world) that could lead to psychological issues like addiction, OCD, or depression.

The discussion also explores the connection between play and mindfulness, as well-attended, playful exploration fosters an open and curious attitude towards the world—a state of beginner's mind. This attitude, in turn, allows for fresh appreciation and wonder in everyday experiences.

Kiverstein emphasizes the need for academic spaces that accommodate play and curiosity to avoid the rigidity often encountered in academia. He argues that fostering playful environments within education can help cultivate creativity, critical thinking, and well-being among students.

In summary, Julian Kiverstein's exploration of play highlights its role as an essential mechanism for cognitive development and well-being through balancing uncertainty and certainty in a safe environment. This process enables individuals to learn, grow, and discover meaning in life while breaking free from rigid patterns or attractors that could potentially lead to mental illness. By promoting playful exploration within academic settings, educators can encourage curiosity, creativity, and overall well-being among students.


### 20250716 - Meaningful Uncertainty and the Intellectual Sweet Spot： Julian Kiverstein on Play & Active Inference

Julian Baggini is a philosopher who studies the mind through the lens of embodied cognitive science, particularly focusing on the free energy principle (FEP) and active inference. His work explores how these principles can be applied to understand various aspects of human experience, including mental illness and wellbeing.

Baggini's interest in this area began with his undergraduate discovery of "The Embodied Mind" by Francisco Varela, which combined cognitive science, Buddhist philosophy, phenomenology, and dynamical systems theory to understand the relationship between mind, body, and human experience. This interdisciplinary approach resonated with Baggini's own interests in Buddhism.

Collaborating with Mark Miller at the University of Toronto, they've investigated mental illness (depression, addiction, OCD) using FEP/active inference. They propose that these disorders involve an intolerance for uncertainty, leading to rigid ways of perceiving and acting in the world. Play, on the other hand, disrupts this rigidity by introducing manageable uncertainty, allowing individuals to learn and grow.

Baggini and his colleagues developed a cognitive theory of play based on valence—the positive or negative feelings associated with emotions. They define play as creating situations with the right amount of surprise (not too difficult or mundane) to resolve uncertainty, foster learning, and promote growth in skills and understanding. This process is grounded in safety and intimacy, allowing individuals to explore their limits while managing the risk involved.

The connection between play and wellbeing lies in the idea that playful disruption can help prevent psychological rigidity. Baggini argues for creating spaces—both physical (like museums) and interpersonal—that encourage this type of exploration, fostering curiosity, openness, and resilience.

In his academic work, Baggini advocates for more playfulness in both the classroom and research environments to promote creativity, exploration, and the ability to tolerate uncertainty. He believes that this can help combat the narrowing effects of rigid motivation structures prevalent in academia, which often prioritize outcomes over the joy of discovery.

Baggini's perspective on love is closely tied to his ideas about openness and curiosity. For him, love is an experience of transcendence towards others and the world, characterized by self-transcendence—a movement beyond oneself towards others. This attitude fosters wonder and awe, which are essential for experiencing the ordinary as extraordinary and engaging with philosophical ideas.

In essence, Baggini's work weaves together embodied cognitive science, philosophy, psychology, and art to explore how playful disruption can promote wellbeing by fostering curiosity, openness, and resilience. He emphasizes the importance of creating safe spaces—physical, interpersonal, and intellectual—that encourage individuals to step beyond their comfort zones and engage with uncertainty in ways that lead to personal growth and a richer understanding of life's mysteries.


### 20250724 - #66 Harmonizing the Dao with analytic philosopher Eric Schwitzgebel

In this conversation between the host and philosopher Eric Schwitzgebel, they discuss various topics including the harmonizing of Tao (Dao) with the weirdness of the world, the nature of consciousness, ethics, and love. Here's a summary of key points:

1. Harmonizing with Dao: The Dao represents everything as processes and patterns rather than a collection of things. It celebrates diversity, including death, disability, and weirdness. This approach contrasts with the Aristotelian ideal of human flourishing that involves being ethically good, creative, productive, and achieving an ideal form.

2. Value Pluralism: Schwitzgebel is a value pluralist who believes there are multiple things with intrinsic value. He argues for embracing various melodies or harmonies within the world without trying to direct or control it, as seen in consequentialist and deontological ethics.

3. Zhuangzi's Philosophy: Schwitzgebel highlights the Chinese philosopher Zhuangzi, known for celebrating weirdness and embracing diverse, unpredictable aspects of life. In contrast to Aristotle's virtue ethics, Zhuangzi views death as part of life's natural cycles and appreciates diversity, including misfits and non-conformists.

4. Conventional vs. Unconventional Ethics: Schwitzgebel acknowledges the appeal of well-ordered societies with roles and rituals (as seen in Confucian thought) while also advocating for a more unpredictable, wild element inspired by Zhuangzi. He suggests a compromise between these views as an ethical ideal.

5. The Weirdness of the World: Schwitzgebel explores this concept through various examples, such as garden snails' mating rituals and their complex behaviors with limited neurons. He questions whether animals like snails have conscious experiences or merely simple sensations.

6. Philosophy of Opening: Schwitzgebel proposes the "philosophy of opening" as an alternative to traditional philosophical approaches that aim to close off possibilities. This approach encourages expanding one's sense of what could be, recognizing multiple, equally plausible options regarding complex questions like consciousness and cosmology.

7. Science Fiction and Philosophy: The conversation touches upon the value of science fiction as a tool for exploring possibilities, expanding our understanding of ethics, and challenging assumptions about consciousness and reality. Schwitzgebel cites Kazuo Ishiguro's "Quara and the Sun" as an example of exploring perspectives beyond human experience.

8. Ethical Studies: Schwitzgebel discusses his research on moral behavior among ethicists, revealing that their actions do not differ significantly from those of other professors. This finding challenges assumptions about the positive impact of studying ethics on one's ethical conduct.

9. Love and Harmony: The conversation concludes by exploring love as an aspect of harmonizing with the world, focusing on conjugal love and marriage vows. Schwitzgebel suggests that the paradox in marriage lies in promising to love someone forever while acknowledging potential changes over time. Embracing this uncertainty highlights a beautiful way to think about love in the context of the weirdness of the world.

The conversation emphasizes the importance of recognizing diverse aspects of reality, questioning assumptions, and embracing the unpredictable nature of existence as a means of enriching our understanding and appreciation for life's complexity.


### 20250802 - #67： Autonomy, Autopoiesis & the Enactive Approach with philosopher Ezequiel Di Paolo (Mirko Prokop)

In this podcast episode, philosopher Zekiel DiPaolo discusses his work on inactive cognition with host Andrea and guest Mirko Prokop. Inactive cognition is a framework that explores how our bodily movements and interactions with the environment are already cognitive processes, challenging traditional computational theories of mind that often view the mind as confined to the brain.

Zekiel introduces the concept of autopoiesis, proposed by Francisco Varela, Humberto Maturana, and Evan Thompson in their 1974 book "Autopoiesis and Cognition." Autopoiesis refers to a self-producing or self-maintaining system, which is a key concept in understanding living organisms. This idea was further developed by Varela in his 1991 book "The Embodied Mind," co-authored with Evan Thompson and Eleanor Rosch.

Zekiel explains the evolution of autonomy from autopoiesis, which is a more domain-specific concept focusing on living cells. Autonomy, in Varela's sense, is a generalization that can be applied to various systems, not just biological ones. He highlights the importance of operational closure or organizational closure, where a system's processes sustain and regenerate its structure without being closed off from the environment.

The conversation then delves into the relationship between autonomy and the environment in inactive cognition, focusing on the idea of "bringing forth the world." This notion emphasizes that organisms do not merely perceive an independent world but actively create it through their interactions with their surroundings. The discussion also touches upon the dialectical tension between self-production and self-distinction, as well as the relational nature of autonomy in the context of social and intersubjective dimensions.

Zekiel further discusses his work on participatory sense-making alongside Hannah Jäger. This approach aims to understand how individuals co-create meaning during interactions through a tension between participants' autonomies and the autonomous dynamics of their encounters. By exploring this primordial tension, they propose that social agency emerges from increasingly complex forms of participatory sense-making, including gestures, dialogue, and reflection.

The conversation also touches on themes like love and philosophy, exploring how the openness required for successful communication and understanding can be seen as an extension of love's existential structure: dependence, openness, and tension. Zekiel discusses authenticity in relation to becoming, emphasizing that it is not static but involves a relational process where individuals continuously negotiate their forms of life within complex relations with others and the environment.

Finally, Zekiel addresses how one navigates academic or reflective contexts while staying true to personal ideas, especially when critically engaging with other scholars' work. He advises maintaining sincerity in one's questions, focusing on directly relevant information, and remaining attuned to emotional responses that indicate the alignment of new knowledge with existing interests or misalignment, signaling that further exploration may be necessary.

Overall, this episode provides an in-depth exploration of Zekiel DiPaolo's work on inactive cognition, autonomy, and participatory sense-making, highlighting their connections to broader philosophical themes such as love, authenticity, and the nature of the mind.


### 20250803 - Weirdness, the Dao, and Whether Trees have Experiences with philosopher Eric Schwitzgebel

In this conversation between the host and philosopher Eric Schwitzgebel, they discuss various topics, including the concept of harmonizing with the Tao (Dao) from classical Chinese philosophy, value pluralism, and the weirdness of the world. Here's a detailed summary and explanation:

1. **Harmonizing with the Dao**: The Dao is an all-encompassing concept in classical Chinese philosophy that refers to everything as processes and patterns rather than static things. It includes the natural world, human interactions, and societal dynamics. Harmonizing with the Dao means aligning oneself with these processes and patterns.

2. **Value Pluralism**: Eric Schwitzgebel identifies as a value pluralist, believing that there are multiple values or melodies in the world, each contributing to the overall harmony (Tao). This contrasts with monistic views (e.g., consequentialism) that aim for a single, optimal outcome. In the value pluralist view, different individuals and entities can harmonize with the Tao through their unique contributions without needing to conform to a singular ideal.

3. **Weirdness of the World**: Schwitzgebel appreciates the weirdness and diversity inherent in nature, arguing that understanding and embracing this weirdness enriches our view of the world. He uses examples like garden snails' mating rituals to illustrate this point.

4. **Consciousness**: The conversation also touches on consciousness and its limits, with Schwitzgebel leaning toward a more inclusive view that acknowledges potential consciousness in various life forms. He distinguishes between sensation (responding to stimuli) and conscious experience, allowing for the possibility of non-human consciousness without equating it to human self-awareness.

5. **Philosophy and Science Fiction**: Schwitzgebel values both philosophy and science fiction as tools for exploring possibilities and challenging our presuppositions about the world. He sees them as complementary ways of expanding our understanding of what could exist or be true.

6. **Ethics and Moral Behavior**: Schwitzgebel discusses his research on moral behavior among ethicists, revealing that they don't behave significantly differently from non-ethicist professionals. This finding challenges the notion that studying ethics leads to better real-world moral behavior. Instead, he proposes a forgiving ethic that acknowledges our limitations and encourages harmonizing with the Tao through individual choices and life projects.

7. **Love**: Schwitzgebel explores love as an extension of one's care and concern for others, emphasizing the paradoxical nature of promising lifelong love in marriage vows while acknowledging the contingencies and uncertainties inherent in human relationships. He suggests that embracing the weirdness of life can make such commitments more beautiful and meaningful.

Throughout the conversation, Schwitzgebel emphasizes the importance of appreciating the complexity and diversity of the world, encouraging listeners to adopt a value pluralist perspective that celebrates multiple melodies contributing to the overall harmony (Tao) of existence.


### 20250807 - Brains are not Computers & Mind is Bodily  #diary #philosophy #care RD12

Andrea begins her research diary by addressing the concern that her discussions about philosophy, science, and technology might be inaccessible to those without a similar educational background. She emphasizes that this is not about having specific degrees or knowledge, but rather about sharing care and understanding of various aspects of our existence.

She then delves into the topic of cognition and mind, challenging the common assumption that the brain is the sole seat of our mind or intelligence – a concept often likened to a computer. Andrea argues that this view is incomplete and misleading. Cognition, she posits, is not confined to the brain but extends throughout the entire body as part of a complex nervous system.

This bodily cognition or embodied cognition perspective suggests that our minds are not isolated entities within our heads but are intricately connected to our physical selves and environments. This means our senses, emotions, and intellectual processes are all part of a continuous, ongoing interaction between our bodies and the world around us. 

Andrea highlights several key points:

1. **Cognition is Ongoing**: It's not something that happens only within the brain but is a dynamic process involving the entire body. This includes our senses, emotions, and even our physical movements, which all contribute to our understanding and interaction with the world.

2. **Relational Nature of Mind**: Our minds are not separate from others or the environment. When people interact, they create a sort of "third entity," emphasizing the collaborative and relational aspect of cognition. 

3. **Embodied Cognition**: This refers to the idea that our bodies and environments play a crucial role in shaping our thoughts and understanding. The brain is indeed vital, but it's not the sole determinant of mind or intelligence. 

4. **Ecological Perspective**: From this viewpoint, cognition isn't something that happens independently within us; instead, it's part of a larger ecological system involving our bodies, other beings, and our surroundings. This perspective encourages us to see cognition as an ongoing, participatory process of sense-making with the world.

5. **Care as Central Theme**: Understanding mind as embodied also underscores the importance of care – caring for oneself, being cared for by others and the environment, and extending that care to all living beings. This care is not sentimental or superficial; it's about recognizing our interconnectedness and mutual dependence.

6. **Critique of Computer Metaphor**: While computers and technology can provide valuable insights into cognitive processes, they are not accurate representations of the human mind. Our brains are bodily organs, and our experiences cannot be reduced to data or information processed by a machine.

Andrea concludes by expressing her hope that this perspective on embodied cognition can help us develop better technologies and understandings of ourselves, fostering a more compassionate and interconnected worldview. She encourages her audience to find something worth caring about – be it a plant, animal, person, or themselves – and to recognize their inherent worthiness of care.


### 20250809 - Synapses of Life： Public Conversation from August of 2024 with Shoma Chaudhury & Andrea Hiott

### 20250811 - Bringing Forth Worlds with enactive philosopher Ezequiel Di Paolo and Mirko Prokop

In this conversation, Sekiel discusses his intellectual journey and the evolution of Francisco Varela's ideas, particularly focusing on the concept of autonomy and its relation to the Inactive Approach. Here is a detailed summary:

1. **Francisco Varela's "Principles of Biological Autonomy"**: The book, originally published in 1979 but written earlier, is a seminal work by Varela that introduces the concept of autopoiesis – self-production or self-distinction of living organisms. Sekiel describes his encounter with this book during his PhD at the University of Sussex and how it inspired him despite its complex language.

2. **Autonomy**: Autonomy, as conceptualized by Varela, is a generalization of autopoiesis. It refers to systems that self-produce and maintain their organization while interacting with an environment. The key aspect is operational closure or organisational closure – the system's ability to reinstate its network through its processes, without being closed off from the environment.

3. **Autonomy and Bringing Forth the World**: Sekiel explains how Varela's ideas of autonomy relate to the Inactive Approach's concept of "bringing forth the world." This notion suggests that organisms are constantly acting in their environment, changing it while also adapting to it. The environment and the embodied agent mutually define each other ongoingly, challenging traditional cognitive science perspectives on perception and representation.

4. **Embodied Mind (1991)**: In this foundational text of the Active Approach, autopoiesis isn't explicitly mentioned. However, it's clear that Varela was developing related ideas. The work emphasizes the importance of incorporating lived experience and experiential perspectives in understanding cognition, moving away from computational metaphors dominant in cognitive science at the time.

5. **Inactive Approach**: This emerged later as an extension of Varela's ideas. It posits that organisms are not just passively receiving sensory data but actively engaging with and bringing forth their world through continuous self-production and self-distinction. The Inactive Approach stresses the dialectical tension between self-production (autopoiesis) and self-distinction, as well as the relational nature of autonomy in various dimensions – sensory motor, interactional, linguistic, social, and existential.

6. **Dimensions of Embodiment**: Sekiel discusses how the Inactive Approach suggests multiple dimensions of embodiment: organic (physiological), sensorimotor (sensory-motor agency), and intersubjective (social interaction). Each dimension is entangled with others, mutually shaping the individual's becoming.

7. **Participatory Sense-making**: In collaboration with Hannah Jäger, Sekiel developed the concept of participatory sense-making to bridge autonomy, embodiment, and social dimensions. It posits that social interactions have their own autonomous dynamics and that participants maintain their individual autonomy within these interactions while also engaging in a primordial tension with each other.

8. **Love and Authenticity**: The conversation then shifts to the connection between love, communication, and authenticity. Sekiel suggests that genuine connections require openness to others, constant negotiation, and a critical reflection on one's own positioning within complex relations. Authenticity, in this context, is relational and historical rather than static or individualistic.

9. **Navigating Academic Inquiry**: Sekiel shares personal strategies for staying true to his intellectual pursuits amidst the pressure to conform to academic standards. He emphasizes the importance of maintaining one's core questions, being discerning about information relevance, and fostering a genuine connection with one's research topic.

10. **Ongoing Research Interests**: Sekiel mentions his ongoing interest in exploring an appropriate ontology for understanding the nature of reality underpinning the Inactive Approach, particularly as it relates to environmental emergencies, politics, and scientific issues like quantum mechanics.

The conversation concludes with both participants reflecting on the breadth covered and expressing satisfaction in their discussion.

Overall, Hypatia's conversation with the unnamed philosopher explores love as a multifaceted mode of knowing that involves risk, discomfort, and the ongoing process of learning and adapting to others. ### 20250824 - Loving and Knowing with philosopher Hanne De Jaegher

Hanna de Jager, a philosopher of cognitive science, discusses her unique approach to understanding thinking and knowledge through the lens of loving relationships. She recalls her childhood experiences with her parents, who encouraged her to think deeply about phenomena from a young age, influenced by their professions as psychologist and literacy advocate. This upbringing instilled in her an appreciation for systems thinking and the relational nature of knowing.

De Jager's work emphasizes the interconnectedness of loving and knowing, arguing that they share a core or entail each other. She challenges the traditional distinction between feelings and thinking, suggesting that both are part of the same sense-making process as living beings. This perspective stems from her ontology, which views beings as relational entities engaging with their environment to meet basic needs and maintain life.

Throughout her career, de Jager noticed patterns in academic literature that didn't align with her lived experiences or the way she understood social relationships. Specifically, she found that prevailing theories of autism and social cognition overlooked the importance of embodiment and rhythmic behavior in autistic individuals' interactions with the world. This led her to develop participatory sense-making as a bridge between cognitive science and sociology, allowing for a more comprehensive understanding of intersubjectivity.

De Jager's motivation for her work is multifaceted. While success and recognition are factors, she also feels driven by a desire to understand and improve the world around her. She acknowledges the tension between the external representations valued in academia (e.g., citations, publications) and the more profound, lived experiences that truly nourish our souls. She believes that holding this tension is crucial for personal well-being and intellectual freedom.

In her personal life, de Jager experienced precarity and illness, which led to a reevaluation of her career path. Now independent from traditional academic institutions, she continues to explore the themes of loving and knowing, participatory sense-making, and the importance of holding spaces for authentic connections and intellectual freedom. De Jager advocates for universal basic income as a means to alleviate precarity, allowing individuals to pursue meaningful work without financial constraints.

Throughout her journey, de Jager has faced pushback for her unconventional approach, particularly when introducing the concept of love into philosophical discourse. She has learned to navigate this resistance by staying committed to her work and ignoring questions that don't align with her framework. De Jager emphasizes the value of finding like-minded dialogue partners who share a common language, allowing for the creation and evolution of new ways of understanding cognition and human relationships.


The conversation revolves around several interconnected themes, primarily centered on participatory sense-making, dialogue, inner work, and love. Here's a detailed summary:

1. **Participatory Sense-Making and Dialogue**: The speaker emphasizes the importance of each individual or group staying with their way of knowing or individuality to allow for later interaction and new understanding. This principle is crucial for participatory sense-making, where different ways of knowing (like language, computational approaches, or brain processing) can eventually dialogue with each other.

This approach fosters active listening, respect for individuality, and self-maintenance - principles the speaker values in both personal relationships and academic discourse.

3. **Holding Space**: The metaphor of 'holding space' is used to describe creating an environment where others can feel safe and seen without judgment. This concept is extended beyond interpersonal relationships to include academia, suggesting that a non-binary, multiplicity-embracing approach can foster richer understandings.

This inner work is essential to prevent dialogue from becoming superficial or detrimental to individual growth.

It also implies embracing the risk and thrill of this intimate connection.

### 20250825 - #69 The philosophy of Bioregionalism with Félix de Rosen

In this conversation between Andrea and Felix de Rozen, they discuss bioregionalism, a philosophy that organizes human societies based on unique qualities of place, emphasizing the importance of aligning economic activity, governance, and ecological stewardship with natural systems.

Felix's background is in landscape architecture, gardening, and environmental activism. Raised between French and American cultures, Felix felt alienated from both, leading him to seek a deeper connection with nature and the living world. His journey towards this understanding began during his early 20s when he faced chronic health issues that forced him to learn self-care and eventually led him to appreciate the natural world as a source of healing and inspiration.

Felix's education at Harvard in political science didn't initially align with his growing interest in nature, beauty, and people. It wasn't until he started studying landscape architecture that he found a practical discipline rooted in both philosophy and hands-on skills. This led him to focus on the interconnectedness of economy, ecology, culture, politics, and spirituality.

Bioregionalism, as Felix describes it, is an applied philosophy that advocates for organizing human societies according to a place's unique characteristics – climate, topography, flora, fauna, and cultural aspects like art and language. This approach contrasts with traditional political boundaries, which often prioritize industrial activities over local ecological needs.

Felix emphasizes the importance of worldview as a powerful lever in systems thinking. He argues that understanding our place within complex, interconnected systems is crucial for addressing contemporary environmental challenges. In his work with bioregionalism and polyculture, Felix seeks to bridge divides by highlighting shared human desires like progress, community well-being, ecological stewardship, local identity, and relocalization.

Felix believes that the left-right political divide is increasingly outdated and that focusing on "bottom" (individual) and "up" (collective) perspectives can foster alliances across traditional divides. He highlights bioregionalism's potential to unite people in recognizing their interdependence with the earth, thus combating feelings of alienation.

Felix's organization, BioFi Project (biofi.earth), works on bioregional projects internationally, including collaborations across national borders and with indigenous communities. He encourages people to embrace their agency in shaping regenerative social, economic, and political systems aligned with the earth.

The conversation also touches upon themes of love as a recognition of our deep interdependence with nature, emphasizing that human beings are creatures of the earth, deeply connected to living landscapes in an ancient, evolutionary way. Bioregionalism, Felix suggests, is more than just a philosophy; it's a reawakening to our place within these complex systems.

### 20250902 - Philosophy of Bioregionalism with Félix de Rosen and Andrea Hiott

Felix de Rozen, a French-born individual raised in the United States, is a landscape architect, gardener, and writer who focuses on bioregionalism and sustainable living. He discusses his unique upbringing between two cultures—French and American—and how this influenced his understanding of place and community.

De Rozen's relationship with nature evolved over time, particularly after experiencing physical health issues in his 20s that led him to learn about healthy living, nutrition, and self-care. This journey eventually brought him closer to the natural world. He emphasizes the interconnectedness between humans and the living world, which is a core principle of animistic and shamanic cultures as well as present in monotheistic religions.

As a child growing up in suburban American culture, de Rozen felt disconnected from his surroundings due to the car-focused lifestyle, efficient land use, and lack of a larger vision aligned with the complexity of life around him. He describes this feeling as alienation, which he later realized stemmed from not identifying with the cultures around him on either side of the Atlantic.

De Rozen studied political science at Harvard University but found it was not an ideal environment for his emotional growth. After a brief marketing career, he dived into climate literature in 2016 and decided to return to school, focusing on landscape architecture at UC Berkeley. He chose this discipline because it combined practical skills with philosophical, spiritual, and conceptual aspects.

Bioregionalism is a philosophy that suggests organizing human societies according to the unique qualities of place, aligning economic activities and natural resource stewardship with local ecological systems and cultures. De Rozen's work involves helping communities develop tools for social and ecological regeneration while welcoming financial capital into these projects.

His book, "The Purpose of a Garden," explores the garden as a complex system with embedded worldviews and values, such as wonder, design, habitat, and more. De Rozen aims to shift perceptions of gardens from isolated nature patches in front or backyards to a way of being that exists everywhere.

In his work, de Rozen advocates for systems thinking and staying with the trouble—embracing uncertainty, difficulty, and challenges—to foster honest relationships with reality. He sees bioregionalism as a social process of bringing citizens together to build common visions for their landscapes, addressing various contradictions and complexities within human societies.

De Rozen emphasizes the importance of shared human desires and wants that transcend differences, particularly in political distinctions like left and right. Bioregionalism can serve as a bridge between these groups by focusing on relocalization, community well-being, ecology, and local identity.

Throughout the conversation, de Rozen touches upon themes of love and connection to place, asserting that humans are creatures of the earth with an evolutionary relationship to living landscapes. By reconnecting with our roots in bioregionalism, we can find healing, purpose, and community in our everyday lives.

To learn more about de Rozen's work, visit his organization's website at biofi.earth or explore his artistic design site polycultura.com.

### 20250923 - #72 Rethinking Cognition： Posthumanism to Planetary Ecology with Professor Katherine Hayles

In this conversation between the host and Kate Hayles, a distinguished research professor at the University of California, Los Angeles (UCLA) and Duke University, they discuss various themes related to cognition, consciousness, human identity, and our relationship with technology. Here's a detailed summary:

1. Rethinking Cognition:
   Kate Hayles argues that we need to reconsider the traditional understanding of cognition. Instead of viewing humans as the pinnacle of cognitive abilities, she suggests that all living organisms possess cognitive capabilities, and some non-living entities (like AI) also exhibit cognitive behaviors. This perspective challenges anthropocentrism, which positions humans as superior to other species, contributing to environmental crises.

2. The Integrated Cognitive Framework:
   Hayles proposes the "Integrated Cognitive Framework," a model that places human cognition in relation to various forms of cognitive abilities found in nature and artificial intelligence. By acknowledging the existence of non-human cognitive capacities, this framework aims to decenter anthropocentrism and broaden our understanding of what it means to be cognitive.

3. Decoupling Consciousness and Cognition:
   Hayles emphasizes that consciousness is just one aspect of a broader spectrum of cognitive processes, including non-conscious cognition. Non-conscious cognition involves rapid information processing beneath the level of awareness, helping filter and prioritize stimuli for conscious attention. This separation allows for the recognition that organisms like bacteria or AI can engage in cognitive activities without necessarily possessing human-like conscious experiences.

4. Meaning and Cognition:
   Hayles redefines meaning as a fundamental aspect of cognitive processes across all living beings, from microorganisms to humans. This meaning serves the organism's survival, even if it doesn't involve complex narratives or self-awareness as we understand them. By expanding the concept of meaning beyond human experiences, Hayles opens up possibilities for exploring cognition in non-human entities and AI systems.

5. Umwelt and Embodiment:
   To understand different organisms' experiences, Hayles introduces the concept of umwelt – the unique world constructed by each species based on its sensory capabilities. In humans, our umwelt involves a rich set of perceptions, while non-human entities have distinct umweten shaped by their biological and technological embodiments. For AI systems, Hayles proposes the idea of "conceptual umwelt," an information-based environment constructed through human-authored text.

6. The Post-Human Book:
   Contrary to common interpretations, Hayles' book "How We Became Posthuman" does not advocate for embracing posthumanism but rather presents a historical analysis of how humans have been extending their cognitive abilities through technologies like AI. She argues that this symbiotic relationship with technology fundamentally changes our understanding of what it means to be human, necessitating new ethical and philosophical frameworks.

7. Human Identity and Care:
   Hayles discusses the evolving nature of human identity, emphasizing our historical inclination to redefine ourselves in response to new circumstances (e.g., the agricultural, industrial revolutions). She suggests that care – for oneself, others, and the environment – should expand beyond narrow boundaries of kinship or nationhood to encompass a broader planetary consciousness. This expanded sense of care is crucial for human survival in an era of unprecedented global challenges, including climate change and emerging AI technologies.

8. Hope and Education:
   Hayles expresses hope for humanity's future but acknowledges the urgent need for accurate information and critical thinking to overcome current challenges. She believes that education can foster enlightened visions, enabling individuals to imagine and work toward positive futures amidst uncertainty. By understanding our complex relationship with technology and each other on a planetary scale, we might navigate the perils of the modern age more effectively.

In essence, Hayles' work encourages a reevaluation of human exceptionalism, emphasizing the ubiquity of cognition across living and artificial entities. By broadening our understanding of meaning, cognition, and embodiment, she aims to foster new ethical frameworks for engaging with AI technologies and addressing global challenges collaboratively – a call for collective care grounded in scientific literacy and planetary consciousness.

### 20250930 - Dialectic, Democracy & the Phenomenology of Double Consciousness with Lucius Outlaw Jr.

The text provided is an excerpt from a conversation between the host and guest Lucius Outlaw, discussing his upbringing in a segregated town in Mississippi during the mid-20th century and how it shaped his philosophical journey. Here's a detailed summary:

1. **Childhood and Upbringing:**
   - Lucius Outlaw was born and raised in Stavrid, MS, a small town with racial segregation. Despite physical boundaries like streets separating black and white neighborhoods, the proximity of white people's homes to his was strikingly close.
   - His parents were his father, a janitor at a large white Baptist church, and his mother, a maid for white families. They emphasized respectability, teaching him to navigate racial norms and protocols of segregation.

2. **Challenges to Segregation:**
   - Despite the rules of segregation, Lucius experienced contradictions early on, such as his father working in a predominantly white church while facing racism from parishioners. These experiences led him to question the system's logic and fairness.

3. **Education and Religious Struggles:**
   - Lucius initially planned to study religion and become a minister but shifted his focus to philosophy after encountering existentialist thinkers like Sartre during college. His mother, deeply rooted in her faith, was distraught by this decision.
   - He had a falling out with his mother when he decided not to pursue religious studies, instead choosing to study philosophy and become a university professor. This decision was influenced by his exposure to diverse intellectual perspectives at Fisk University and the nonviolent direct action movement led by Martin Luther King Jr.

4. **Fisk University:**
   - Lucius attended Fisk University, an interracial institution known for its academic rigor and demanding standards. He excelled acadically and became involved in student politics, eventually becoming president of the student council.
   - The university's emphasis on intellectual growth resonated with him, as did the presence of esteemed professors like John Oliver Killings, a prominent black writer who taught writing at Fisk.

5. **Black Power Movement and Personal Evolution:**
   - In 1967, during his senior year, the Black Power movement was gaining traction both nationally and on campus. This led to student activism, including petitioning for a Student Nonviolent Coordinating Committee (SNCC) chapter at Fisk.
   - Lucius, as president of the student council, found himself in the middle of this movement, negotiating with both the administration and SNCC members. The experience pushed him to reevaluate his commitment to "student power" versus other forms of activism.

### 20251003 - Bacteria to AI： our Planetary Cognitive Ecology with professor N. Katherine Hayles

Katherine Hayles is a distinguished research professor at the University of California, Los Angeles (UCLA) and James B. Duke Professor at Duke University. She has written extensively on literature, science, technology, and their intersections in the 20th and 21st centuries. Her work challenges anthropocentric views by proposing a new framework for understanding cognition called the Integrated Cognitive Framework (ICF).

The ICF aims to reconfigure human cognition, placing it in relation to other biological cognitions and synthetic intelligences like AI. Hayles argues that all living organisms have cognitive capabilities, not just humans, and she includes forms of artificial intelligence within this framework. This perspective counters the traditional view that places human cognition at the apex of a pyramidal hierarchy of cognitive abilities.

One central idea in Hayles' work is the decoupling of consciousness and cognition. Consciousness, she posits, is a subset of cognition rather than its whole. It's one form of cognition that humans experience subjectively as awareness of our thoughts. Non-conscious cognition, on the other hand, operates below this level and processes information much faster without our conscious awareness.

Hayles emphasizes the significance of non-conscious processing in understanding cognition, arguing that it's crucial for protecting consciousness from an overload of sensory stimuli. She uses examples like the gorilla suit video to illustrate how our brains selectively attend to information, formulating narratives to make sense of the world.

The concept of umwelt, borrowed from Jakob von Uexküll, plays a vital role in Hayles' work. Umwelt refers to each organism's unique sensory and experiential world. This term helps explain how different species perceive their environment differently, even though they share the same physical space.

Hayles applies the idea of umwelt to both biological organisms and artificial intelligences (AI). For non-conscious organisms like bacteria or plants, the umwelt is primarily constructed through sensory information. In contrast, AI systems have a conceptual umwelt derived from human-authored texts ingested by the system.

Hayles' work also touches on symbiosis, emphasizing how our relationship with technology has evolved into a form of symbiosis that reshapes human cognition and culture. Her book "How We Became Posthuman: Virtual Bodies in Literature, Cybernetics, and Informatics" (1999) explores these themes within the context of cybernetics, reflexivity, and autopoiesis—concepts central to understanding how boundaries between individuals and their environments are constructed.

Her latest book, "Bacteria to AI," further develops her ideas on cognitive ecology and relationality. It explores the implications of AI in a planetary context while acknowledging both its potential benefits and risks. Hayles advocates for an enlarged vision of care that transcends individual or national boundaries, emphasizing the importance of understanding our interconnectedness with all life forms on Earth.

Ultimately, Katherine Hayles' work encourages a rethinking of human cognition and identity in light of technological advancements, pushing us to consider broader perspectives that include non-human entities and artificial intelligences within our collective understanding of cognition.

### 20251010 - E-cognition： the Embodied Extended Enactive En-sensed Mindset

The text provided is a reflection on the concept of 4E Cognition (Embodied, Embedded, Extended, Active), a shift from traditional cognitive science's view of thinking as solely occurring within the brain to understanding it as a more expansive, multifaceted process. 

1. **Embodied**: The author emphasizes that the body is not separate from the mind but an integral part of it. Cognition isn't just happening in our heads; it's happening throughout our bodies. For instance, when navigating a space, the hippocampus (a brain structure associated with memory and spatial navigation) plays a crucial role. Even before conscious decision-making, our bodies are already making cognitive processes like turning at a corner. 

2. **Embedded**: This concept underscores that our cognition is deeply influenced by the environment in which we find ourselves. The same map read at home and in an unfamiliar city can yield different cognitive experiences due to varying contexts. Our cognition isn't isolated but shaped by the parameters set by our surroundings, including social interactions, technological tools, and physical spaces.

3. **Extended**: This E refers to how external tools and technologies can extend our cognitive abilities. For example, a GPS on a smartphone allows us to navigate complex urban environments without needing to memorize every street or detail. Even a simple tool like a cane for the visually impaired becomes an extension of their sensory-motor system after prolonged use, providing feedback about the environment through touch and proprioception (the sense of body position).

4. **Active/Inactive**: This term challenges the notion that cognition is something we possess or control passively. Instead, it's an ongoing process of relational sense-making, a participatory activity where we constantly interact with our environment, including ourselves and our thoughts. It's not about having knowledge or love but making these through continuous engagement with the world around us.

5. **Ecological**: This E highlights that cognition isn't confined to individual minds; it's an emergent property of complex, nested systems involving bodies, social groups, technologies, and natural environments. It’s a planetary process where every thought or action is influenced by and contributes to this vast web of relationships. 

The author concludes by noting that these E's—Embodied, Embedded, Extended, Active/Inactive, and Ecological—are different ways to understand one continuous process of cognition rather than separate entities. They encourage us to see ourselves as part of this intricate web of relationships, where every connection we make can expand our cognitive potential and alter shared patterns guiding us collectively. This holistic view of cognition is central to understanding love and philosophy, emphasizing that by attending to these connections, we engage in acts of both loving and knowing.


### 20251014 - Hippocampus Love： The Neural Representation Debate & Cognitive Maps with Lynn Nadel (BONUS EPISODE)

In this conversation between Andrea Hyatt and Lynn Nadelle, they discuss the complex topic of representation in cognitive neuroscience. They explore the question of how the brain represents the world and guides behavior, focusing on the mind-body problem and the nature of consciousness.

1. **Brain as a Relational Device**: Lynn Nadelle emphasizes that the brain is fundamentally a relational device, meaning everything it does involves relationships between inputs from various sources. This includes associations, which are not unique to the hippocampus but are a fundamental aspect of how all parts of the brain function.

2. **Tolman-Eichenbaum Model**: They discuss the Tolman-Eichenbaum model, which posits that the hippocampus is a relational memory device that forms associations between different pieces of information. However, both Lynn and Andrea argue that this idea is vacuous because it fails to acknowledge that every part of the brain processes relational information. They believe that associative learning is not a special ability of the hippocampus but a universal feature of brain function.

3. **Cognitive Maps**: The conversation touches on Edvard Tolman's concept of cognitive maps, which suggests that animals form mental representations of their environment to navigate and predict outcomes. Andrea proposes that this idea can be extended beyond physical spaces to include abstract knowledge spaces, emphasizing the commonalities in how we process regularities across different domains.

4. **Association vs Non-Associative Learning**: Lynn clarifies that the distinction between associative and non-associative learning is misleading because all brain processes involve relating inputs from multiple sources. Habituation and sensitization, often categorized as non-associative memory, are actually forms of association.

5. **Brain as a Continuous Process**: Both discuss how understanding the brain as a continuous process that extracts statistical regularities from sensory input can provide insights into cognition and behavior. They suggest that a comprehensive theory should reconcile internal (brain structure) and external (world experience) factors, emphasizing the importance of reentrant, recursive interactions between the organism and its environment.

6. **The Role of Association**: Lynn Nadelle suggests that association might play a crucial role in understanding how brain networks acquire meaning. He proposes viewing the hippocampus as an associative device within a broader framework that acknowledges the relational nature of all brain processes.

7. **The Middle Ground**: They agree that a complete understanding of representation in the brain requires finding a middle ground between internal (innate) and external (experiential) factors. This middle ground should account for how pre-wired structures interact with sensory inputs and experiences to shape cognitive processes and behavior.

8. **Emergence of Grid Cells**: They mention the emergence of grid cells as a result of experience refining an initial, pre-wired structure (the taurus). This highlights how ontogenetic (developmental) factors interact with phylogenetic (evolutionary) ones to shape neural representations.

9. **Future Research**: Andrea expresses hope that ongoing research, particularly from the Mosers' lab, will help bridge these gaps and provide a more nuanced understanding of how the brain represents the world, incorporating both internal structures and external experiences.

This conversation underscores the complexity of representation in cognitive neuroscience and the need for a framework that reconciles internal brain structures with external experiences to fully grasp how the brain guides behavior and creates our sense of self in the world.

### 20251024 - Radical Embodied Memory & Navigation： Communicating with the world is remembering with it

Andrea's diary entry discusses her recently published paper titled "Radical Embodied Relation at any Scale from Navigating to Remembering." The paper challenges conventional neuroscience and philosophical views on memory and thought, proposing a novel perspective grounded in radical embodied cognition.

The core idea is that our memories aren't stored as discrete entities within the brain, like files in a cabinet or data on a hard drive, but rather are communicative processes arising from interactions between the body and environment. This concept is illustrated through various analogies, including the polo shirt collar example – something we're familiar with yet rarely ponder its origin and purpose.

Andrea highlights that for over a century, neuroscientists have sought "engrams," or traces of memories, within the brain. However, despite numerous brain scans and fMRI images, definitive evidence remains elusive. The paper argues this is because we've been misled by our language and familiar assumptions, mistaking representations for the actual processes they depict – much like confusing weather maps with the actual weather.

Central to her argument is the hippocampus, a seahorse-shaped brain region long associated with memory. Researchers like John O'Keefe have discovered that cells within this area fire based on our spatial navigation patterns in the environment. These cells, called place cells and grid cells, form a "brain's GPS system."

Andrea suggests that memory and navigation might not be separate processes as previously thought but rather two sides of the same cognitive coin – both relying on the hippocampus' neural machinery for their functioning. This implies our memories aren't stored like files in a brain filing cabinet; instead, they're emergent properties of ongoing interactions between the body and environment.

To further elaborate:

1. **Radical Embodied Cognition**: Andrea references radical embodied cognition – an interdisciplinary approach that argues for understanding cognition (including memory and thought) as deeply rooted in the body's interactions with its environment, rather than residing within the brain alone.

2. **Weather Map Analogy**: She uses a weather map analogy to explain how we often mistake representations (like fMRI images or memory traces) for the actual processes they depict. Just as a weather map isn't the weather itself but a useful way to communicate about it, our brain scans and memory concepts may be representations of cognitive processes rather than the processes themselves.

3. **Hippocampus' Role**: The hippocampus plays a crucial role in both navigation and memory according to recent research. Place cells fire when we're at specific locations, while grid cells create a hexagonal grid pattern reflecting our spatial orientation. These findings suggest that the same neural mechanisms underlie both navigating physical space and mental "space" or memory recall.

4. **Memory as Process, Not Storage**: Andrea emphasizes that memories aren't stored entities but emergent properties of ongoing body-environment interactions. When we recall a memory (e.g., where we parked our car), it's less like retrieving a file and more like navigating through an internal mental landscape using the same neural machinery involved in physical navigation.

In conclusion, Andrea's paper proposes a shift from viewing memories as stored entities within the brain to understanding them as dynamic, communicative processes arising from our bodily interactions with the world. This radical embodied perspective challenges long-held assumptions about the nature of memory and cognition, opening up new avenues for research and self-understanding.

9. The need for ongoing dialogue and collaboration across various fields to cultivate a more holistic understanding of learning and human development.

Tim and Andrea also touch upon the implications of artificial intelligence (AI) in education, acknowledging that AI could both challenge and augment existing educational paradigms. They discuss the potential for new technologies to support alternative ways of sensing, engaging with, and understanding the world, emphasizing the importance of nurturing care, presence, and non-judgmental awareness in these emerging spaces.
