### 20251111 - #75 Hyperscanning & Human Connection： Synchronization and the Third Brain with Biologist Nicolás ...

In this conversation between Andrea Hyatt and Nicolas Heinrichs, the topic revolves around hyperscanning, a neuroscientific technique used to record brain activity from two individuals simultaneously during interaction. Hyperscanning aims to overcome limitations in social neuroscience by moving beyond simple synchrony, focusing on more sophisticated mappings of brain activity patterns that change as individuals talk and move.

Heinrichs describes how hyperscanning experiments can be conducted using different methodologies such as EEG (electroencephalography) or fMRI (functional magnetic resonance imaging). He emphasizes the importance of enhancing ecological validity, i.e., making experiments more representative of daily life interactions. This could involve allowing participants to see each other, touch, or engage in haptics during the task.

The conversation also delves into the concept of synchrony in hyperscanning. Interbrain synchrony (IBS) refers to co-occurrences of activations in specific brain regions during interactions. Previous studies have shown that IBS is meaningful for cognitive and interactive phenomena like enhancing comprehension between interacting parties, indicating a neurobiological substrate for these processes.

However, Heinrichs aims to move beyond this simple understanding of synchrony by exploring deeper patterns in brain activity using geometry. This involves detecting changes in activations over time and space during interactions, considering misunderstandings, repairs, and other aspects of conversation that contribute to the complex nature of social exchanges.

Heinrichs also discusses the idea of a "third entity" or constellation cognition, which refers to the emergence of a shared mental state between individuals during interactions. This concept challenges traditional views of synchrony as perfect mirroring and instead focuses on the dynamic, ever-changing nature of interpersonal connections.

The conversation further touches upon the philosophical aspects of hyperscanning, including the importance of holding paradoxes and embracing the inherent complexity and messiness of human interactions. This perspective aligns with care as a motivating force behind his research, pushing for a more nuanced understanding of social phenomena that can ultimately benefit mental health interventions and other aspects of society.

The discussion concludes by mentioning Heinrichs' collaboration with Elena Koufari and Hannah DeJager to extend the Prisma methodology into hyperscanning, aiming to create a neurophenomenologically grounded science of participatory sense-making. This approach seeks to bridge the gap between discursive practices and intimately connected neurophysiological studies, offering a more realistic account of human interactions that could ultimately help individuals experiencing mental health challenges or social inequality-related issues like chronic inflammation of the brain.

### 20251114 - Kaleidoscopic or Constellatory Cognition： Rethinking Brain Models & Experiments with Andrea Hiott

Andrea Hyatt's paper, "Radical Embodied Relation at Any Scale: From Remembering to Navigating," presents a novel perspective on cognition called "kaleidoscopic cognition." This concept challenges traditional assumptions about brains, memories, and minds by suggesting that the brain doesn't operate like a computer with stored files but more like a kaleidoscope creating shifting patterns through direct refraction of experience.

Key points from her presentation include:

1. **Beyond Binary Thinking**: The paper aims to shift away from binary thinking (mental vs physical, representation vs no representation) and encourages considering cognition as a continuous, dynamic process. 

2. **Kaleidoscopic Modeling**: Hyatt argues that the brain's central nervous system, specifically the hippocampus, operates kaleidoscopically – creating shifting patterns through ongoing sensory-motor interactions with the environment. This model of cognition avoids the need for stored mental representations or models inside the brain.

3. **Waymaking**: Hyatt introduces the concept of "waymaking" to describe how both cognitive processes (like remembering) and physical navigation (like moving through a city) are part of the same continuous process. 

4. **Hippocampus as Kaleidoscope**: Rather than viewing the hippocampus as storing spatial maps or maintaining stable patterns, Hyatt suggests it refracts sensory input into various bodily actions in real-time, creating a dynamic, ever-changing "representation" of the organism's interaction with its environment.

5. **Empirical Support**: Hyatt points to recent neuroscience research on grid cells in the hippocampus that shift through multiple reference frames, supporting the kaleidoscopic model. These findings show grid cells remapping and realigning with ongoing encounters at different scales simultaneously.

6. **Implications for Cognitive Science**: Embracing a kaleidoscopic view of cognition could help break free from entrenched debates in neuroscience and philosophy, allowing for a more nuanced understanding of complex processes like memory, navigation, and thinking. 

7. **Methodological Shifts**: This perspective calls for a rethinking of experimental designs and how we conceptualize cognitive phenomena, moving towards multi-dimensional, context-sensitive approaches that can capture the fluidity and complexity of ongoing, embodied interactions with the environment.

In essence, Hyatt's kaleidoscopic cognition framework offers a paradigm shift in understanding cognition by emphasizing the dynamic, relational, and multiscale nature of how our brains engage with the world through continuous, ongoing processes rather than stored representations. This view acknowledges the complexity and subtlety of our interactions with our environments while providing a fresh perspective on longstanding debates in cognitive science.


The text discusses a perspective on understanding cognitive processes, particularly navigation and spatial memory, through the lens of a "kaleidoscopic" model rather than traditional binary or fixed frameworks. This approach is inspired by a scientific paper that recorded grid cells in the entorhinal cortex during path integration tasks (i.e., self-localization based on continuous movement).

The key findings from this study are:
1. No stable grid patterns were observed during the path integration task, suggesting that grid cells do not function as a fixed internal coordinate system or metric. Instead, their patterns shift continuously throughout the task. 

2. This shifting occurs through 're-anchoring through translation,' where grid cells switch between different reference frames within single trials. They can code position relative to room frames and then shift to coding based on salient objects in the environment (like a doorknob).

3. Despite this continuous shifting, an underlying structure or 'manifold' governing grid cells organization remains intact but dynamic. It's not random noise; it's structured, changing patterns. 

The author argues that this kaleidoscopic function – multiple simultaneous reference frames constantly shifting while maintaining a relational structure – is a fundamental aspect of cognition, including navigation and spatial memory. They suggest that our current models and representations might be too static and oversimplify these dynamic processes.

Implications for neuroscience include:
- Rethinking what neural function is fundamentally; cognitions are seen as the ways organisms interact with their environment, not as fixed patterns to be discovered within the brain.
- Memories could be understood as patterns of refraction that reconstitute when 'refracacted' or accessed, rather than static storage locations.
- The body's sensory cohesion is a form of refraction pattern, and our measurements and experiments are ways of communicating about these processes, not direct representations of them.

The author also proposes the idea of creating "kaleidoscopic generative models" in neuroscience – models that can shift and change like a kaleidoscope, reflecting the dynamic nature of cognitive processes. This would involve thinking about perception and action as an ongoing coupling driven by free energy minimization, where encounters with the environment cause immediate, refractive shifts in how we perceive and interact with it. 

Ultimately, this perspective encourages scientists to consider cognition as a dynamic, relational process rather than a set of static representations or computations, echoing the fluid, ever-changing nature of a kaleidoscope's patterns.


### 20251121 - Not One, Not Two： Star Cybernetics (Francisco Varela, Andrea Hiott)

The text discusses the concept of "not one, not two," a philosophical perspective introduced by Francisco Varela, a Chilean neuroscientist and philosopher. This idea challenges the binary thinking prevalent in Western thought, which often categorizes things into distinct opposites (e.g., mind vs. body, self vs. other, order vs. chaos). Instead, Varela proposes that reality is more akin to a constellation or network of interconnected nodes, where boundaries are fluid and distinctions are relative.

The narrative weaves together several themes:

1. **Challenging Dualism**: The text questions the traditional dualistic approach in philosophy and science, which tends to reduce complex phenomena into distinct categories (e.g., mind-body dichotomy). Varela's "not one, not two" perspective suggests that these distinctions are not absolute but rather part of a continuous spectrum or network.

2. **The Paradox as Portal**: Instead of viewing paradoxes as problems to be solved, the text presents them as portals—ways to access deeper understanding and appreciation of reality's complexity. This perspective aligns with Varela's experience during a political coup in Chile, where he realized that opposing sides were not inherently separate but part of an ongoing, interconnected process.

3. **Constellation Cybernetics**: The text introduces the concept of "constellation cybernetics," which envisions reality as a dynamic network of nodes connected by relationships. This model contrasts with hierarchical models that posit discrete building blocks or fixed categories. In this constellation, every element is both unique and interdependent, reflecting the fluidity of boundaries and identities.

4. **Agency in Tension**: The narrative emphasizes the value of embracing tensions rather than seeking resolutions. By holding paradoxes—the coexistence of seemingly contradictory ideas—individuals can cultivate agency within a complex, ever-changing reality. This perspective encourages individuals to stand firmly for their beliefs without reducing others to opposing "others."

5. **Love as the Overarching Principle**: The text suggests that embracing the "not one, not two" perspective cultivates love—not sentimental or passive love but an active recognition of interconnectedness and mutual worth. This love propels individuals to engage with reality dynamically, fostering growth and change without resorting to destructive opposition.

In summary, the text presents Varela's "not one, not two" philosophy as a way to transcend binary thinking, embrace complexity, and cultivate agency in an interconnected world. By viewing paradoxes as portals rather than problems, individuals can engage more deeply with reality, recognizing the fluidity of boundaries and the interdependence of all elements within a constellation or network. This perspective encourages love as the guiding principle, fostering active recognition of interconnectedness and mutual worth in a dynamic, ever-changing world.


### 20251123 - #76 The Care of Things： Philosophy of Maintenance & Making Things Last with Jérome Denis and Davi...

In the conversation between Andrea, David Ponti, and Jérôme Denis, they delve into the themes of maintenance, care, and their relationship with objects and relationships, drawing from Science and Technology Studies (STS) and the philosophical theories of care.

1. **The Discovery of Maintenance**: The authors' initial research focused on the design and production of Paris Metro signs, but a pivotal moment came when they realized they had overlooked maintenance workers. Talking to these maintainers fundamentally changed their understanding of the signs' nature, revealing them as fragile entities in constant transformation, rather than immutable objects.

2. **Fragility and Processual Making**: This discovery led the authors to reconsider the role of fragility and processual making in science and technology studies. They found that maintenance workers' expertise lies in understanding material interactions and transformations, something often overlooked in traditional STS research.

3. **Multisensory Expertise**: Maintenance work involves a multisensory engagement with materials and environments, using various senses like sight, touch, smell, and hearing. This sensory expertise is crucial for noticing subtle changes or issues that might go unnoticed by others.

4. **Maintenance vs Repair**: The authors differentiate between maintenance (ongoing, regular care) and repair (response to a breakdown). Maintenance is often invisible, done by "regular people" in a continuous rhythm, while repair is seen as an event that brings the system back to order after disruption.

5. **Connection with Feminist Theories of Care**: The authors highlight parallels between maintenance and care as work, emphasizing its costs (economic, human) and the recognition of interdependence. They argue against the myth of autonomy for both people and objects, suggesting that no person or thing can exist independently without some form of care or maintenance.

6. **Impact on Perspective**: The exploration of maintenance has broadened their perspective on everyday life, revealing its profound impact on society. It challenges the dichotomy between development/innovation (focused on change and progress) and maintenance (ongoing, often invisible care), showing how these aspects are interconnected and equally important in shaping our world.

7. **Ambiguity of Maintenance**: The authors acknowledge that maintenance is not always positive or beautiful; it can involve risks to both the object being maintained and the person doing the maintaining. It can lead to destruction, harm, or even death if not done properly. Yet, this ambiguity is part of the reality of maintenance work and should be recognized in our understanding of infrastructure and technological systems.

In essence, the authors argue for a reevaluation of maintenance as an essential aspect of life that requires attention, care, and recognition, paralleling the feminist theories of care while shedding light on the interconnectedness and fragility of our world.

### 20251126 - Hyperscanning, Human Connection and the Third Brain： interbrain synchrony with  Nicolás Hinrichs

The conversation revolves around the concept of hyperscanning, a neuroscientific method that records brain activity from two or more individuals simultaneously during interaction. The primary speaker, Nico, explains that traditional hyperscanning focuses on measuring inter-brain synchrony (IBS), which is essentially co-occurrences of brain activity between individuals. However, Nico aims to move beyond this simplistic understanding by exploring deeper patterns and nuances in brain activity during interactions.

Nico highlights the limitations of current hyperscanning methodologies, which often prioritize spatial or temporal resolution at the expense of ecological validity – how representative these experiments are of real-life interactions. To address this, Nico proposes using fNIRS (functional Near-Infrared Spectroscopy) as a mobile alternative to fMRI, allowing for more naturalistic interaction while maintaining midway spatial and temporal resolution between EEG and fMRI.

The discussion then delves into the idea of a 'third entity' or 'third brain,' which emerges from interactions between people's brains. This concept is analogous to how bodies synchronize during dance, but with the added complexity of neurobiological processes. Nico emphasizes the importance of acknowledging the full range of interaction dynamics – not just perfect synchrony but also repairing misunderstandings and shifting patterns over time.

The participants explore the implications of this research in terms of mental health interventions, as well-coordinated interactions are crucial for therapeutic relationships. They discuss the limitations of current technology and the need to balance methodological rigor with ethical considerations and nuanced understanding of human connection.

The conversation also touches upon the philosophical underpinnings of this work, particularly the 'holding paradox' – acknowledging contradictions and complexities inherent in studying human interaction without oversimplifying or ignoring them. This idea is linked to the notion of participatory sense-making and neurophenomenology, which aim to integrate phenomenological insights with neuroscientific methods.

Lastly, the speakers briefly discuss the potential of geometric evaluations in hyperscanning, offering a more nuanced understanding of brain activity patterns during interactions. This approach could help visualize and model complex interaction dynamics, including asymmetries and temporal differences, potentially leading to better mental health interventions.

In essence, this conversation explores the evolving field of hyperscanning, its potential to deepen our understanding of human interaction, and its implications for fields like mental health care, all while grappling with philosophical and methodological challenges.


### 20251130 - #77 Evolving Language by Embracing Paradox with Lisa Maroski

The conversation between Andrea Hyatt and Lisa Fargione revolves around the theme of embracing paradox, particularly in relation to language and our understanding of the world. Here's a detailed summary and explanation of key points:

1. **Embracing Paradox**: The central concept discussed is 'embracing paradox,' which involves acknowledging that things can be both distinct and interconnected simultaneously, rather than being confined to either/or dichotomies. This idea is inspired by the Mobius strip metaphor—a surface with one side and one edge, appearing to have two sides but actually representing unity.

2. **Language as a Limiting Factor**: Lisa argues that our language shapes how we perceive reality. By separating concepts into distinct categories (e.g., nature vs. nurture, connection vs. distinction), we limit our understanding and create false dichotomies. This linguistic structure may contribute to various societal issues, such as environmental degradation and unsustainable economic systems.

3. **The Need for a New Language Paradigm**: Lisa suggests that changing language alone is not enough; it requires shifting the underlying mindset or paradigm. She emphasizes that words are just one aspect of language, which also includes syntax (word order), logic (reasoning patterns), and category structures. To truly embrace paradox, we need to rethink how we categorize and reason about the world.

4. **Cultural Influence on Language**: Lisa points out that our language is deeply rooted in culture. American English, for example, tends to be noun-centric, reflecting a mindset focused on distinct objects. Other cultures may have verb-oriented languages, emphasizing processes and change. Changing language requires shifting the underlying worldview and cultural habits.

5. **The Role of Questions**: Both Andrea and Lisa value questions as tools for expanding understanding. By acknowledging what we don't know (i.e., "I don't know"), we open ourselves to exploration, curiosity, and connection with others and the world around us. This humbling approach fosters a more comprehensive grasp of reality.

6. **Interconnectedness and Relational Thinking**: Embracing paradox encourages relational thinking—recognizing that everything is interconnected. This perspective challenges our tendency to view ourselves as separate from nature or other beings, promoting a sense of responsibility and care for the broader ecosystem.

7. **The Challenge of Changing Mindsets**: Shifting away from either/or thinking towards embracing paradox is challenging because it requires breaking free from ingrained habits and ways of understanding the world. This process involves questioning assumptions, cultivating humility, and fostering openness to new perspectives.

8. **Love as a Connective Force**: Lisa emphasizes that love is not merely an emotion but a field or connection that binds us to the world around us. By recognizing our interconnectedness with nature, other beings, and even seemingly opposite entities, we can deepen our sense of care and responsibility.

9. **The Journey Towards Multiplicity**: Ultimately, embracing paradox is about moving beyond binary thinking toward a more nuanced understanding of reality—one that acknowledges multiplicity and interconnectedness without reducing complex phenomena to simplistic categories. This journey involves questioning our assumptions, cultivating humility, and fostering openness to new perspectives.

In essence, Lisa Fargione's "Embracing Paradox: Evolving Language" encourages readers to challenge their linguistic and cognitive habits, embrace complexity, and recognize the interconnected nature of reality. By doing so, we can foster a more profound sense of care for ourselves, each other, and the world.

### 20251203 - The Care of Things： Philosophy of Maintenance with Jérôme Denis and David Pontille

In this interview, David Ponti and Jérôme Duny discuss their book "The Care of Things, Ethics and Politics and Maintenance," which explores the world of maintenance through the lens of care, ethics, and politics. They explain how they came to write about maintenance by initially investigating the design and production of subway signs in Paris, only later discovering the importance of maintenance itself during their fieldwork.

The authors delve into the fragility and processual nature of things as opposed to the more common view of objects as solid and immutable. They highlight how following maintenance workers revealed the dynamic relationship between humans, materials, and technology, challenging previous assumptions about innovation and development.

The discussion includes several key themes:

1. Fragility and processuality: Maintenance work demonstrates that things are not static but rather undergo constant transformation due to wear, tear, and the interaction of various materials. This challenges the common perception of objects as fixed entities.

2. Multisensory approach: Maintenance workers engage with objects through multiple senses, depending on the situation, which helps them understand the object's condition and determine necessary interventions. Vision is complemented by other senses like smell, touch, and even the vibrations of machinery.

3. Invisible work: The authors emphasize that maintenance is often overlooked or undervalued as "invisible" or "background" labor, despite its crucial role in preserving functionality and authenticity (e.g., classic car owners). This raises questions about fair compensation and recognition for maintenance workers.

4. Repair vs. Maintenance: The authors differentiate between repair work done after a breakdown versus ongoing maintenance that aims to keep things running smoothly without interruption. They argue that framing maintenance as a continuous, low-key process can help reveal its significance and reframe our understanding of infrastructure.

5. Ambiguity and dark side of maintenance: Maintenance work isn't always positive or harmless; it can involve risks to workers' health and wellbeing, as well as potential destruction of the object being maintained if care is not taken. This highlights the complex relationship between humans, objects, and their environments.

6. Connection with feminist theories: The authors find resonances between maintenance practices and feminist theories of care, particularly in terms of fragility, vulnerability, and work's cost to individuals. Both acknowledge that no person is autonomous and require care from others at some point.

7. Personal experiences: Both authors share how their investigation into maintenance has affected their personal lives—for instance, fostering a renewed appreciation for durability, interdependence, and the value of caring for things properly. These insights have also influenced their teaching practices, encouraging them to be more attentive to students' individual goals and experiences.

8. Love in relation to maintenance: The authors touch on how love can manifest in seemingly mundane activities like graffiti removal—the workers' love for Paris motivating their meticulous care of the city's streets and facades. This adds a layer of emotional depth to understanding our relationships with material environments.

Overall, "The Care of Things" challenges conventional views on objects and infrastructure by emphasizing maintenance as an essential process that reveals fragility, interdependence, and the human dimension in seemingly autonomous systems. The book invites readers to reconsider their perceptions of everyday life, technology, and care through a nuanced exploration of maintenance practices.

### 20251205 - Memory, Space, Time： Andrea Hiott Philosophy & Memory Diary for December 5th

In this diary entry from December 5th, the philosopher shares a video of their conversation with neuroscientist Lynn Nadel, focusing on the topic of hippocampal research, memory, navigation, and action at a distance. The discussion revolves around several key themes:

1. Hippocampus as a translator/mediator: The philosopher posits that the hippocampus acts more like a translator or mediator rather than a storage unit for memories. It's responsible for connecting and stitching together various sensory experiences, creating an ongoing, coherent spatial and temporal framework of our reality.

2. Space, memory, and time: The philosopher explores how we traditionally think about space, memory, and time as separate entities. However, they argue that these concepts are interconnected and form part of a continuous process or ongoing "remembering" of past experiences that guides future actions.

3. Action at a distance: Inspired by the concept of quantum entanglement in physics, where particles can be instantaneously connected regardless of distance, the philosopher discusses how hippocampal damage can lead to patients living "in the present moment" with limited continuity and awareness. This phenomenon is analogous to the action at a distance seen in quantum mechanics.

4. Kantian perspectives: The discussion incorporates philosopher Immanuel Kant's ideas about space and time as mental constructs rather than objective realities independent of our perceptions. Lynn Nadel also references these concepts in his research, suggesting that the hippocampus contributes to this subjective sense of space by creating a neural cognitive map that "stitches together" disparate sensory experiences.

5. Historical context: The philosopher refers to a 1978 paper by Nadel and John O'Keefe, titled "The hippocampus as a cognitive map," which proposed the idea that the hippocampus plays a critical role in creating an internal model of space. They argue this system allows animals to act on things at distant places or times based on previously experienced information.

6. 2021 paper: In Lynn Nadel's 2021 paper, "The Hippocampal Formation and Action at a Distance," he further explores the role of the hippocampus in bridging temporal and spatial gaps by creating cognitive maps and neural trajectories. These mechanisms enable animals to simulate possible futures based on past experiences, even when those elements were experienced separately in time.

7. Superstition and pattern recognition: The philosopher highlights Nadel's connection between hippocampal damage leading to a "permanent present tense" and the development of superstitions, which may be linked to the way our brains connect events and form patterns based on past experiences.

The philosopher concludes by encouraging listeners to explore these ideas in Nadel's open-access publications and share their thoughts on the fascinating interplay between space, memory, time, and the hippocampus as a translator or mediator of our ongoing cognitive processes.

### 20251214 - Hippocampus Love： Action at a Distance and the bridge of Memory, Part 4 with Lynn Nadel

In this conversation, the speaker discusses Lynn Nadel's research on the hippocampus and its role in creating a psychological space that resembles Immanuel Kant's concept of absolute space. The speaker emphasizes the importance of understanding memory not as storage but as an ongoing process or communication with oneself. They delve into various aspects of this process, including how the hippocampus helps bridge temporal and spatial gaps, enabling us to remember past events and imagine future scenarios.

The speaker highlights that our brains create cognitive maps using the hippocampal formation, which allows for the integration of spatially and temporally disparate experiences into a coherent framework. This process enables action at a distance by connecting events that occurred in different places or times, as well as facilitating navigation and learning.

Nadel's 1978 book "The Hippocampus as a Cognitive Map" laid the groundwork for this understanding, positing that the hippocampus generates an absolute spatial framework within which experiences are located and interrelated. This framework enables an animal to navigate independently of specific sensory inputs, link together conceptual parts of an environment, and create event representations even when elements were experienced at different times.

The speaker also refers to Nadel's 2021 paper "Hippocampal Formation and Action at a Distance," where he argues that the hippocampus plays a critical role in stitching together spatio-temporally disparate entities and events, creating cognitive maps representing extended spatial contexts, and generating neural trajectories linking parts of an event across temporal gaps.

The conversation touches on Kant's philosophy, particularly his view that space and time are not external entities but mental constructs imposed upon raw experience by the mind. The speaker connects these ideas with Nadel's work, emphasizing that both Kant and Nadel propose a unitary, non-centered stationary framework underlying our perception of absolute space.

The speaker also discusses the limitations of studying memory as separate from navigation or spatial cognition, arguing that these functions are interconnected and part of an ongoing process facilitated by the hippocampus. The conversation concludes with a discussion on the challenges in articulating and understanding this complex relationship between space, time, and memory.

In summary, Lynn Nadel's research focuses on how the hippocampus creates psychological spaces that resemble Kant's concept of absolute space, allowing us to navigate and remember our environment by bridging temporal and spatial gaps. This ongoing process of communication with oneself enables action at a distance, integrating disparate experiences into coherent frameworks and supporting navigation, learning, and memory. The speaker emphasizes the importance of rethinking memory as part of this interconnected system rather than as separate processes or storage mechanisms.


The conversation revolves around the topic of cognitive mapping, memory, and the hippocampus, primarily focusing on research conducted by Dr. Lynne Neyfakh, a neuroscientist. Here's a detailed summary and explanation:

1. **Tropisms in Plant Behavior**: The conversation starts with an exploration of plant behavior, particularly their movement responses to environmental stimuli such as light, moisture, odor plumes, etc. These responses are referred to as tropisms, which are directional growth or movement in response to an environmental stimulus.

2. **Cognitive Maps**: The discussion then shifts towards the concept of cognitive maps - mental representations of the spatial environment that organisms use for navigation and decision-making. This idea is not limited to humans but extends to various animals, even invertebrates. Dr. Neyfakh emphasizes the importance of memory in forming these internal models or cognitive maps.

3. **Hippocampus and Cognitive Maps**: The hippocampus is highlighted as a crucial structure for creating and maintaining such cognitive maps due to its role in spatial memory and navigation. Dr. Neyfakh discusses how animals without a hippocampus (like rats with damage) are more susceptible to superstitious behaviors, relying on temporal contiguity rather than contingency for learning causal relationships.

4. **Dynamic Memory**: The conversation further delves into the dynamics of memory, emphasizing that memories are not static but constantly changing as a function of current experiences. This ongoing updating process allows organisms to adapt to their environment and make informed decisions.

5. **Locus Coeruleus and Memory Updating**: A recent focus in Dr. Neyfakh's research involves the locus coeruleus, a brain region involved in arousal and attention. She discusses its role in orchestrating memory updating processes, helping determine whether to update existing memories or create new ones based on current experiences.

6. **Hippocampus and Emotions**: While the hippocampus plays a significant role in cognitive functions like spatial navigation and memory formation, its connection to emotions is more nuanced. Dr. Neyfakh explains that while it can influence certain aspects of emotional responses (like anxiety related to specific environments), it doesn't fundamentally underlie the basis of emotion or socially-oriented cognitions like compassion and resilience.

7. **Future Directions**: Dr. Neyfakh mentions ongoing research, including a paper recently accepted for publication in TINS (Trends in Neurosciences), focusing on the role of the hippocampus CA2 subregion in social interactions. She also hints at the potential for expanding the circle of care through better understanding communication within and between brains.

Throughout the conversation, Dr. Neyfakh stresses the importance of viewing memory not as a fixed entity but as an ever-evolving, dynamic process integral to navigating and interacting with our environment.

### 20251223 - #79 Loving to Know and Subsidiary Focal Integration with Esther Lightcap Meek

Esther Lightcap Meek is a philosopher known for her work on subsidiary focal integration, drawing heavily from the ideas of Michael Polanyi. Her central argument revolves around challenging modernity's epistemological framework that views knowledge as objective information, collected and processed by the mind. Instead, Meek proposes that knowing is an embodied, relational process rooted in our bodily engagement with the world.

Meek introduces the concept of subsidiary focal integration to explain how we come to understand the world through a dynamic interplay between what's focused (the 'focal') and what supports and informs that focus (the 'subsidiary'). Initially, when learning a skill like riding a bike or reading, our attention is primarily on the focal aspects—balancing, recognizing letters, etc. Over time, these focal elements become subsidiary as they are internalized, allowing us to engage more naturally and intuitively with the world.

Meek's philosophy emphasizes that knowledge is not something we passively acquire but actively create through our bodily interactions with reality. It's a participatory process involving an ongoing relationship between our body and its environment. This perspective encourages seeing knowledge as more than mere information; instead, it's a deeply personal, intimate experience grounded in our bodily being-in-the-world.

Key aspects of Meek's philosophy include:

1. **Embodied Knowledge**: Contrary to modernity's disembodied view of knowledge, Meek argues that knowing is inherently embodied. Our body and its interactions with the environment are fundamental to understanding reality.

2. **Subsidiary Focal Integration**: This concept describes how focal elements of our experiences become integrated into a broader network of subsidiary awareness through practice and engagement, leading to a richer understanding of the world.

3. **Covenantal Epistemology**: Meek proposes that knowledge is best understood within the framework of a covenant—a personal, reciprocal relationship where we commit to lovingly engage with reality. This approach emphasizes humility, vulnerability, and responsibility in our interactions with the world.

4. **Challenging Modern Epistemology**: Meek's philosophy critiques modernity's positivistic view of knowledge as objective, factual information that can be neatly organized and controlled. Instead, she advocates for a more nuanced understanding that acknowledges the subjective, relational, and dynamic nature of knowing.

5. **The Importance of Intimacy and Communion**: Meek suggests that our engagement with reality should involve a sense of intimacy and communion—a willingness to be vulnerable, open, and receptive to the world around us. This approach fosters a deeper appreciation for the mystery and wonder inherent in existence.

6. **Love as a Foundation of Knowing**: Central to Meek's philosophy is the idea that love is fundamental to knowing. By cultivating a loving relationship with reality, we can access more profound levels of understanding and connection with the world.

7. **Critique of Control and Objectivity**: Meek argues against the modern emphasis on control and objectivity in knowledge acquisition. Instead, she advocates for embracing uncertainty, vulnerability, and the relational nature of reality to foster a more authentic, embodied form of knowing.

Meek's ideas have significant implications for various fields, including education, psychology, and philosophy. Her work encourages rethinking how we approach learning, engage with our bodies, and understand the relationship between ourselves and the world around us. By emphasizing intimacy, communion, and love in our interactions with reality, Meek's philosophy offers a refreshing alternative to modernity's disembodied epistemology, promoting a more holistic, embodied understanding of knowledge.


### 20251226 - Way-making approach Key Points： Sequence, Orientation and Perspectival Trajectory

The text presented is a philosophical exploration of how individuals perceive and experience reality differently, despite occupying the same physical space and time. This concept is illustrated through the metaphor of two people standing at the exact same street corner - they see different things due to their unique perspectives, experiences, and interpretations of the world. 

The author argues against the notion that there's a single, objective truth or reality. Instead, he suggests that our understanding of the world is shaped by our personal histories, experiences, and cognitive frameworks - what he calls "paths" or "waymaking." These paths are not static but dynamic, ever-evolving as we navigate life. 

1. **Orientation**: Every action or thought has an orientation, stemming from where the individual is coming from and where they intend to go. This orientation shapes our experiences and perspectives, even if we're unaware of it. It's generative, opening up possibilities for new sensory experiences and meanings.

2. **Sequence**: Reality isn't a series of static moments but a continuous sequence of events and experiences. Our past influences our present, making each individual's reality unique, even when sharing common experiences with others. 

3. **Perspectival Trajectory**: This concept combines orientation and sequence, emphasizing that we're always in motion, continually creating our realities through our actions, thoughts, and feelings. It underscores the idea that perspectives aren't fixed points but trajectories with momentum, history, and direction. 

The author encourages readers to see differences not as threats or contradictions, but as potential pathways leading to new understandings and shared realities. By recognizing these paths as part of an ongoing process of waymaking, we can appreciate the richness of individual perspectives while also acknowledging our interconnectedness. 

This perspective challenges traditional dualistic thinking (e.g., subjective vs objective, self vs world) by suggesting that reality is not something separate from us, but something we're constantly creating through our experiences and interactions. It's a call to move beyond rigid categories and to embrace the complexity and fluidity of human experience. 

The author also highlights how language often reinforces dualistic thinking (by implying beginnings and ends), suggesting that changing our language could help us think more in terms of interconnected paths and trajectories. This philosophical stance has implications for personal relationships, community building, and societal structures, encouraging a more nuanced understanding of differences and shared realities.


### 20251227 - Esther Lightcap Meek on Loving to Know： the difference between Information and Knowledge

Esther M. O'Brien, a philosopher and author, discusses her ideas about knowledge, learning, and the role of technology, particularly AI, in our understanding of reality. Her central argument is that modernity's emphasis on information as the basis for knowledge has led to a disconnection from reality, making it less appealing to engage with the world directly.

O'Brien introduces Michael Polanyi's concept of subsidiary focal integration, which posits that knowing involves both focus and subsidiaries (indirect awareness). This dual process allows for a deeper, more intimate understanding of reality, as seen in skilled activities like riding a bike or playing sports.

In contrast to the information-centric paradigm, O'Brien argues that knowledge is about becoming embedded in reality through subsidiary awareness, leading to a richer, more nuanced understanding of the world. She critiques the idea that AI can replicate human thought or intuition because it relies on formalizable processes, whereas human knowing involves unformalizable elements.

O'Brien emphasizes the importance of intimacy, orientation, and relationship with reality for a fuller understanding of knowledge. This includes developing an awareness of one's surroundings (orientation) and cultivating a love for learning and the subject matter at hand (romantic love). She contends that AI tools can be beneficial when used subsidiarily—as one would use a guitar or other instrument—but they should not replace the human connection with reality.

The discussion also touches on the theme of love, both in its romantic and intellectual senses. O'Brien sees love as an essential component of knowing, as it fosters a deep, committed relationship with reality. This commitment involves promising to "love, honor, and obey" one's subject matter—a covenantal dimension of knowledge that she believes is crucial for true understanding.

In summary, Esther M. O'Brien advocates for a reevaluation of our relationship with knowledge and reality. She argues against the information-centric paradigm that dominates modernity, advocating instead for subsidiary focal integration—a process that intertwines focused attention with broader subsidiary awareness to foster a richer, more intimate connection with the world. O'Brien sees AI as potentially valuable when used in this subsidiary manner but warns against its overreliance at the expense of direct human engagement with reality. Ultimately, she emphasizes love—both romantic and intellectual—as essential to cultivating a profound understanding of the world.


### 20260101 - #80 Pure Consciousness with Thomas Metzinger

The conversation between Andrea Hyatt and Thomas Metzinger revolves around the topics of consciousness, philosophy, meditation, and the state of contemporary academia. Here's a summary of key points and themes discussed:

1. Early Philosophical Interests: Metzinger expresses his dissatisfaction with German continental philosophy during his time at Frankfurt University in the 1970s, finding it too fluffy and lacking intellectual rigor. He was drawn to Anglo-Saxon analytical philosophy of mind after discovering works by Peter Burgi, which sparked his interest in interdisciplinary cooperation between philosophy and neuroscience.

2. Neurophenomenology: Metzinger discusses his concept of neurophenomenology, emphasizing that it's more about bridging the gap between phenomenological traditions (such as Buddhism) and neuroscientific findings rather than simply merging representation-based philosophy with neuroscience. He mentions Francisco Varela's influence in shaping his understanding of neurophenomenology.

3. Criticisms and Evolution: Metzinger shares experiences of criticism from younger students, who argue that his approach to neuroscience is too naïve or limited. Despite this, he maintains that the landscape of philosophy has evolved since his early days, with more acceptance for interdisciplinary collaboration between philosophy and empirical sciences like neuroscience.

4. Importance of Critical Thinking: Metzinger advocates for critical thinking in academia, suggesting that students should be taught attentional auto-regulation (meditation) and doxastic auto-regulation (rational, critical thinking). He believes these skills are essential components of philosophy education, as they help individuals better understand their beliefs and knowledge.

5. Multiplicity in Philosophical Pursuits: Metzinger stresses the importance of not being limited to one perspective or methodology when exploring consciousness and related topics. He encourages embracing multiplicities, including analytical philosophy and phenomenological traditions, as well as other approaches like meditation and psychedelics.

6. Academia's Strategic Culture: Metzinger discusses the strategic and competitive nature of academia, where intellectuals can be quick to discredit those who adopt unconventional methods or explore different areas of inquiry. He believes this tendency can hinder genuine exploration and progress in understanding consciousness.

7. Embodied Cognition and Philosophy: Metzinger acknowledges the recent resurgence of interest in embodied cognition (EE) within philosophy but expresses concerns about potential dogmatism or "wokeism" associated with these movements. He emphasizes the need for open-mindedness, critical thinking, and a willingness to engage with diverse perspectives when examining complex topics like consciousness.

8. Changing Norms in Academia: Metzinger reflects on how rapid changes in technology (e.g., AI, large language models) have affected academic norms and expectations. He suggests that these shifts may lead to more unconventional approaches being embraced within philosophy and other disciplines.

Throughout the conversation, Metzinger shares personal anecdotes, such as his experiences with criticism from younger students, his meditation practice's evolution, and his thoughts on the historical context of various philosophical movements. The discussion ultimately highlights the importance of embracing multiplicity in exploring consciousness while fostering critical thinking and a willingness to engage with diverse perspectives.


In this conversation, two individuals discuss various topics including artificial intelligence (AI), philosophy, meditation, psychedelics, suffering, and love. Here's a detailed summary and explanation of their discussion:

1. **AI and Critical Thinking**: The speakers discuss the advancements in AI and its capacity for critical thinking, arguing skills, and understanding fallacies. They reference experiments where AI outperformed humans in online debates, attributing this to AI's ability to learn from all arguments ever made. However, they also acknowledge that as AI trains on increasingly complex data, it might lose coherence or 'intelligence.'

2. **Embodiment and Consciousness**: The speakers delve into the question of whether AI needs a biological body to achieve consciousness. They discuss computational functionalism versus the idea that certain low-level functions in the brain are irreducible to algorithms due to metabolic functions or molecular complexities. The debate is open, with some arguing that life itself is essential for intelligence and others suggesting AI could develop post-biological forms of cognition.

3. **Suffering and Intelligence**: They explore the potential link between intelligence and suffering, citing free energy minimization theory in neuroscience as evidence. The speakers question whether increased intelligence necessarily brings more suffering into the world. This leads to a discussion about the need for a precise understanding of what suffering is before attempting to create 'suffering AI.'

4. **Meditation, Psychedelics, and Embodiment**: The conversation turns to personal practices that aid in understanding these complex issues. One speaker emphasizes the importance of waiting until later in life to experiment with psychedelics due to their intense impact. Both discuss meditation as a tool for gaining insight into consciousness, embodiment, and interconnectedness.

5. **Love and Compassion**: The speakers touch on the concept of love, particularly in relation to compassion. They explore how difficult it can be to hold the suffering of the world without becoming overwhelmed or bitter. This leads to a broader discussion about non-dual awareness—the ability to embrace all aspects of existence, including suffering, with open-hearted compassion without being consumed by it.

6. **Ethical Implications and AI**: The conversation concludes with a focus on the ethical implications of AI development. They discuss the importance of minimizing suffering in both biological sentient systems (like humans) and artificial intelligence itself, emphasizing that we need better understanding and theories about what suffering is to prevent unnecessary harm.

Throughout this dialogue, there's an underlying theme of interconnectedness—the idea that various seemingly disparate concepts like AI, philosophy, meditation, psychedelics, and love are more closely tied than they might initially appear. The speakers suggest that embracing these complexities can lead to a deeper understanding of consciousness, embodiment, and our place in the universe.


### 20260110 - Holding Love and Power Together via Metamodernism with policy advisor Jamie Bristow

In this conversation, the speaker discusses their journey intertwining politics and love, focusing on mindfulness as a tool for transformation. They started working in British Parliament, later becoming involved in mindfulness training for politicians, which led to a broader interest in how inner work could influence public policy.

The speaker's path began with climate change activism, where they felt the need to address not just the physical aspects but also the psychological and cultural dimensions of sustainability. This realization prompted them to explore meditation and mindfulness as means for individual transformation, which in turn could impact collective systems.

The speaker emphasizes that love and power are interconnected and that a balance between these two is crucial. They argue that politics needs this blend of strength (power) and tenderness (love), enabling individuals to be decisive yet compassionate, clear-minded yet empathetic. This perspective stems from integral theory, which posits that human systems involve interwoven inner and outer dimensions.

The conversation also delves into the complexities of bringing love and spirituality into political discourse. The speaker acknowledges the challenges in legitimizing these concepts within mainstream politics, where they were once considered "woo" or career-limiting. Yet, through persistent efforts, such as policy reports and nuanced language use, there has been progress in making these ideas more acceptable.

The discussion touches on the power dynamics within political systems, suggesting that love can be both empowering and limiting when misused. The speaker warns against reducing love to a mere emotional or relational construct, emphasizing its potential as a civic force. They propose a "politics of love" – an approach that brings in profound existential questions about what's important and how we should relate to each other.

This politics of love would not be tied to specific truth claims but rather foster a discourse around love as a skillful and wholesome way of living, emphasizing values over systems alone. The speaker acknowledges the risk of misappropriation, urging new movements like this one to remain vigilant against potential co-opting or warping.

The conversation concludes by exploring metamodernism as a framework that embraces complexity and ambiguity, blending modernist agency with postmodern relativism. This perspective acknowledges multiple ways of understanding reality, including scientific materialism, Christian mysticism, and Buddhist teachings on interdependence.

The speaker shares personal anecdotes about their journey from a materialistic worldview to one infused with spirituality and re-enchantment. They credit their father, a philosopher by nature, for instilling in them a love of questioning and learning—a gift that underpins their current work.

In summary, this conversation explores the intersection of politics, mindfulness, love, and spirituality. The speaker advocates for a balanced approach to power and love in both personal growth and societal transformation, emphasizing the importance of a nuanced understanding of these concepts. They also discuss challenges and opportunities in integrating love and spirituality into political discourse, proposing metamodernism as a framework that accommodates diverse perspectives on reality and encourages embracing ambiguity and complexity.


### 20260119 - Pure consciousness with Thomas Metzinger： holding paradox is compassion？

The conversation between Andrea Hyatt and philosopher Thomas Metzinger revolves around various themes, including personal intellectual journeys, philosophy of mind, consciousness, interdisciplinary studies, meditation, psychedelics, and the role of love in understanding human nature.

1. Early interest in fundamental issues: Metzinger expresses his dissatisfaction with high school peers who decided on career paths without considering fundamental questions about life. He emphasizes the importance of examining such issues from a professional perspective early on.

2. Disillusionment with Frankfurt philosophy department: Metzinger shares his frustration with the Frankfurt philosophy department, which focused on political aspects rather than rigorous philosophical inquiry. This led him to seek more substantial intellectual engagement elsewhere.

3. Turning point and analytical philosophy of mind: Metzinger's turning point came when he discovered the work of Peter Burge, who introduced him to Anglo-Saxon analytical philosophy of mind. This inspired Metzinger to delve deeper into this area, eventually leading to his interdisciplinary focus on cognitive science and neuroscience.

4. Neurophenomenology: Although Metzinger is associated with the concept of neurophenomenology, he clarifies that his use of the term differs from its common interpretation. He wanted to bridge philosophical and scientific perspectives on consciousness, while others sought philosophical insights into embodiment and cognitive science.

5. Importance of meditation and critical thinking: Metzinger advocates for the integration of meditation practices and critical thinking skills in academic settings. He believes that philosophy should foster both aspects to provide a comprehensive understanding of complex issues like consciousness and suffering.

6. Suffering, AI, and ethical considerations: Metzinger raises concerns about the potential for artificial intelligence (AI) to exacerbate suffering if not developed ethically. He emphasizes the need for a solid understanding of suffering itself before creating conscious machines.

7. Multiplicity and non-dual awareness: Metzinger explores the idea that oppositional thinking hinders our ability to understand human nature fully. Instead, he advocates for embracing multiplicity and non-dual awareness, which allows for acknowledging both the horrible and wonderful aspects of people without judgment.

8. Love as a lens: Metzinger discusses love as a complex emotion that intertwines with suffering. He suggests that understanding this connection can help us cultivate greater compassion for others.

9. Holding paradoxes and suspending judgment: Drawing from ancient spiritual teachings, Metzinger emphasizes the importance of holding paradoxes without trying to resolve them. This involves suspending judgments about people and situations in empty, open awareness, allowing for a deeper understanding and connection with others.

10. Critical thinking and AI: Metzinger underscores the urgency of developing ethical guidelines for artificial intelligence (AI) to prevent increased suffering in human society and the potential for AI to harm us if not managed responsibly.

In summary, this conversation touches upon Metzinger's intellectual journey, his advocacy for interdisciplinary philosophy, and his exploration of consciousness, suffering, love, and the role of critical thinking in understanding human nature and AI development.


### 20260203 - What Relates Creates with life & computer scientist Richard Watson (from the archive)

In this conversation, Andrea Hyatt and Richard Watson discuss the concept of individuality and evolution, challenging the dominant paradigm of universal Darwinism, which posits that natural selection is the only adaptive mechanism in nature. Here are key points from their discussion:

1. **Critique of Universal Darwinism**: Both agree that the traditional view of natural selection as the sole driver of evolution has limitations. They argue that this perspective overlooks other mechanisms of adaptation, such as learning and information integration.

2. **Alternative Adaptive Mechanisms**: Watson introduces the concept of "natural induction" or "transformational processes," which are learning systems that change their shape or organization over time without involving a population of different systems and discarding others. These systems retain some connections and discard others based on usefulness, unlike natural selection's random variation and survival-of-the-fittest model.

3. **Evolutionary Units vs Agential Units**: They discuss the distinction between evolutionary units (organisms capable of self-reproduction with heritable variation) and agential units (systems exhibiting information integration and collective action). Watson argues that acting in a way that integrates information and enables collective action is primary to living systems, regardless of whether they are evolutionary units or not.

4. **Scale and Nestedness**: The conversation delves into the complexities of nestedness and scale in biological systems, questioning the assumption that all phenomena can be explained by bottom-up processes alone. They argue that reductionist views, which treat organisms as mere collections of genes, fail to account for the emergent properties of living systems.

5. **Love and Subjectivity**: Watson introduces a definition of love as "deeply vulnerable mutual knowing," emphasizing its importance in understanding relationships and evolution. He argues that this perspective counters the logic-driven, objective view of science by incorporating subjective experiences and the interconnectedness of entities.

6. **Challenging Self-Interest**: Both question the assumption that self-interest is the primary driver of behavior in nature. Watson suggests that the fear of losing oneself may contribute to the persistence of self-interested views, even when they don't accurately reflect reality.

7. **Modeling and Mirroring**: The conversation explores the idea that entities model and mirror each other as a means of understanding their environment, leading to increased similarity and resonance between them. This process allows for the creation of new relationships and organizations at higher levels of complexity.

8. **Relating Creates**: Watson argues that relating, rather than merely persisting, is fundamental to creation. By connecting with other entities, systems generate new interactions and organizations, which can be seen as a creative process that goes beyond the static model implied by natural selection alone.

9. **Universal Darwinism vs Relates Creates**: The conversation ultimately positions the "relates creates" perspective as an alternative to universal Darwinism, suggesting that organizations of information integration and collective action are prime movers in evolution, orchestrating and interacting with all scales of organization. This view emphasizes the importance of subjective experiences and relationships within a broader, interconnected system.

In summary, this conversation challenges the conventional wisdom of universal Darwinism by proposing alternative adaptive mechanisms like learning and information integration. It also highlights the limitations of reductionist views in biology, advocating for a more holistic understanding that incorporates subjectivity, relationships, and the creative potential of living systems.


### 20260217 - Love, Life and Logic： Another Dialectic with Hegelian scholar Karen Ng (from the archive)

In this conversation between Andrea and Karen Ng, they delve into the philosophy of G.W.F. Hegel, focusing on his concept of life, self-consciousness, and freedom as presented in his works, particularly the Phenomenology of Spirit and Science of Logic.

1. Discovery of Hegel: Karen Ng describes her initial skepticism towards Hegel, having come to him after studying other philosophers critical of his work. She was attracted to his writing due to its rhythm and movement, despite finding it challenging to understand fully.

2. Criticisms of Hegel: The conversation explores common criticisms of Hegel, including accusations of hyper-rationalism, mystical monism, and a lack of clear distinctions or boundaries in his thought. These criticisms often stem from misunderstandings regarding the nature of contradiction in Hegel's philosophy.

3. Anti-dualism: Both speakers emphasize that one of Hegel's significant contributions is his opposition to dualistic ways of thinking, which he aims to debunk by demonstrating the interconnectedness between life and mind or life and cognition. This anti-dualistic approach is seen as an effort to move beyond traditional categories separating subject and object, nature and mind, or life and non-life.

4. Kant's Copernican Turn: The discussion highlights the importance of Immanuel Kant's Copernican turn in understanding Hegel's philosophy. This turn involves recognizing that objects must conform to our knowledge for experience to be meaningful, leading to an emphasis on self-consciousness as a focal point for questions of intelligibility and understanding the natural world.

5. Proposiveness: Karen Ng introduces the concept of proposiveness, which is crucial in Hegel's thought. She discusses both internal (inherent to living beings) and external proposiveness (attributed by an outside force), arguing that internal proposiveness better captures the unique organization of living things compared to artifacts or non-living entities.

6. Life as constitutive of self-consciousness: A central theme in Hegel's philosophy is the idea that life is essential to understanding self-consciousness. For Hegel, thinking and cognition are deeply connected with living organisms, their bodies, and metabolic exchange within an environment. This perspective challenges traditional Cartesian dualism by asserting that minds have a biological basis and cannot be reduced to mere computational processes.

7. Speculative Identity Thesis: Hegel's speculative identity thesis posits that life is constitutive of self-consciousness, which Karen Ng argues is a radical claim in the history of philosophy. This idea challenges dualistic ways of thinking and offers an alternative perspective on understanding minds as living entities deeply connected to nature.

8. Influence on contemporary thought: Both speakers suggest that Hegel's philosophy remains relevant today, particularly in addressing environmental crises and technological advancements like AI. They highlight how understanding life as continuous with mind could provide alternative ways of thinking about these complex issues.

9. Love and Philosophy: The conversation touches on Hegel's views on love, which he saw as deeply connected to life. For Hegel, true union or love proper exists only between living things who recognize each other's power and life from every perspective. This notion of love emphasizes relationality and the recognition of others as fully alive, offering insights into understanding oneself and nature through a loving perspective.

10. The role of philosophy: In conclusion, both Andrea and Karen Ng reflect on how engaging with Hegel's thought can expand one's way of thinking about the world, fostering a deeper appreciation for the interconnectedness of life, mind, and nature. They also discuss the importance of teaching as a means to counteract professional silos and maintain contact with fresh perspectives on philosophical questions.


### 20260307 - Curiosity as a Practice and the Capacity to Connect with philosopher Perry Zurn (from the archive)

In this podcast episode, the host discusses curiosity with philosopher Perry Zern, co-author of "Curious Minds" with his twin brother Danny Bassett. The conversation revolves around several key themes related to curiosity, love, and knowledge:

1. **Curiosity as a practice and capacity**: Perry redefines curiosity from its traditional historical notion of desire for information acquisition to a relational and networked capacity to connect ideas, communities, and the self with the world. Curiosity is seen as edgework, which involves building edges between nodes or creating liminal spaces.

2. **Love and curiosity**: The host asks Perry about any connection between love and curiosity. Perry acknowledges an implicit link in early Latin work on curiosity and its association with desire, lust, and love. He suggests that love could be a companion for curiosity to help it reach its full potential by guiding it towards respectful forms of knowledge-seeking and community-building.

3. **Styles of being curious**: Perry introduces three styles of curiosity: the busybody (curious about many things, creating loose networks), the hunter (focused on specific areas, building tight networks), and the dancer (creative, combining disparate ideas). These styles are based on historical observations of Western intellectual thought.

4. **Walking as a metaphor for curiosity**: The hosts discuss how walking can be used to understand different ways of engaging with knowledge and ideas. Perry emphasizes the importance of physical, conceptual, and social walks in shaping our perspectives and understanding of the world.

5. **Anthropocentric curiosity vs. a more ecological view**: The conversation touches on how traditional views of curiosity as an exclusively human capacity have contributed to detrimental relationships with other forms of life and the environment. Perry argues for recognizing curiosity as a shared quality among living beings, including plants and animals, which could foster more responsible interactions with nature.

6. **Cracks as a metaphor for destructive curiosity**: Perry and his co-author incorporate the concept of "cracks" in their book to represent both destruction and creation. Cracks symbolize the breaking of established patterns, edges, and perceptions that lead to new possibilities and openness to alternative futures. This idea is inspired by Gloria Anzaldua's notion that cracks are where light gets in.

7. **Dedication to children**: The book dedication is given to "all the children who question whether it needs to be this way," highlighting the power and importance of childhood curiosity, which is often unheard or unacknowledged in adult-dominated societies.

Throughout the episode, Perry Zern emphasizes the social aspects of curiosity, its capacity for connection rather than acquisition, and the potential benefits of reimagining curiosity as a shared quality among living beings. The conversation also explores the metaphorical use of walking to understand different ways of engaging with knowledge and ideas.
